The Attribute's Suitability for Multiple Aspects and the Need for a Preponderant
Refutes the need for a preponderant in divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the argument refutes the necessity of a preponderant factor for God's will, preserving His free agency.

Core Idea
If God's will needs a preponderant, infinite regress; if not, power is independent.

This passage tackles a subtle point in divine attributes: does the fact that God's will could relate to many possible outcomes require something to tip the balance? The author argues that either way leads to absurdity, preserving God's free agency. Let us examine the logical steps.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal English translation. Notice the logical structure: the author presents two main divisions and examines each.
The text assumes prior knowledge of divine attributes. If terms like 'preponderant' are unfamiliar, note them.
2
Identify the two divisions
The passage first considers whether the suitability of an attribute (like will) for multiple aspects requires a preponderant. Then it examines the implication if the will is eternal and related to a specific time.
The first division is a conditional (if...then), the second is a separate scenario.
3
Follow the conditional argument
Trace the reasoning: if a preponderant is required, the will needs another preponderant (infinite regress). If not, power would not need will. Both are problematic.
This shows that the initial assumption leads to contradiction.
4
Understand the second division's conclusion
The second division argues that if the will is eternal and fixed to one time, then without preponderant, God would be a necessary agent, not free. This corrupts the root of proving God as omnipotent free chooser.
The author concludes the discussion here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم بوقت آخر . فإن كان الأول فنقول : صلاحية الصفة للوجوه الكثيرة إما أن تحوج إلى المرجح أو لا تحوج ، فإن أحوجته إليه لزم افتقار الإرادة إلى مرجح آخر ، وإن لم تحوجه إليه وجب أن لا تحتاج القدرة إلى الإرادة وأما القسم الثاني : فنقول : لما كانت الإرادة أزلية ممتنعة العدم ، وكانت متعلقة بأحداث العالم في وقت معين ، وما كانت صالحة للتعلق بإحداث العالم في وقت آخر ، فحينئذ يصير الإله موجباً بالذات غير فاعل على سبيل الصحة . ومعلوم أن هذه المسألة فرع على إثبات القادر المختار ، وكل فرع يوجب فساد الأصل كان باطلاً . وأيضاً : فإذا جاز أن يقال : تلك الإرادة المخصوصة متعينة التعلق بذلك الوجه الواحد فلم لا يجوز أن يقال تلك الإرادة المخصوصة متعينة التعلق بوجه واحد وحينئذ تكون القدرة غنية عن الإرادة المرجحة ؟ وهذا تمام البحث في هذا الدليل .
The world at another time. If it is the first, we say: The suitability of the attribute for many aspects either requires a preponderant or does not require it. If it requires it, then the will would be in need of another preponderant, and if it does not require it, then it would be necessary that power does not need will. As for the second division, we say: Since the will is eternal, impossible of non-existence, and it is related to the events of the world at a specific time, and it was not suitable for relation to causing the world to exist at another time, then the deity becomes necessary by essence, not an agent on the basis of soundness. And it is known that this issue is a branch of proving the omnipotent free chooser, and every branch that corrupts the root is invalid. Also, if it is permissible to say: that specific will is determined in its relation to that one aspect, then why is it not permissible to say: that specific will is determined in its relation to one aspect? Then power would be independent of the preponderating will. And this is the end of the discussion in this proof.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of a claim regarding the necessity of a preponderant factor for God's will. The author first considers two possibilities: either the suitability of an attribute (here, will) for multiple aspects requires a preponderant, or it does not. If it requires a preponderant, then the will would need another preponderant, leading to an infinite regress, which is absurd. If it does not require a preponderant, then power would not need will, which contradicts the established principle that God is a free agent. The author then presents a second scenario: since God's will is eternal and specifically related to a particular time for the creation of the world, if it were not suitable for another time, God would be a necessary agent rather than a free agent, which is false because this issue is a branch of establishing God as omnipotent and free. The argument concludes by pointing out the inconsistency: if one asserts that the specific will is necessarily related to one aspect, then why can it not be said that it is necessarily related to a single aspect, making power independent of will? Thus, the proof is complete.

Key Terms
المرجح
الإرادة
القدرة
الوجوه الكثيرة
صلاحية الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if will does not need a preponderant, then power is independent, but this leads to denying God's free choice.
Correction: The passage shows that not needing a preponderant does not imply independence of power; rather, it preserves the will's intrinsic determinative role.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if the suitability of an attribute for multiple aspects does NOT require a preponderant factor?
A) The will would need another preponderant.
B) Power would not need will.
C) God would become a necessary agent.
D) The attribute would be impossible.

Key Takeaway
The argument demonstrates that requiring a preponderant for God's will leads to absurdities, confirming that will itself suffices to determine its object without external preponderance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's will is self-determining and needs no external cause, what does that mean for your own attempts to manipulate outcomes through worldly means? Do you truly rely on His will, or do you secretly seek other 'preponderants' to feel secure in your life's direction?
?
This argument shows that requiring a preponderant for God's will leads to absurdity. Yet, how often do you act as if God's will is dependent on your own choices, as if your decisions force His hand? What would it take for you to surrender completely to the sufficiency of His will?
?
The passage warns that if will were not specific to a particular time, God would become a necessary agent, losing free choice. In your spiritual life, do you treat God as a free agent who can respond to your prayers, or do you secretly believe His actions are mechanically determined, thus making your supplications pointless? How does this lesson challenge that assumption?

Four Arguments Against Divine Will and Their Refutation
Lists four arguments by deniers of divine will, each ending with a declaration of invalidity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four main arguments used by those who deny God's attribute of will and why each argument is considered invalid within the Ash'ari framework.

Core Idea
Each proof against divine will collapses under its own logical assumptions.

In this passage from the Matālib al-‘Āliyah, the author presents four proofs that opponents have used to deny that God possesses a will (irādah). These proofs draw on ideas about completeness, meaning, temporality, and preponderance. The author lists them as part of a refutation, showing that each leads to a contradiction or an unacceptable consequence. As you read, note how each proof is structured and where the refutation targets its weak point.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the flow. Notice the four numbered proofs.
The word 'الضرر' after the first proof may be a marginal note.
2
Examine proof one: completeness
The first proof argues that if will is complete, the act would be necessary; if incomplete, impossible. Why does this reasoning fail?
The refutation usually points out that divine will does not imply necessity in the sense of compulsion.
3
Examine proof two: meaning of will
The second proof restricts will to seeking benefit or repelling harm. Why is this definition inadequate for God?
Ash'aris hold that God's will is not subject to such human analogies.
4
Examine proof three: eternity or origination
The third proof splits will into eternal or originated. Both are deemed impossible for God. What is the flaw?
An eternal will can still relate to temporal events without being temporal itself.
5
Examine proof four: necessity or possibility
The fourth proof concerns preponderance: either necessary (making God a necessary agent) or possible (no preponderance with equivalence). How is this argument answered?
The solution is that God's will is a determining factor that resolves equivalence without imposing necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثالثة : واحتج المنكرون لهذه الصفة بوجوه : الحجة الأولى : إنها إن كانت تامة في جميع جهات المؤثرية وجب الفعل ، وإن كانت غير تامة امتنع الفعل ، فالقول بإثبات هذه الإرادة محال . الضرر . الحجة الثانية : إنا لا نعقل من الإرادة إلا الميل إلى جلب النفع أو إلى دفع الضرر. وذلك في حق الله محال . وأما الإرادة بمعنى آخر فلا نعقله البتة . فكان القول بإثباته الله محالاً . الحجة الثالثة : لو حصلت الإرادة لكانت إما أن تكون قديمة أو حادثة ، والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بإثبات الإرادة باطل . وهو معلوم . الحجة الرابعة : إن اقتضاء هذا الترجيح إما أن يكون على سبيل الوجوب ، أو على سبيل الإمكان ، والأول باطل ، وإلا لزم أن يصير موجباً بالذات ، والثاني باطل ، لأن مع الجواز والتساوي يمتنع حصول الترجيح . وهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب . والله أعلم .
The third issue: The deniers of this attribute argued with proofs: The first proof: If it (the will) is complete in all aspects of efficacy, then the act is necessary, and if it is incomplete, then the act is impossible, so asserting this will is impossible. Harm. The second proof: We do not understand from will except inclination to bring benefit or repel harm, and that is impossible with respect to God. But as for will in another sense, we do not understand it at all. Thus the assertion of it for God is impossible. The third proof: If will existed, it would either be eternal or originated, and both divisions are invalid, so the assertion of will is invalid. And this is known. The fourth proof: The implication of this preponderance is either necessarily or possibly. The first is invalid, otherwise it would necessitate that He becomes a necessary agent. The second is invalid, because with permissibility and equivalence, the occurrence of preponderance is impossible. And this is the end of the discussion in this chapter. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage enumerates four proofs used by those who deny the attribute of will (الإرادة) to God. Each proof is presented, and the author implicitly regards them as invalid, as the discussion is part of a refutation. The first proof argues that if the will is complete in all respects, the act would be necessary (contradicting free choice), and if incomplete, the act would be impossible, so will cannot exist. The second proof restricts the meaning of will to inclination towards benefit or repelling harm, which is not applicable to God, and denies any other meaning. The third proof splits will into eternal or originated, both deemed unacceptable: eternal will would entail temporal effects, and originated will would imply change in God. The fourth proof considers whether the preponderance (الترجيح) claimed for the will is necessary or possible: if necessary, God would be a necessary agent; if possible, with equivalence preponderance cannot occur. The author concludes the chapter with 'And God knows best.'

Key Terms
المنكرون
الإرادة
المؤثرية
القدم
الحدوث
الترجيح
الوجوب
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these proofs are sound and therefore God cannot have a will.
Correction: The author presents these proofs as part of a refutation; each is shown to be logically flawed from an Ash'ari perspective.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof of the deniers, what is the only meaning of will they recognize?
A) The inclination to benefit or repel harm.
B) The determination of the agent.
C) The power to act.
D) The knowledge of the best.

Key Takeaway
The four proofs against divine will are all invalid because they either misunderstand the nature of God's will or apply human standards to the divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems unfair or harmful, do you ever doubt that God's will is truly good? How does the truth that God's will is not driven by need or benefit challenge your response to hardship? Will you commit to trusting His will even when it conflicts with your desires?
?
The deniers claimed that will must be for benefit or harm. In your own life, do you realize how often you judge God's actions based on whether they benefit you? What would it mean for you to worship God purely out of submission, not for what you get out of it?
?
The third argument questions how an eternal will can relate to temporary events. Have you ever felt that God's plan seems inconsistent because your circumstances change? How can you anchor your soul in the belief that God's will is eternal and unchanging, regardless of your shifting feelings? Will you today choose to rest in that unchanging will?

The Impossibility of Corporeal Pleasure for God and Proof of His Spiritual Pleasure
Classification and partial proof of spiritual pleasure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between corporeal and spiritual pleasure, why corporeal pleasure is impossible for God, and the philosophical proof that God loves His own essence.

Core Idea
God's pleasure is spiritual, not bodily, proven through His perfect self-knowledge.

This lesson opens the second chapter on pleasure and pain. The author first distinguishes two types of pleasure and denies the first for God. He then presents the philosophers' argument that God experiences spiritual pleasure, grounding it in His self-knowledge and perfection. The passage introduces two claims, with the first fully proved and the second begun.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of pleasure
Read the first few lines where the author divides pleasure into corporeal and spiritual. Note that corporeal pleasure is immediately ruled out for God because He is not a body.
This sets the stage for the rest of the argument.
2
Identify the two claims of the philosophers
Find where the author says the philosophers assert two things: that God loves His essence and that He delights in His perfections. These are the two components of spiritual pleasure.
The second claim is only introduced in this passage, not fully proven.
3
Study the proof for the first claim
Read the explanation: knowledge of a perfect thing causes love. The example of hearing about heroes shows this. Then apply to God: His knowledge and perfection are greatest, so He must have perfect love for Himself.
This is an analogical argument from human experience.
4
Observe the introduction of the second claim
Notice the author begins to explain the second claim (delight) but the text ends mid-sentence. This shows the argument continues.
The second claim also relies on the idea that knowledge of one's own perfection brings delight.
5
Reflect on the overall argument structure
Consider how the author uses the classification to deny one type and affirm the other, then provides rational proof grounded in necessary truths about knowledge and perfection.
This is a typical kalām approach: first establish categories, then prove the relevant one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في اللغة والألم اعلم . إنك قد عرفت أن اللذة ، إما أن تكون جسمانية وإما أن تكون روحانية . أما اللذة الجسمانية فعلى الله محال ، لأنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى ليس بجسم كانت اللغة الجسمانية عليه ممتنعة . وأما اللذة الروحانية فقد أطبقت الفلاسفة على إثباتها لواجب الوجود . واحتجوا عليه بأن قالوا : ندعي حصول أمرين : أحدهما : كونه تعالى محباً لذاته والثاني : كونه تعالى مبتهجاً بكمالاته ملتذاً بها . أما الأول فتقريره : أن علم الشيء يكون الشيء كاملا يوجب محبة ذلك الشيء . ودليله : الاستقرار . فإنا إذا سمعنا بشجاعة رستم ، واسفيد نار ، حصل في قلبنا حب شديد وميل عظيم ، وليس هناك إلا أن اعتقادنا لثبوت الكمالات لهم ، أفاد ذلك الحب إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : علمه تعالى أفضل العلوم ، وكماله أفضل الكمالات . فإذا علم بذلك العلم الكامل ذلك الكمال التام لذاته ، وجب أن يترتب على ذلك العلم التام : حصول الحب التام . وأما الثاني وهو كونه مبتهجاً بذاته ، ملتذاً بكمالاته . فتقريره : أن علم الشيء بكمال نفسه ، كما يوجب الحب الشديد له ، فكذلك يوجب الابتهاج والالتذاذ . ولما حصل ذلك العلم
The second chapter on pleasure and pain. Know that you have learned that pleasure is either corporeal or spiritual. As for corporeal pleasure, it is impossible for God, because since it is established that He, exalted, is not a body, corporeal pleasure is impossible for Him. As for spiritual pleasure, the philosophers have unanimously affirmed it for the Necessary Existent. They argued for it by saying: we claim the occurrence of two matters: one: that He, exalted, is a lover of His essence; and the second: that He, exalted, is delighted with His perfections and taking pleasure in them. As for the first, its explanation: that knowledge of a thing when the thing is perfect necessitates love for that thing. Its proof is: experience. For when we hear of the bravery of Rustam and Asfandiyar, there occurs in our hearts intense love and great inclination, and there is nothing other than our belief in the establishment of perfections for them, which yields that love. If this is established, we say: His knowledge, exalted, is the best of knowledges, and His perfection is the best of perfections. So when He knows with that perfect knowledge that perfect perfection of His essence, it is necessary that from that perfect knowledge results the attainment of perfect love. As for the second, which is that He is delighted with His essence, taking pleasure in His perfections, its explanation: that knowledge of a thing of its own perfection, just as it necessitates intense love for it, likewise it necessitates delight and pleasure. And when that knowledge occurs...
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage begins the second chapter on pleasure (اللذة) and pain (الألم). The author first distinguishes two types of pleasure: corporeal (جسمانية) and spiritual (روحانية). He asserts that corporeal pleasure is impossible for God because God is not a body. Then he states that philosophers unanimously affirm spiritual pleasure for the Necessary Existent (واجب الوجود). They argue by claiming two things: (1) God loves His own essence, and (2) God delights in His perfections and takes pleasure in them. The proof for the first claim: knowledge of a thing when it is perfect necessitates love for that thing. The evidence is experiential: when we hear of the bravery of heroes like Rustam and Asfandiyar, we feel intense love based on our belief in their perfections. Applying this to God: His knowledge is the best, His perfection is the best, and when He knows His perfect essence, perfect love necessarily follows. The author then begins the explanation of the second claim, that knowledge of one's own perfection also necessitates delight and pleasure, but the text cuts off mid-sentence. This argument aims to prove that God is not merely a passive knower but actively delights in His perfections.

Key Terms
لذة
جسمانية
روحانية
محباً لذاته
مبتهجاً بكمالاته
ملتذاً
الاستقرار
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that pleasure is only corporeal, so God cannot have any pleasure.
Correction: The author distinguishes spiritual pleasure, which is not bodily, and proves that God experiences spiritual pleasure through self-love and delight.

Checkpoint
Why is corporeal pleasure impossible for God according to the text?
A) Because God is not a body.
B) Because God is self-sufficient.
C) Because pleasure is only spiritual.
D) Because the philosophers denied it.

Key Takeaway
God's pleasure is spiritual, not bodily; His self-knowledge and perfection necessitate perfect self-love and delight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's pleasure is entirely spiritual and rooted in His perfect self-knowledge, are you ever tempted to think that your worship 'adds' to His happiness? How must you recalibrate your understanding of why you worship?
?
The passage argues that God's love for Himself is a necessary result of His perfect knowledge. Do you find it difficult to love yourself in a way that acknowledges your imperfections? How does this lesson challenge your view of self-love?
?
Since God's delight is in His own perfections, not in anything external, what does that imply about the ultimate purpose of your actions? Are you seeking His pleasure as a goal that benefits you, or as a state that is already perfectly complete in Him? Make a concrete decision today to shift your intention accordingly.

3 min
Bodily Pleasure is Impossible for God
Classification of pleasure and impossibility of bodily pleasure for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why bodily pleasure is impossible for God and the distinction between bodily and spiritual pleasure.

Core Idea
Bodily pleasure requires a body; God has none.

Before exploring the nature of God's pleasure, it is essential to understand the two categories of pleasure: bodily and spiritual. The passage begins by establishing that bodily pleasure, which depends on physical organs, is utterly impossible for God because He is not a body. This sets the stage for discussing spiritual pleasure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division of pleasure
Read the first sentence that divides pleasure into bodily and spiritual. Notice that this is a classification accepted by the author.
2
Identify why bodily pleasure is impossible for God
The author gives a clear reason: since God is not a body, bodily pleasure cannot apply to Him. Reflect on the logic: if pleasure requires a body, then a non-physical being cannot have bodily pleasure.
This is a straightforward theological principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في اللذة والألم اعلم (۱) . إنك قد عرفت أن اللذة ، إما أن تكون جسمانية وإما أن تكون روحانية . أما اللذة الجسمانية فعلى الله محال ، لأنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى ليس بجسم كانت اللذة الجسمانية عليه ممتنعة . وأما اللذة الروحانية فقد أطبقت الفلاسفة على إثباتها لواجب الوجود . واحتجوا عليه بأن قالوا : ندعي حصول أمرين : أحدهما : كونه تعالى محباً لذاته والثاني : كونه تعالى مبتهجاً بكمالاته ملتذاً بها . أما الأول فتقريره : أن علم الشيء يكون الشيء كاملا يوجب محبة ذلك الشيء . ودليله : الاستقرار . فإنا إذا سمعنا بشجاعة رستم ، واسفيد نار ، حصل في قلبنا حب شديد وميل عظيم ، وليس هناك إلا أن اعتقادنا لثبوت الكمالات لهم ، أفاد ذلك الحب إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : علمه تعالى أفضل العلوم ، وكماله أفضل الكمالات . فإذا علم بذلك العلم الكامل ذلك الكمال التام لذاته ، وجب أن يترتب على ذلك العلم التام : حصول الحب التام . وأما الثاني وهو كونه مبتهجاً بذاته ، ملتذاً بكمالاته . فتقريره : أن علم الشيء بكمال نفسه ، كما يوجب الحب الشديد له ، فكذلك يوجب الابتهاج والالتذاذ . ولما حصل ذلك العلم في حق الله تعالى وجب أن يحصل ذلك الابتهاج وذلك الالتذاذ إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : يتفرع على هذا الأصل فرعان : الفرع الأول : إن نسبة ابتهاجه والتذاذه بكمالات ذاته إلى ابتهاج الواحد منا بكمالات ذاته ، كنسبة علمه إلى علمنا ، وكنسبة كمالات ذاته إلى كمالات ذواتنا ، ولما كان لا نسبة لعلمه وكماله إلى علم غيره وكمالات غيره ، فكذلك لا نسبة لابتهاجه بكمالات ذاته إلى ابتهاج غيره بكمالات ذاته . الفرع الثاني : إن الموجودات المفارقة المسماة في لسان الشريعة : بالملائكة . وفي لسان الفلاسفة : بالعقول والنفوس كلها مبتهجة بأنفسها ملتذة بكمالاتها . إلا أن درجات ابتهاجاتها بأنفسها، على حسب درجاتها في كمالاتها ، وكما أن أكمل الموجودات هو الله - سبحانه - فكذلك أجل مبتهج بذاته هو الأول
The second chapter: On pleasure and pain. Know (1) that you have already known that pleasure is either bodily or spiritual. As for bodily pleasure, it is impossible for God, because since it has been established that He is not a body, bodily pleasure is impossible for Him. As for spiritual pleasure, the philosophers have agreed on affirming it for the Necessary Existent. They argued for it by saying: we claim the occurrence of two things: one is that He is loving of His essence, and the second is that He is rejoicing in His perfections, taking pleasure in them. As for the first, its exposition: that knowledge of a thing when the thing is perfect necessitates love of that thing. Its proof: induction (al-istiqrār). For when we hear of the bravery of Rustam and Isfandiar, an intense love and great inclination arise in our hearts, and there is nothing except our belief in the affirmation of perfections for them that produces that love. If this is established, we say: His knowledge is the best of knowledges, and His perfection is the best of perfections. So when He knows by that perfect knowledge that perfect perfection of His essence, it is necessary that the perfect love results from that perfect knowledge. As for the second, namely that He is rejoicing in His essence, taking pleasure in His perfections: its exposition is that knowledge of a thing of its own perfection, just as it necessitates intense love for it, so too it necessitates rejoicing and taking pleasure. And when that knowledge occurs in the case of God, it is necessary that that rejoicing and that taking pleasure occur. If you know this, we say: two branches branch off from this principle: The first branch: that the proportion of His rejoicing and taking pleasure in the perfections of His essence to the rejoicing of one of us in the perfections of his essence is like the proportion of His knowledge to our knowledge, and like the proportion of the perfections of His essence to the perfections of our essences. And since there is no proportion of His knowledge and perfection to the knowledge and perfections of others, so too there is no proportion of His rejoicing in the perfections of His essence to the rejoicing of others in the perfections of their essences. The second branch: that the separate existents called in the language of the Law: angels, and in the language of the philosophers: intellects and souls, all rejoice in themselves and take pleasure in their perfections. But the degrees of their rejoicing in themselves are according to their degrees in perfections. And just as the most perfect of existents is God—glory be to Him—so too the most magnificent in rejoicing in His essence is the First.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof for the spiritual pleasure of God and its implications. The author begins by stating a known division of pleasure into bodily and spiritual. Bodily pleasure is impossible for God because He is not a body. Spiritual pleasure, however, is affirmed by the philosophers for the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd). They argue by claiming two things: that God loves His essence, and that He rejoices in His perfections and takes pleasure in them. The proof for the first claim uses induction: when we hear of the bravery of great heroes like Rustam and Isfandiar, we feel love and inclination in our hearts solely because we believe they possess perfections. Applying this to God: His knowledge is the best knowledge, and His perfection is the best perfection. When He knows His own perfect essence with that perfect knowledge, perfect love necessarily results. The proof for the second claim is similar: knowledge of one's own perfection causes not only love but also rejoicing and pleasure. Since God has that knowledge, He must have that rejoicing and pleasure. From this principle, the author derives two subsidiary points. First, the proportion of God's rejoicing and pleasure to ours is like the proportion of His knowledge and perfections to ours: utterly incomparable, because His attributes are infinite and ours are finite. Second, separate substances (angels in religious language, intellects and souls in philosophical language) all rejoice in themselves and take pleasure in their perfections, but their degrees of rejoicing correspond to their degrees of perfection. Just as God is the most perfect existent, so He is the most magnificent in rejoicing in His essence.

Key Terms
اللذة
الجسمانية
الروحانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God experiences pleasure in a physical sense.
Correction: The passage clarifies that bodily pleasure is impossible for God because He is not a body; only spiritual pleasure can be attributed to Him.

Checkpoint
Why is bodily pleasure impossible for God according to the passage?
A) Because pleasure is always evil.
B) Because God is not a body.
C) Because God is too great for pleasure.
D) Because bodily pleasure leads to imperfection.

Key Takeaway
Bodily pleasure requires a body; since God is not a body, He cannot experience bodily pleasure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that God cannot experience bodily pleasure because He is not a body. Think about how you imagine God when you pray or worship. Have you ever unconsciously pictured Him as having a body or physical attributes? How might this lesson correct that imagination and deepen your awe of His transcendence?
?
If bodily pleasure is impossible for God, what does that tell you about the nature of the pleasure He experiences? More importantly, what kind of pleasure should you seek in your relationship with Him—bodily or spiritual? Honestly, which one do you prioritize right now, and what change might you make starting today?
?
The passage divides all pleasure into two categories: bodily and spiritual. Which category dominates your life? Reflect on your daily habits—are you more driven by physical satisfaction or spiritual fulfillment? In light of this lesson, how can you realign your pursuit of pleasure to reflect the reality that your Creator is beyond bodily pleasures?

God Loves His Essence: The Philosophers' Proof
Proof that God loves His essence using inductive reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical proof that God loves His essence.

Core Idea
Knowledge of perfection generates love; God's perfect knowledge of His perfect essence necessitates perfect love.

Having ruled out bodily pleasure, the philosophers turn to spiritual pleasure, which they affirm for God. Their argument begins by claiming two things: that God loves His essence and that He rejoices in His perfections. This lesson focuses on the first claim and its proof, which uses an inductive example from human experience.

Guided Path
1
Learn the philosophers' claim about God's love
The passage states that one of the two things claimed is that God is loving of His essence. This is the first part of the spiritual pleasure argument.
2
Understand the proof via induction
The proof uses induction: when we hear of a great hero, we feel love based on our belief in their perfections. This shows that knowledge of perfection causes love.
The example of Rustam and Isfandiar is used to illustrate the principle.
3
Apply to God
Since God's knowledge is the best and His perfection is the best, His knowledge of Himself necessitates the best love. This is the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في اللذة والألم اعلم (۱) . إنك قد عرفت أن اللذة ، إما أن تكون جسمانية وإما أن تكون روحانية . أما اللذة الجسمانية فعلى الله محال ، لأنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى ليس بجسم كانت اللذة الجسمانية عليه ممتنعة . وأما اللذة الروحانية فقد أطبقت الفلاسفة على إثباتها لواجب الوجود . واحتجوا عليه بأن قالوا : ندعي حصول أمرين : أحدهما : كونه تعالى محباً لذاته والثاني : كونه تعالى مبتهجاً بكمالاته ملتذاً بها . أما الأول فتقريره : أن علم الشيء يكون الشيء كاملا يوجب محبة ذلك الشيء . ودليله : الاستقرار . فإنا إذا سمعنا بشجاعة رستم ، واسفيد نار ، حصل في قلبنا حب شديد وميل عظيم ، وليس هناك إلا أن اعتقادنا لثبوت الكمالات لهم ، أفاد ذلك الحب إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : علمه تعالى أفضل العلوم ، وكماله أفضل الكمالات . فإذا علم بذلك العلم الكامل ذلك الكمال التام لذاته ، وجب أن يترتب على ذلك العلم التام : حصول الحب التام . وأما الثاني وهو كونه مبتهجاً بذاته ، ملتذاً بكمالاته . فتقريره : أن علم الشيء بكمال نفسه ، كما يوجب الحب الشديد له ، فكذلك يوجب الابتهاج والالتذاذ . ولما حصل ذلك العلم في حق الله تعالى وجب أن يحصل ذلك الابتهاج وذلك الالتذاذ إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : يتفرع على هذا الأصل فرعان : الفرع الأول : إن نسبة ابتهاجه والتذاذه بكمالات ذاته إلى ابتهاج الواحد منا بكمالات ذاته ، كنسبة علمه إلى علمنا ، وكنسبة كمالات ذاته إلى كمالات ذواتنا ، ولما كان لا نسبة لعلمه وكماله إلى علم غيره وكمالات غيره ، فكذلك لا نسبة لابتهاجه بكمالات ذاته إلى ابتهاج غيره بكمالات ذاته . الفرع الثاني : إن الموجودات المفارقة المسماة في لسان الشريعة : بالملائكة . وفي لسان الفلاسفة : بالعقول والنفوس كلها مبتهجة بأنفسها ملتذة بكمالاتها . إلا أن درجات ابتهاجاتها بأنفسها، على حسب درجاتها في كمالاتها ، وكما أن أكمل الموجودات هو الله - سبحانه - فكذلك أجل مبتهج بذاته هو الأول
The second chapter: On pleasure and pain. Know (1) that you have already known that pleasure is either bodily or spiritual. As for bodily pleasure, it is impossible for God, because since it has been established that He is not a body, bodily pleasure is impossible for Him. As for spiritual pleasure, the philosophers have agreed on affirming it for the Necessary Existent. They argued for it by saying: we claim the occurrence of two things: one is that He is loving of His essence, and the second is that He is rejoicing in His perfections, taking pleasure in them. As for the first, its exposition: that knowledge of a thing when the thing is perfect necessitates love of that thing. Its proof: induction (al-istiqrār). For when we hear of the bravery of Rustam and Isfandiar, an intense love and great inclination arise in our hearts, and there is nothing except our belief in the affirmation of perfections for them that produces that love. If this is established, we say: His knowledge is the best of knowledges, and His perfection is the best of perfections. So when He knows by that perfect knowledge that perfect perfection of His essence, it is necessary that the perfect love results from that perfect knowledge. As for the second, namely that He is rejoicing in His essence, taking pleasure in His perfections: its exposition is that knowledge of a thing of its own perfection, just as it necessitates intense love for it, so too it necessitates rejoicing and taking pleasure. And when that knowledge occurs in the case of God, it is necessary that that rejoicing and that taking pleasure occur. If you know this, we say: two branches branch off from this principle: The first branch: that the proportion of His rejoicing and taking pleasure in the perfections of His essence to the rejoicing of one of us in the perfections of his essence is like the proportion of His knowledge to our knowledge, and like the proportion of the perfections of His essence to the perfections of our essences. And since there is no proportion of His knowledge and perfection to the knowledge and perfections of others, so too there is no proportion of His rejoicing in the perfections of His essence to the rejoicing of others in the perfections of their essences. The second branch: that the separate existents called in the language of the Law: angels, and in the language of the philosophers: intellects and souls, all rejoice in themselves and take pleasure in their perfections. But the degrees of their rejoicing in themselves are according to their degrees in perfections. And just as the most perfect of existents is God—glory be to Him—so too the most magnificent in rejoicing in His essence is the First.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

If you believed that loving Him is the most rational and necessary response to who He is, what would change in your prayer, your priorities, or your daily thoughts starting today?
?

If the latter is weaker, what step will you take this week to learn about His attributes until your heart is kindled with love?

God Rejoices in His Perfections: The Philosophers' Proof
Proof that God rejoices in His perfections through self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical proof that God rejoices in His perfections.

Core Idea
Knowledge of one's own perfection causes both love and rejoicing; God's self-knowledge thus entails His rejoicing and pleasure.

The second claim in the philosophers' argument is that God rejoices in His perfections and takes pleasure in them. This lesson presents the proof, which parallels the previous one: just as knowledge of perfection causes love, so too it causes rejoicing and pleasure. Since God has perfect self-knowledge, He must experience the highest rejoicing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the second claim
Read the opening sentence that states the second claim: God is rejoicing in His essence, taking pleasure in His perfections.
This is a separate claim from the love claim.
2
Understand the parallel proof
The proof mirrors the love proof: knowledge of one's own perfection causes rejoicing. The passage states this as a general principle.
3
Apply to God
Since God has that knowledge, He must have that rejoicing and pleasure.
The conclusion is drawn directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Two Branches: Incomparability of Divine Pleasure and Angels' Pleasure
Two corollaries: incomparability of divine pleasure and angels' pleasure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two important consequences of God's rejoicing: its incomparability to human rejoicing and the rejoicing of angels.

Core Idea
God's rejoicing is unique and incommensurable, and all separate intellects rejoice according to their perfection.

Having established that God rejoices in His perfections, the author now draws two subsidiary conclusions. The first is that God's rejoicing is totally beyond comparison with ours, just as His knowledge and perfections are incomparable. The second is that all separate substances (angels) also rejoice in themselves, but their degrees of rejoicing correspond to their degrees of perfection, with God being the most magnificent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first branch: incomparability
Read the first branch which states that God's rejoicing is proportioned to His knowledge and perfection, which have no proportion to ours, so His rejoicing is likewise incomparable.
This reinforces divine transcendence.
2
Understand the second branch: angels' rejoicing
The second branch discusses that angels (separate intellects) also rejoice and take pleasure in themselves, but in varying degrees based on their perfection.
3
Connect to the main principle
Both branches flow from the principle that rejoicing arises from knowledge of perfection.

الفصل الثاني في اللذة والألم اعلم (۱) . إنك قد عرفت أن اللذة ، إما أن تكون جسمانية وإما أن تكون روحانية . أما اللذة الجسمانية فعلى الله محال ، لأنه لما ثبت أنه تعالى ليس بجسم كانت اللذة الجسمانية عليه ممتنعة . وأما اللذة الروحانية فقد أطبقت الفلاسفة على إثباتها لواجب الوجود . واحتجوا عليه بأن قالوا : ندعي حصول أمرين : أحدهما : كونه تعالى محباً لذاته والثاني : كونه تعالى مبتهجاً بكمالاته ملتذاً بها . أما الأول فتقريره : أن علم الشيء يكون الشيء كاملا يوجب محبة ذلك الشيء . ودليله : الاستقرار . فإنا إذا سمعنا بشجاعة رستم ، واسفيد نار ، حصل في قلبنا حب شديد وميل عظيم ، وليس هناك إلا أن اعتقادنا لثبوت الكمالات لهم ، أفاد ذلك الحب إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : علمه تعالى أفضل العلوم ، وكماله أفضل الكمالات . فإذا علم بذلك العلم الكامل ذلك الكمال التام لذاته ، وجب أن يترتب على ذلك العلم التام : حصول الحب التام . وأما الثاني وهو كونه مبتهجاً بذاته ، ملتذاً بكمالاته . فتقريره : أن علم الشيء بكمال نفسه ، كما يوجب الحب الشديد له ، فكذلك يوجب الابتهاج والالتذاذ . ولما حصل ذلك العلم في حق الله تعالى وجب أن يحصل ذلك الابتهاج وذلك الالتذاذ إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : يتفرع على هذا الأصل فرعان : الفرع الأول : إن نسبة ابتهاجه والتذاذه بكمالات ذاته إلى ابتهاج الواحد منا بكمالات ذاته ، كنسبة علمه إلى علمنا ، وكنسبة كمالات ذاته إلى كمالات ذواتنا ، ولما كان لا نسبة لعلمه وكماله إلى علم غيره وكمالات غيره ، فكذلك لا نسبة لابتهاجه بكمالات ذاته إلى ابتهاج غيره بكمالات ذاته . الفرع الثاني : إن الموجودات المفارقة المسماة في لسان الشريعة : بالملائكة . وفي لسان الفلاسفة : بالعقول والنفوس كلها مبتهجة بأنفسها ملتذة بكمالاتها . إلا أن درجات ابتهاجاتها بأنفسها، على حسب درجاتها في كمالاتها ، وكما أن أكمل الموجودات هو الله - سبحانه - فكذلك أجل مبتهج بذاته هو الأول
The second chapter: On pleasure and pain. Know (1) that you have already known that pleasure is either bodily or spiritual. As for bodily pleasure, it is impossible for God, because since it has been established that He is not a body, bodily pleasure is impossible for Him. As for spiritual pleasure, the philosophers have agreed on affirming it for the Necessary Existent. They argued for it by saying: we claim the occurrence of two things: one is that He is loving of His essence, and the second is that He is rejoicing in His perfections, taking pleasure in them. As for the first, its exposition: that knowledge of a thing when the thing is perfect necessitates love of that thing. Its proof: induction (al-istiqrār). For when we hear of the bravery of Rustam and Isfandiar, an intense love and great inclination arise in our hearts, and there is nothing except our belief in the affirmation of perfections for them that produces that love. If this is established, we say: His knowledge is the best of knowledges, and His perfection is the best of perfections. So when He knows by that perfect knowledge that perfect perfection of His essence, it is necessary that the perfect love results from that perfect knowledge. As for the second, namely that He is rejoicing in His essence, taking pleasure in His perfections: its exposition is that knowledge of a thing of its own perfection, just as it necessitates intense love for it, so too it necessitates rejoicing and taking pleasure. And when that knowledge occurs in the case of God, it is necessary that that rejoicing and that taking pleasure occur. If you know this, we say: two branches branch off from this principle: The first branch: that the proportion of His rejoicing and taking pleasure in the perfections of His essence to the rejoicing of one of us in the perfections of his essence is like the proportion of His knowledge to our knowledge, and like the proportion of the perfections of His essence to the perfections of our essences. And since there is no proportion of His knowledge and perfection to the knowledge and perfections of others, so too there is no proportion of His rejoicing in the perfections of His essence to the rejoicing of others in the perfections of their essences. The second branch: that the separate existents called in the language of the Law: angels, and in the language of the philosophers: intellects and souls, all rejoice in themselves and take pleasure in their perfections. But the degrees of their rejoicing in themselves are according to their degrees in perfections. And just as the most perfect of existents is God—glory be to Him—so too the most magnificent in rejoicing in His essence is the First.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical proof for the spiritual pleasure of God and its implications. The author begins by stating a known division of pleasure into bodily and spiritual. Bodily pleasure is impossible for God because He is not a body. Spiritual pleasure, however, is affirmed by the philosophers for the Necessary Existent (wājib al-wujūd). They argue by claiming two things: that God loves His essence, and that He rejoices in His perfections and takes pleasure in them. The proof for the first claim uses induction: when we hear of the bravery of great heroes like Rustam and Isfandiar, we feel love and inclination in our hearts solely because we believe they possess perfections. Applying this to God: His knowledge is the best knowledge, and His perfection is the best perfection. When He knows His own perfect essence with that perfect knowledge, perfect love necessarily results. The proof for the second claim is similar: knowledge of one's own perfection causes not only love but also rejoicing and pleasure. Since God has that knowledge, He must have that rejoicing and pleasure. From this principle, the author derives two subsidiary points. First, the proportion of God's rejoicing and pleasure to ours is like the proportion of His knowledge and perfections to ours: utterly incomparable, because His attributes are infinite and ours are finite. Second, separate substances (angels in religious language, intellects and souls in philosophical language) all rejoice in themselves and take pleasure in their perfections, but their degrees of rejoicing correspond to their degrees of perfection. Just as God is the most perfect existent, so He is the most magnificent in rejoicing in His essence.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Angels and God experience pleasure in the same way.
Correction: The passage states that their degrees are according to their perfections; God's is the greatest and incomparable.

Checkpoint
According to the first branch, why is God's rejoicing incomparable to ours?
A) Because God's rejoicing is different in kind.
B) Because there is no proportion between God's knowledge and perfection and ours.
C) Because humans cannot experience spiritual pleasure.
D) Because God does not actually rejoice.

Key Takeaway
God's rejoicing is utterly transcendent and incomparable, while all immaterial beings rejoice in proportion to their perfection, with God being the greatest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's rejoicing is utterly incomparable and beyond proportion to yours, how can you ever measure your joy against His? Yet do you sometimes feel that your happiness is what truly matters to God, as if He shares your emotions? Is that not a subtle form of anthropomorphism that this passage explicitly corrects? What would it mean for your relationship with God to accept that His joy is not like yours, that He does not need your happiness to be complete? In your next moment of gratitude, will you acknowledge that your joy is a gift, not a shared experience, and let that awe reshape your worship?
?
The passage says all angels rejoice in themselves to the degree of their perfection. But you, a human, are not a separate intellect; you are a composite being. Do you ever feel a gap between your spiritual aspirations and your bodily inclinations? How does this passage challenge you to seek perfection in a different way—not to be like an angel, but to rejoice in the unique self-knowledge God has given you? Are you content with your own degree of perfection, or do you envy others? Remember, God's rejoicing is not even comparable; what then is the basis for your spiritual pride or despair?
?
This lesson teaches that God's rejoicing is incomparable to yours, and that all beings rejoice according to their perfection. If you take this seriously, it means that your greatest joy should come from knowing your own soul in relation to God, not from external achievements. When you examine your life, what do you find yourself rejoicing in most? Is it your perfections that reflect God, or is it worldly successes? Will you let this truth reorient your pursuit of happiness?

The Two Branches of Divine Delight in His Perfections
Author enumerates two branches derived from divine self-delight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two branches derived from God's delight in His own perfections: the incomparability of His delight to ours, and the delight of separate existents (angels/intellects) proportional to their perfection.

Core Idea
God's delight in His perfections is incomparable, yet all creatures delight proportionately.

This passage builds on the established principle that God necessarily delights in His own perfections. From this, the author derives two important branches. The first emphasizes God's transcendence—His delight is beyond any comparison to ours. The second extends the concept to separate existents, showing that even the highest creatures experience delight in themselves according to their perfection. This deepens our understanding of both divine and creaturely enjoyment.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle of divine delight
Before diving into the branches, remember that the author has already established that God necessarily experiences delight (ibthihāj) and pleasure (iltidhādh) in His own perfections.
This principle is the foundation for the two branches.
2
Examine the first branch: incomparability
Read the first branch carefully. Notice the proportional analogy: God's delight to our delight is like His knowledge to our knowledge and His perfections to our perfections. Conclude that His delight is utterly incomparable.
This reinforces divine transcendence (tanzīh).
3
Examine the second branch: delight of separate existents
Read the second branch. Identify the separate existents (angels/intellects/souls). Understand that they all delight in themselves, but their delight varies according to their perfection. God is the most perfect and thus has the highest delight.
This shows a hierarchy of delight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في حق الله تعالى وجب أن يحصل ذلك الابتهاج وذلك الالتذاذ إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : يتفرع على هذا الأصل فرعان :
الفرع الأول : إن نسبة ابتهاجه والتذاذه بكمالات ذاته إلى ابتهاج الواحد منا بكمالات ذاته ، كنسبة علمه إلى علمنا ، وكنسبة كمالات ذاته إلى كمالات ذواتنا ، ولما كان لا نسبة لعلمه وكماله إلى علم غيره وكمالات غيره ، فكذلك لا نسبة لابتهاجه بكمالات ذاته إلى ابتهاج غيره بكمالات ذاته .
الفرع الثاني : إن الموجودات المفارقة المسماة في لسان الشريعة : بالملائكة . وفي لسان الفلاسفة : بالعقول والنفوس كلها مبتهجة بأنفسها ملتذة بكمالاتها . إلا أن درجات ابتهاجاتها بأنفسها، على حسب درجاتها في كمالاتها ، وكما أن أكمل الموجودات هو الله - سبحانه - فكذلك أجل مبتهج بذاته هو الأول
In the case of God Almighty, it is necessary that this delight and this pleasure occur. If you have understood this, we say: Two branches branch off from this principle:
The first branch: Indeed, the proportion of His delight and pleasure in the perfections of His essence to the delight of any one of us in the perfections of his essence is like the proportion of His knowledge to our knowledge, and like the proportion of the perfections of His essence to the perfections of our essences. And since there is no proportion of His knowledge and perfection to the knowledge and perfections of others, likewise there is no proportion of His delight in the perfections of His essence to the delight of another in the perfections of his essence.
The second branch: Indeed, the separate existents called in the language of the Law: angels, and in the language of the philosophers: intellects and souls, all are delighted in themselves and pleased with their perfections. However, the degrees of their delight in themselves are according to their degrees in perfections. And just as the most perfect of existents is God Almighty, so likewise the most majestic in delight in His essence is the First.
Source pages p. 185

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage that presents two branches derived from a prior principle that God necessarily has delight and pleasure (in His perfections). The author first states that if you understand this, there are two branches.

The first branch explains the incomparability of God's delight: the proportion of His delight to ours is like the proportion of His knowledge to ours and His perfections to ours. Just as His knowledge and perfections have no proportion to others, so too His delight has no proportion to anyone else's delight. This emphasizes divine transcendence (tanzih).

The second branch discusses separate existents (al-mawjudat al-mufariqa) known as angels in religious language and as intellects and souls in philosophical language. They all delight in themselves and enjoy their perfections, but their degrees of delight vary according to their degrees of perfection. The highest in delight is God, just as He is the most perfect in existence. This shows that even the highest creatures experience delight proportional to their perfection, but God's delight is utterly unique.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think God experiences delight in a manner similar to human delight, subject to change or limitation.
Correction: The first branch explicitly states that God's delight is incomparable to human delight, just as His knowledge and perfections are beyond proportion. God's delight is unique and transcendent.

Checkpoint

A) It is similar in degree but different in kind.
B) It is like the proportion of His knowledge to our knowledge and His perfections to our perfections.
C) It is higher but still comparable.
D) It is exactly proportionate to His majesty.

Key Takeaway
God's delight in His perfections is incomparable to any creature's, and even the highest separate existents (angels) delight in themselves only to the degree of their perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The angels delight in themselves according to their degree of perfection. Do you delight in your own strengths and accomplishments, or do you only seek validation from others? In light of this lesson, how can you cultivate a healthy self-appreciation that points back to God as the source of all perfection?
?
This lesson teaches that God's delight is incomparable. Have you ever thought of God as delighting in His attributes? How does that change your relationship with Him, and what would it mean for you to delight in His perfections rather than your own? Will you now, in your prayers, praise God not just for what He gives but for who He is?

The Fifth Chapter: Divine Hearing and Sight – Three Divisions
Chapter heading outlining three research divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold division of the chapter on God's hearing and sight, which systematically covers definition, affirmation, and refutation.

Core Idea
Divine hearing and sight are explored through definition, proof, and polemic.

This lesson introduces the fifth chapter of the text, which focuses on the divine attributes of hearing (sam‘) and sight (baṣar). The author presents a clear three-part plan: first, investigating the essence of these attributes; second, establishing proofs that affirm them for God; and third, examining the arguments of those who deny them. This structured approach will guide the subsequent discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the chapter heading
Begin by reading the Arabic heading and its translation to understand the topic and the author's announced plan.
The heading uses 'الباب الخامس' indicating this is the fifth chapter.
2
Identify the three divisions
The passage explicitly lists three sections. Identify each one: (1) essence of hearing and sight, (2) proofs for affirmation, (3) proofs of deniers.
These divisions form the skeleton of the chapter.
3
Understand the logical flow
Notice that the order is logical: first define the concepts, then prove them, then respond to opponents. This is a common pattern in theological works.
4
Prepare for deep study
This lesson sets the stage. In subsequent lessons, each division will be explored in detail. For now, grasp the overall structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الخامس في كونه تعالى سميعاً بصيراً
هذا البحث أيضاً ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام :
أولها : البحث عن ماهية السمع والبصر
·
وثانيها: البحث عن الدلائل الدالة على إثبات تلك الصفة الله - سبحانه -
وثالثها : البحث عن دلائل من ينكر ثبوتها الله - سبحانه -
The Fifth Chapter: Concerning Him Almighty being Hearing and Seeing
This research is also divided into three sections:
The first of them: research into the essence (mahiyya) of hearing and sight.
·
And the second of them: research into the proofs indicating the affirmation of that attribute for God Almighty.
And the third of them: research into the proofs of those who deny its affirmation for God Almighty.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage is a chapter heading and a classification of the upcoming discussion into three logical divisions. The author introduces the fifth chapter on God being Hearing (Sami‘) and Seeing (Basir). He then states that this research is divided into three parts.

The first division is a conceptual inquiry: research into the essence (mahiyya) of hearing and sight—presumably defining what these attributes mean in the context of God.

The second division is a constructive theological proof: research into the proofs that establish these attributes for God.

The third division is a polemical inquiry: research into the proofs of those who deny these attributes for God (e.g., the Mu‘tazila or others who might reject literal hearing and sight).

This sets up a systematic approach: define, prove, and refute objections.

Key Takeaway
The chapter on God's hearing and sight is organized into three logical divisions: defining the essence, proving the attributes, and refuting deniers.

Vision Beyond Knowledge: Proof of Impression
Definition and proof: vision as an additional state beyond knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that vision is an additional state beyond mere knowledge, and how the physical impression of the image in the eye is proven through two empirical observations.

Core Idea
Seeing is not just knowing; it involves a physical impression in the eye.

How do we know that seeing is more than just knowing? This passage begins by drawing an intuitive distinction between having knowledge of an image and actually seeing it. It then presents a physical explanation—that the image is impressed in the pupil—and supports it with two simple but powerful observations. Let us explore these arguments step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the English translation. Notice how the author structures the argument: a thought experiment, a conclusion, then two supporting proofs.
The passage uses a familiar example (Zayd's image) to make the point accessible.
2
Understand the thought experiment
Consider the example of looking at Zayd's image, then closing your eyes. You still have knowledge of the image, but you notice a clear difference. What does this show about vision?
The difference is not in knowledge but in an additional state called 'seeing'.
3
Examine the two proofs
Identify the first proof (sun disk afterimage) and the second proof (green garden afterimage). How do they demonstrate that the image is physically impressed in the pupil?
Both proofs rely on persistent afterimages that cannot be dismissed, showing the impression is real.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some might think seeing is just a form of knowing. This passage argues it is additional. How does this distinction matter for understanding divine attributes?
This lays groundwork for discussing God's vision without attributing physical impression to Him.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث عن ماهية السمع والبصر. أما البحث الأول: فاعلم أنا إذا نظرنا إلى صورة زيد، وحصل علمنا بتلك الصورة على أكمل الوجوه، ثم غمضنا العين وجدنا تفرقة بديهية بين الحالتين، مع أن العلم بتلك الصورة حاصل في الوقتين، فعلمنا بهذا الطريق أن الإبصار حالة زائدة على العلم. واختلف الناس فيه. فقال بعضهم: إن ذلك يرجع إلى أن صورة المرئي وشكله يحصل في الحدقة وينطبع فيها، فالتفاوت عائد إلى تأثر الحدقة بتلك الصورة. واحتجوا على إثبات هذا الانطباع بوجوه: الأول: إن من نظر إلى قرص الشمس نظراً طويلاً تاماً، ثم غمض عينيه فإنه يجد قرص الشمس منطبعاً في خياله انطباعاً تاماً، حتى أنه بعد تغميض العين كأنه ينظر إلى ذلك القرص ويشاهده، ولو أراد الإنسان دفع ذلك الخيال لم يقدر عليه، فعلمنا أن صورة المرئي ترتسم في الحدقة. الثاني: إن من نظر إلى روضة خضراء نظراً طويلاً بالاستقصاء التام، ثم حول حدقته إلى شيء أبيض اللون، فإنه يرى ذلك الأبيض كأنه لون ممزوج.
Investigation into the essence of hearing and sight. As for the first investigation: know that when we look at the image of Zayd, and our knowledge of that image attains the most complete manner, then we close the eye, we find an intuitive distinction between the two states, even though knowledge of that image is present in both moments. Thus we know by this method that seeing is a state additional to knowledge. People differed concerning it. Some said: That returns to the fact that the form and shape of the seen object occurs in the pupil and is impressed in it; the difference is due to the pupil's being affected by that form. They argued for proving this impression with proofs: The first: He who looks at the disk of the sun with a long, complete look, then closes his eyes, finds the disk of the sun impressed in his imagination completely, so that after closing the eye it is as if he is looking at that disk and beholding it, and if a person wanted to repel that imagination he would not be able to. So we know that the image of the seen object is imprinted in the pupil. The second: He who looks at a green garden with a long, exhaustive look, then turns his pupil to something white in color, he sees that white as if it is a mixed color.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It defines the nature of sight (ibṣār) as a state additional to mere knowledge, and then argues for the physical impression of the image in the eye. The author begins by setting up a thought experiment: when we see Zayd, we have knowledge of his image. But if we close our eyes, we still have that knowledge, yet we notice an obvious difference between seeing and simply knowing. This shows that seeing (ibṣār) is an extra state beyond knowledge. Some people explained this difference by saying the image and shape of the seen object occurs and is impressed in the pupil (ḥadaqa), so the variation between seeing and not seeing is due to the pupil's being affected. They then provide proofs for this impression. The first proof: after staring at the sun disk and closing the eyes, one still sees the sun disk vividly in the imagination, unable to dispel it. This shows the image is imprinted in the pupil. The second proof: after staring at a green garden and then looking at a white object, the white appears tinged with green, again demonstrating the persistence of the image in the eye. These proofs aim to establish that sight involves a physical impression, not just abstract knowledge.

Key Terms
إبصار
علم
حدقة
انطباع
صورة المرئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seeing is just a form of knowing; there is no difference between knowledge and vision.
Correction: The passage demonstrates an intuitive distinction: after closing the eyes, knowledge remains but the experience of seeing is absent. Therefore, vision is a state additional to knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the first proof that the image of the seen object is imprinted in the pupil?
A) Seeing Zayd after closing the eyes proves the image remains.
B) After staring at the sun disk, closing the eyes still shows the disk impressed.
C) The green garden afterimage shows the image is in the brain.
D) People agreed that vision is knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Vision is not reducible to knowledge; it involves a physical impression of the image in the eye, as shown by afterimage experiments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that seeing is an additional state beyond knowing. When you read the Quran, do you merely know the meanings of the words, or do you truly 'see' their reality as if they were impressed upon your heart? What would it take for you to move from knowledge to visionary certainty?
?
The afterimage of the sun remains even after you close your eyes—a persistent trace. What spiritual 'impressions' have you allowed to remain in your heart from your past actions? Are there sins or distractions that still color your perception of truth, like a green tint on white? Will you consciously purify your vision?

Sight is Additional to Knowledge – The Self-Evident Distinction
Sanusi differentiates sight from knowledge via a self-evident experiment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that sight is a distinct state beyond mere knowledge, and how the author demonstrates this through a simple self-evident observation.

Core Idea
Seeing is not the same as knowing – there is a clear difference.

The author begins by asking what vision is. He points to a basic experience: when you look at someone and then close your eyes, you still have knowledge of their image, but the experience of seeing is gone. This simple test shows that sight adds something beyond knowledge. Let's read his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author uses a concrete example (looking at Zaid) to make his point.
Pay attention to the phrase 'تفرقة بديهية' – a self-evident distinction.
2
Identify the two states
The author compares two moments: when the eyes are open (seeing) and when they are closed (only knowing). What is present in both? What is different?
Knowledge remains in both, but sight is only present in the first state.
3
Reflect on the theological significance
Ask yourself: if sight is extra to knowledge, then Allah's sight must be a distinct attribute, not merely His knowledge. This becomes important in later discussions about divine attributes.
This connects the lesson to broader kalam themes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث عن ماهية السمع والبصر. البحث الأول: فاعلم أنا إذا نظرنا إلى صورة زيد، وحصل علمنا بتلك الصورة على أكمل الوجوه، ثم غمضنا العين وجدنا تفرقة بديهية بين الحالتين، مع أن العلم بتلك الصورة حاصل في الوقتين فعلمنا بهذا الطريق أن الإبصار حالة زائدة على العلم. واختلف الناس فيه. فقال بعضهم: إن ذلك يرجع إلى أن صورة المرئي وشكله يحصل في الحدقة وينطبع فيها، فالتفاوت عائد إلى تأثر الحدقة بتلك الصورة. واحتجوا على إثبات هذا الانطباع بوجوه:
الأول: إن من نظر إلى قرص الشمس نظر طويلاً تاماً، ثم غمض عينيه فإنه يجد قرص الشمس منطبعاً في خياله انطباعاً تاماً، حتى أنه بعد تغميض العين كأنه ينظر إلى ذلك القرص ويشاهده ولو أراد الإنسان دفع ذلك الخيال لم يقدر عليه، فعلمنا أن صورة المرئي ترتسم في الحدقة.
الثاني: إن من نظر إلى روضة خضراء نظرا طويلا بالاستقصاء التام، ثم حول حدقته إلى شيء أبيض اللون، فإنه يرى ذلك الأبيض كأنه لون ممزوج بالبياض مع الخضرة، ولولا أن العين قد تأثرت بتلك الخضرة، لما حصل هذا الامتزاج، أما لما تكيفت الحدقة بلون الخضرة، ثم نظر إلى الجسم الأبيض امتزجت الخضرة الحاصلة في الحدقة بالبياض القائم بذلك الشوب، فصار المرئي كأنه ملون بلون ممتزج من هذين اللونين.
الثالث: إنا إذا نظرنا إلى الحدقة المقابلة لوجه الانسان رأينا صورة الوجه في تلك الحدقة وذلك يدل على أن صورة المرىء انتقشت في الحدقة وارتسمت فيها.
الرابع: إنا إذا نظرنا إلى قرص الشمس وهورها الكامل، تأثرت الحدقة وضعفت. وإذا داومنا على ذلك النظر حصل في الحدقة كلال وضعف، وتفرق في أجزائه وذلك يدل على أن الحدقة تتأثر من صورة المرئي.
Investigation about the essence of hearing and sight. First investigation: Know that when we look at Zaid's image, and our knowledge of that image is obtained in the most complete manner, then we close our eyes, we find a self-evident distinction between the two states, despite the fact that knowledge of that image exists in both times. Thus we know by this method that sight is a state additional to knowledge. People differed concerning it. Some said: That [difference] returns to the fact that the image and form of the visible object occurs in the pupil and is imprinted in it, so the variation is due to the impression of the pupil by that image. And they argued for proving this impression with proofs:
First: That if someone looks at the disk of the sun with a long complete look, then closes his eyes, he finds the disk of the sun imprinted in his imagination completely, so that after closing his eyes it is as if he is looking at that disk and seeing it, and if a person wanted to repel that image he would not be able. Thus we know that the image of the visible object is inscribed in the pupil.
Second: That if someone looks at a green meadow with a long exhaustive look, then turns his pupil to something white, he sees that white as if it is a color mixed with whiteness and greenness. And if the eye had not been affected by that greenness, this mixing would not have occurred. But when the pupil is tinged with the color of greenness, then he looks at the white body, the greenness present in the pupil mixes with the whiteness subsisting in that object, so the visible becomes as if colored with a color mixed from these two colors.
Third: That if we look at the pupil facing a person's face, we see the image of the face in that pupil. That indicates that the image of the visible object is engraved in the pupil and inscribed in it.
Fourth: That if we look at the disk of the sun with its full illumination, the pupil is affected and weakens. And if we continue that looking, fatigue and weakness occur in the pupil, and dispersion in its parts. That indicates that the pupil is affected by the image of the visible object.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of vision and presents the first opinion that visual perception consists in the physical impression of the image on the pupil. The author begins with a self-evident observation: when you look at an object with eyes open and then close them, you still have knowledge of the object, but there is a clear difference between seeing and merely knowing. This shows that sight is an additional state beyond mere knowledge. Then the author states that people differed about the nature of this extra state. The first group claims that the difference is due to the image actually imprinting on the pupil. They advance four empirical proofs. The first proof from afterimages: after staring at the sun, the image persists in the imagination even with eyes closed, unremovable. The second proof from color mixing: after staring at green, a white surface appears tinted green, indicating the eye is physically affected. The third proof from reflected images: you can see the image of a face reflected in another person's pupil, demonstrating that the image is inscribed there. The fourth proof from fatigue: prolonged staring at the sun weakens the eye, showing physical impact. These proofs aim to establish that the eye is physically affected by the visible object.

Key Terms
الإبصار
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that sight is identical to knowledge.
Correction: The author shows that knowledge remains even when sight is absent, proving they are distinct.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what happens when you close your eyes after looking at Zaid?
A) Knowledge of Zaid disappears
B) Knowledge of Zaid remains but sight is gone
C) Both sight and knowledge remain
D) Neither sight nor knowledge remains

Key Takeaway
Sight is an extra state on top of knowledge, as shown by the difference between seeing and merely knowing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are reading this lesson, so you have both sight and knowledge. When you close your eyes, the knowledge remains. But what about the truths of faith you claim to know—do you truly 'see' them with the heart, or are you content with mere intellectual knowledge? What one truth can you contemplate today to transform knowledge into inner sight?
?
The author uses a simple experiment to show that sight is an extra gift. Are you grateful for your ability to see—not just physical sight, but the spiritual vision to perceive Allah's signs? Write down one blessing you have 'seen' today that you previously only 'knew' about. How will you act on that realization?

Four Proofs for the Impression Theory of Vision
Four proofs from afterimages, color mixing, reflection, and fatigue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four empirical arguments used to prove that vision involves the physical impression of the image on the pupil.

Core Idea
The eye is physically affected by what we see – like a camera.

After establishing that sight is distinct from knowledge, the author presents the view of some scholars who explain this extra state as the imprinting of the image on the pupil. They provide four everyday examples to support this. Let's examine each proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage of four proofs
Read the Arabic and translation for all four proofs. Notice how each proof uses a different phenomenon (afterimage, color mixing, reflection, fatigue).
These are empirical arguments, not purely philosophical.
2
Analyze the first proof: afterimage
The first proof: after staring at the sun, the image remains even with eyes closed. What does this suggest about the eye?
The image is 'imprinted' – it cannot be willfully removed.
3
Analyze the second proof: color mixing
After staring at green, a white surface appears greenish. Why does this happen according to the argument?
The pupil becomes tinged with green, affecting subsequent vision.
4
Consider the third and fourth proofs
Third: you see a face reflected in another's pupil. Fourth: the eye weakens after staring at the sun. How do these support the impression theory?
Both show the eye is physically affected by the image.
5
Reflect on the overall claim
These four proofs aim to show that the image physically impresses on the pupil. Do you find them convincing? Why might they matter for understanding vision?
This connects to medieval theories of vision and the nature of perception.
