Vision as a Distinct Perception from Knowledge
The author presents the second opinion that vision is distinct from knowledge, with two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that visual perception is a state distinct from intellectual knowledge, even when physical impression occurs, and the proof from the impossibility of large images fitting in the small eye.

Core Idea
Seeing is not merely physical impression; it is a distinct perception.

In this passage, the author presents the second view among scholars regarding the nature of vision. While some attribute the difference between seeing and not seeing solely to physical affection of the eye, others argue that even when such affection occurs, visual perception itself is a unique state separate from intellectual knowledge. Two proofs are offered, the first based on the impossibility of a large image fitting in the small eye, and the second (though incomplete) suggesting that the reception of an image is not the same as seeing.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context
The author has been discussing two states: seeing and not seeing. Some say the difference is due to the eye being affected by the visible object. The second opinion accepts the affection but insists that the perception itself is different.
2
Read the first proof
Consider the example of seeing the hemisphere of the world. The image cannot physically fit in the eye, yet we see it. Therefore, seeing is distinct from impression. Also note the intuitive distinction between seeing and knowing.
The proof is based on the size disparity argument.
3
Examine the second proof
The passage begins to explain that the affection of the eye is the reception of an image, but this reception is not the same as the act of seeing. The proof is cut off but the idea is that the state of receiving an image is different from the state of perception.
The second proof is incomplete in the source.
4
Reflect on the implications
This argument supports the idea that vision is a separate cognitive faculty, not reducible to physical processes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: من الناس من قال: التفرقة الحاصلة بين الحالتين عائدة إلى أن الحدقة تتأثر من المرىء حال النظر إليه، ولم يحصل هذا التأثر في الحالة الثانية. ومن الناس من قال: إن هذا التأثر حاصل، ولا نزاع فيه، ولكن مع ذلك فالإدراك البصري في نفسه حالة مغايرة للإدراك العلمي. والذي يدل عليه وجوه:
الأول: إنا نبصر نصف كرة العالم بالعين، ومن المعلوم أن صورة نصف كرة العالم يمتنع انطباعها في العين، لأن العظيم لا ينطبع في الصغير، فيثبت أن صورة نصف كرة العالم غير منطبعة في العين، مع أن رؤية نصف كرة العالم حاصلة، فوجب أن تكون الرؤية مغايرة للإنطباع. ثم إن الحالة المسماة بالرؤية ممتازة عن الحالة المسماة بالعلم، امتيازاً معلوماً بالضرورة، فعلمنا أن هذا التفاوت عائد إلى الإدراكين في أنفسهما.
والثاني: إن تأثر العين وانفصاله عن الشيء لا معنى له، إلا أنه حصلت صورة مساوية لصورة ذلك المرىء فيها، وقبولها لتلك الصورة بعد عدمها، وحصولها فيها بعد لا حصولها هو المسمى بالتأثر والقبول والتصور. وهذه الحالة
When you know this, we say: Among people are those who said: The difference occurring between the two states is due to the pupil being affected by the visible object at the time of looking, and this affection does not occur in the second state. And among people are those who said: This affection is present, and there is no dispute about it, but despite that, visual perception in itself is a state different from intellectual perception. What indicates this are proofs:
First: We see the hemisphere of the world with the eye, and it is known that the image of the hemisphere of the world is impossible to imprint in the eye, because the large cannot be imprinted in the small. Thus it is established that the image of the hemisphere of the world is not imprinted in the eye, while the seeing of the hemisphere of the world occurs. So it is necessary that seeing is different from impression. Then the state called seeing is distinguished from the state called knowledge by a necessarily known distinction. Thus we know that this variation is due to the two perceptions themselves.
Second: The affection of the eye and its separation from the thing means nothing except that an image equal to the image of that visible object has occurred in it, and its acceptance of that image after its non-existence, and its occurrence in it after non-occurrence is what is called affection, acceptance, and representation. And this state...
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents the second opinion on the nature of vision. The author first summarizes the first opinion that the difference between seeing and not seeing is purely due to the eye being physically affected by the image. Then he presents the second opinion, which accepts that such affection occurs but argues that visual perception itself is a distinct state from intellectual knowledge (ilm). Two proofs are given. The first proof is based on the impossibility of a large image (the entire hemisphere of the world) being imprinted in the small eye. Yet we do see the hemisphere, so seeing must be something other than impression. Furthermore, seeing is intuitively distinct from knowledge. The second proof (incomplete) explains that the affection of the eye is simply the reception of an image, but this reception is not the same as the act of seeing. The passage argues for a separate faculty of vision beyond mere physical impression.

الإدراك البصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a large image cannot fit in the eye, then we cannot see large objects.
Correction: The proof demonstrates that seeing occurs even without physical impression, so vision is not dependent on the image fitting in the eye.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof of the second opinion, why cannot the image of the hemisphere of the world be imprinted in the eye?
A) Because the eye is too dark
B) Because the large cannot fit in the small
C) Because the hemisphere is not visible
D) Because the eye is spherical

Key Takeaway
Visual perception is a distinct state from intellectual knowledge and is not reducible to physical impression, as shown by the impossibility of large images fitting in the small eye.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at something as vast as the sky, do you ever pause to realize that your seeing is not just a physical occurrence but a distinct act of perception that Allah has gifted you? How does this awareness change the way you value your sight and use it to worship Him?
?
The passage shows that seeing is different from merely knowing intellectually. Is there a truth about your faith that you 'know' but have not truly 'seen' in your heart? What would it take for you to move from intellectual acceptance to a direct, living perception of that reality?
?
If visual perception is distinct from physical impression, what does that say about the reality behind the images you see? Are you content with the superficial appearances of things, or do you strive to perceive the deeper spiritual truths that lie beyond them?

Can Disclosure Be Attributed to God?
Discusses whether the superadded perceptive state can be attributed to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two possibilities regarding the attribution of the superadded perceptive state (tajallī) to God and why the author suspends judgment while leaning towards affirmation.

Core Idea
The disclosure of God is not ruled out by dependence on physical organs; it may exist without them, as God's existence is self-sufficient.

In this second investigation, the author tackles a crucial question: can the state of manifestation (tajallī) be attributed to God? Having established that this state is distinct from physical impressions, he now examines two possibilities: either it requires physical instruments (and thus cannot apply to God), or it can occur without them (as with separate entities). By drawing an analogy with existence, he shows that the second possibility is plausible, leading him to suspend judgment but ultimately favor affirmation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English translation to get the flow of the argument. The passage ends mid-sentence but the argument is complete for this page.
Notice the use of technical terms like 'ta'aththur' and 'ālāt'.
2
Identify the two possibilities
Find where the author states possibility A (conditional on instruments) and possibility B (possible without instruments).
The author uses 'يحتمل' to introduce each possibility.
3
Understand the analogy
Examine the analogy with the existence of contingent beings and the Necessary Existence. How does it support possibility B?
The analogy draws a parallel between perception and existence to show that what is conditional for some may be self-sufficient for others.
4
Follow the conclusion
Notice that the author suspends judgment (tawaqquf) but then says the more appropriate principle (al-awla wa al-akhlaq) leads to affirmation. Why does he lean this way?
The principle of what is more fitting for God's majesty suggests that such a state should be attributed to Him.
5
Reflect on implications
Think about the implications for divine attributes: can God have states that typically require physical organs?
This ties into broader discussions about God's attributes being beyond human limitations.

البحث الثاني : في أنه هل دل الدليل على إثبات هذا النوع من التجلي والانكشاف لله تعالى ؟ اعلم . أنا وإن دللنا على أن هذه الحالة مغايرة لتأثر العين والأذن ، إلا أنا متوفقون في مقام آخر ، فإنه من المحتمل أن يكون حصول هذه الحالة مشروطاً بحصول هذا التأثر في العين والأذن . والدليل عليه : أنه كلما اختلت هذه الآلات اختلت هذه الإدراكات ، وكلما كانت الآلات سليمة كانت الإدراكات سليمة ، فهذا يوهم كونها مشروطة بسلامة هذه الآلات ، ويحتمل أيضاً : أن يقال : إن حصول هذا النوع من الإدراكات للنفس البشرية ، وإن كانت مشروطة بسلامة هذه الآلات ، إلا أنه (۳) لا يمتنع حصولها في حق المفارقات (4) من غير هذه الآلات، كما أن حصول الوجود لهذه الممكنات مشروط بتأثير المؤثر فيه ، ثم إن الوجود حاصل لواجب الوجود لذاته ، من غير حصول إلى حصول هذا المؤثر . فهذان الاحتمالان قائمان. وعلى التقدير الأول يكون إثبات هذه الصفة لله محالاً وعلى التقدير الثاني لا يكون محالاً ، فلا جرم وجب التوقف . إلا أن المقدمة المبنية على الأولى والأخلق يقتضي إثباتها لله تعالي . وذلك لأنه سبحانه
The second investigation: whether evidence indicates the affirmation of this type of manifestation and disclosure for God Almighty. Know that although we have shown that this state is different from the impression on the eye and ear, we are still at another position: it is probable that the occurrence of this state is conditional upon the occurrence of this impression in the eye and ear. The evidence for it is that whenever these instruments are impaired, these perceptions are impaired; when instruments are sound, perceptions are sound. This suggests that they are conditional on the soundness of these instruments. It is also possible to say: The occurrence of this type of perception for the human soul, although conditional on the soundness of these instruments, it is not impossible for it to occur in the case of separate entities (al-mufāriqāt) without these instruments, just as the occurrence of existence for these contingent beings is conditional upon the influence of an influencer upon it, then existence is necessary for the Necessary Existence by itself, without the occurrence of that influencer. So these two possibilities stand. On the first estimation, affirming this attribute for God is impossible; on the second estimation, it is not impossible. Therefore, suspension (tawaqquf) is necessary. However, the principle based on the first and more appropriate (al-awla wa al-akhlaq) necessitates its affirmation for God Almighty. And that is because He, glorified be He,...
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a second investigation into whether the perceptive state of disclosure (tajallī/inkishāf) can be attributed to God. The author has proven that hearing and vision are states beyond mere physical impressions. Now he considers two possibilities: either this state is conditional upon physical organs (so cannot apply to God), or it can exist without them, as existence is contingent beings relies on an influencer but God is self-sufficient. He concludes that both possibilities are viable, so he suspends judgment. However, the sounder and more appropriate principle (al-muqaddamatu al-mabniyyatu ʿalā al-ūlā wa al-akhlaq) requires affirming it for God. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is that the appropriate principle leads to affirmation.

Key Terms
تجلي (tajallī)
انكشاف (inkishāf)
تأثر (ta'aththur)
آلات (ālāt)
مفارقات (mufāriqāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author definitively concludes that disclosure cannot be attributed to God because it requires physical organs.
Correction: The author suspends judgment and indicates that the more appropriate view is that it can be attributed to God, based on the principle of what is more fitting.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities regarding the perceptive state of disclosure?
A) It is either identical to the impression or not.
B) It is either conditional on physical organs or can occur without them.
C) It is either eternal or created.
D) It is either possible or impossible for humans.

Key Takeaway
The attribution of disclosure (tajallī) to God is not directly ruled out; it depends on whether such a state can exist without physical instruments, and the more appropriate principle affirms it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's knowledge, don't you unconsciously measure it against your own limited, sense-dependent knowing? This passage challenges that. Can you truly imagine a way of knowing that doesn't rely on anything physical—and are you willing to accept that God knows in that way, even if you can't fathom it?
?
The author suspends judgment between two possibilities, yet says the more appropriate and fitting principle requires affirming disclosure for God. Are you content to accept theological conclusions that go beyond your full comprehension, or do you demand that everything about God fit neatly into your categories?
?
You've read that what is conditional for humans may be essential for God. Take one of your own attributes—say, your ability to learn—and ask: how does it fall short of God's attribute? Are you ready to let go of the subtle anthropomorphism that creeps into your worship?

The Third Proof: Vision and Hearing as Additional States
Razi argues that vision and hearing are states beyond knowledge and physical affect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's third proof that vision and hearing are states beyond mere knowledge and physical affect.

Core Idea
Vision and hearing are not reducible to physical affect or knowledge; they are superior states of disclosure.

Razi presents a logical argument to demonstrate that perception—specifically hearing and vision—cannot be identical to physical affect or knowledge. He uses a conditional proof: if hearing were simply the affect of the ear, then stronger affect would imply stronger hearing, which is false. Hence, hearing must be an additional state. The same applies to vision. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the argument's logical structure come through.
Notice the conditional phrase 'لو كانت... لوجب' which sets up the proof.
2
Identify the conditional structure
Locate the if-then reasoning: if hearing equals affect, then stronger affect implies stronger hearing. Understand why the consequent is false.
The falsehood of the consequent leads to rejecting the antecedent.
3
Understand the conclusion
The conclusion states that vision and hearing are additional states beyond knowledge and physical affect, providing a stronger disclosure.
This upgrades the concept of perception from mere knowledge to a higher form of manifestation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : ما ذكرناه في الإبصار وهو أنه لو كانت الحالة المسماة بالسماع عبارة عن هذا التأثر ، وجب أن يقال : كلما كان التأثر أقوى وأمكن وجب أن يكون السماع أكمل وأقوى ولما كان الثاني باطلا . كان المقدم باطلاً . قيثبت بهذه الوجوه التي أوضحناها ، أن الإبصار حـالـة زائدة على العلم ، وعلى تأثر العين ، أو أن السماع حالة زائدة على العلم وعلى تأثر الأذن . ولا شك أنها حالة تفيد نوعاً من الانكشاف والتجلي ، أكمل وأقوى من الحالة المسماة بالعلم .
The third argument: what we mentioned concerning vision, which is that if the state called hearing were [nothing but] this affect, then it would be necessary to say: whenever the affect is stronger and more possible, then hearing must be more complete and stronger. And since the second [consequent] is false, the antecedent is false. Thus it is established, by these aspects which we have clarified, that vision is an additional state beyond knowledge and beyond the affect of the eye, or that hearing is an additional state beyond knowledge and beyond the affect of the ear. And there is no doubt that it is a state that brings about a kind of disclosure and manifestation, more complete and stronger than the state called knowledge.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that vision and hearing are states distinct from mere knowledge and physical affect. The author argues by conditional: if hearing were identical to the physical affect of the ear, then stronger affect would necessarily result in stronger hearing. But this conditional consequence is false, because there are cases where the affect is strong yet perception is weak, or vice versa. Therefore, the antecedent is false: hearing is not merely physical affect. The same reasoning applies to vision. The conclusion is that both vision and hearing are additional states (حالة زائدة) beyond knowledge and beyond the physical affect of the sense organs. Moreover, these states are described as providing a kind of disclosure (انكشاف) and manifestation (تجلي) that is more complete and stronger than the state called knowledge. This establishes that perception is not reducible to either knowledge or physical impression.

Key Terms
الحجة الثالثة
الإبصار
السماع
التأثر
حالة زائدة
العلم
الانكشاف
التجلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision and hearing are purely physical responses of the sense organs.
Correction: Razi shows they are independent cognitive states beyond physical affect and even beyond knowledge, providing a stronger disclosure.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, if hearing were merely the physical affect of the ear, what would necessarily follow?
A) Hearing would be identical to knowledge.
B) Whenever the affect is stronger, hearing would be more complete and stronger.
C) Hearing would require no affect at all.
D) Hearing would be weaker when the affect is stronger.

Key Takeaway
Vision and hearing are additional states that provide a more complete disclosure than knowledge and are not reducible to physical affect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever had an experience where seeing or hearing something gave you a conviction that no amount of intellectual knowledge could match? How does that experience align with Razi's claim that perception provides a 'more complete disclosure' than knowledge? What does that tell you about the limits of relying solely on rational arguments in your faith?
?
If vision and hearing are not reducible to physical processes but are superior cognitive states, how should that affect the way you use your senses in worship? Are there moments when you treat your sight and hearing as mere biological functions rather than as gifts that reveal deeper truths? What would change if you approached each act of seeing and hearing as a potential moment of divine disclosure?
?
The author describes these states as providing a disclosure 'more complete and stronger than the state called knowledge.' In your own life, have you prioritized 'knowing about' God (through books and lectures) over 'perceiving' Him through His signs and your own direct experience? What practical step can you take today to seek that deeper disclosure?

The Two Possibilities: Hesitation in Affirming Divine Vision
Razi presents two possibilities regarding the conditionality of perception and concludes with necessary hesitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational reflection leads to hesitation in affirming vision and hearing for Allah, given their dependence on organs in humans.

Core Idea
Human perception depends on organs, but that does not necessarily apply to God.

In this discussion, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi examines whether rational proof can establish that Allah possesses the kind of direct disclosure we experience through vision and hearing. He begins by noting that while these perceptions are distinct from mere physical affect, their occurrence in humans is correlated with healthy sensory organs. This raises a question: Are they always conditioned by such instruments? Razi presents two possibilities, leading to a necessary hesitation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Context
This is the second discussion (al-bahth al-thani) about whether manifestation and disclosure can be affirmed for Allah. Read the title carefully.
2
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the two possibilities presented.
The author uses a careful, conditional structure.
3
Identify the Evidence for Conditionality
Find the evidence that perception is conditioned by organs: the correlation between instrument health and perception quality.
This is an empirical observation.
4
Analyse the Second Possibility
Understand the analogy: just as contingent existence requires a cause but the Necessary Existent does not, perception may not require organs for separate entities.
This is a rational argument by analogy.
5
Recognize the Conclusion of Hesitation
See how the two possibilities lead to a suspension of judgment (tawaqquf) – we cannot decide based on this evidence alone.
The author uses 'فلا جرم وجب التوقف'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : في أنه هل دل الدليل على إثبات هذا النوع من التجلي والانكشاف الله تعالى ؟ اعلم . أنا وإن دللنا على أن هذه الحالة مغايرة لتأثر العين والأذن ، إلا أنا متوفقون في مقام آخر ، فإنه من المحتمل أن يكون حصول هذه الحالة مشروطاً بحصول هذا التأثر في العين والأذن . والدليل عليه : أنه كلما اختلت هذه الآلات اختلت هذه الإدراكات ، وكلما كانت الآلات سليمة كانت الإدراكات سليمة ، فهذا يوهم كونها مشروطة بسلامة هذه الآلات ، ويحتمل أيضاً : أن يقال : إن حصول هذا النوع من الإدراكات للنفس البشرية ، وإن كانت مشروطة بسلامة هذه الآلات ، إلا أنه لا يمتنع حصولها في حق المفارقات من غير هذه الآلات، كما أن حصول الوجود لهذه الممكنات مشروط بتأثير المؤثر فيه ، ثم إن الوجود حاصل لواجب الوجود لذاته ، من غير حصول إلى حصول هذا المؤثر . فهذان الاحتمالان قائمان. وعلى التقدير الأول يكون إثبات هذه الصفة الله محالاً . وعلى التقدير الثاني لا يكون محالاً ، فلا جرم وجب التوقف . إلا أن المقدمة المبنية على الأولى والأخلق يقتضي إثباتها الله تعالي . وذلك لأنه سبحانه أكمل الموجودات ، فوجب أن يكون موصوفاً بكل صفات الكمالات ، ومعلوم أن الحالة المسماة بالإبصار والسماع صفة من صفات الكمال . فإنا بينا أن من علم شيئاً ثم رآه ، فإنه يجد في نفسه كأنه انتقل من نقصان إلى كمال ، ومن خفاء إلى جلاء ، فإذا كانت هذه الحالة من صفات الكمال ، وكان عدمها من صفات النقصان ، وكان الحق سبحانه وتعالى منزهاً عن جميع صفات النقص ، واجب الاتصاف بجميع صفات الكمال ، لزم بمقتضى العقل الأول أن نعتقد كونه تعالى موصوفاً به ، منزهاً عن النقص الحاصل بسبب فقدانه .
The second discussion: concerning whether the proof indicates the affirmation of this type of manifestation and disclosure for God, exalted. Know that even though we have proven that this state is different from the affect of the eye and ear, we are hesitant in another position, for it is possible that the occurrence of this state is conditioned by the occurrence of this affect in the eye and ear. The evidence for that: whenever these instruments are impaired, these perceptions are impaired, and whenever the instruments are sound, the perceptions are sound. This suggests that they are conditioned by the soundness of these instruments. And it is also possible to say: the occurrence of this type of perception for the human soul, even if conditioned by the soundness of these instruments, is not impossible to occur in the case of separate entities (al-mufariqat) without these instruments, just as the occurrence of existence for these contingent beings is conditioned by the influence of the influencer, yet existence is inherent to the Necessary Existent by Its essence, without requiring the occurrence of that influencer. So these two possibilities stand. On the first assumption, affirming this attribute for God is impossible. On the second assumption, it is not impossible, so hesitation becomes necessary. However, the premise based on the first and more appropriate [principle] requires affirming it for God, exalted. That is because He, glorified, is the most perfect of existents, so He must be described with every attribute of perfections. And it is known that the state called vision and hearing is an attribute of perfection. For we have explained that someone who knows something and then sees it finds within himself as if he moved from deficiency to perfection, from concealment to clarity. So if this state is one of the attributes of perfection, and its absence is one of the attributes of deficiency, and the Truth, glorified and exalted, is transcendent above all attributes of deficiency and necessarily characterized by all attributes of perfection, then it follows by the primary intellect that we believe Him to be characterized by it, transcendent above the deficiency resulting from its lack.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a discussion (بحث) about whether rational proof establishes that God possesses the special kind of disclosure and manifestation that vision and hearing provide. The author acknowledges that although we have shown that vision and hearing are different from mere physical affect, we must hesitate: these perceptions in humans are conditioned by the soundness of sensory organs. The correlation suggests conditionality. However, it is also possible that such perceptions, though conditioned in humans, could occur for separate entities (such as angels or God) without organs. The analogy is existence: contingent beings require an efficient cause, but the Necessary Existent exists by itself. So two possibilities remain. If the first (conditionality) is true, then affirming these attributes for God is impossible. If the second is true, it is possible, so we must suspend judgment. But then the author introduces a stronger principle: God is the most perfect being and must possess all perfections. Since vision and hearing are perfections (moving from knowledge to seeing is a perfection), and their absence is a deficiency, it follows by primary rational necessity that God possesses them. Thus the argument from perfection overrides the hesitation, and we affirm that God has vision and hearing without organs, transcendent above deficiency.

Key Terms
البحث الثاني
التجلي
الانكشاف
مشروط
الآلات
المفارقات
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because human perception requires organs, God cannot perceive.
Correction: The passage shows that while conditionality is plausible for humans, it does not necessarily apply to God; we must hesitate, not reject outright.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does hesitation arise regarding affirming vision and hearing for Allah?
A) Because the empirical evidence is conclusive that God cannot perceive.
B) Because two possibilities exist: perception may or may not be conditioned by organs.
C) Because the analogy with existence fails.
D) Because the instruments are not relevant.

Key Takeaway
Razi's analysis leads to a necessary hesitation: the evidence for conditionality is strong but not conclusive for God, so we must suspend judgment.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely never paused to consider that your own inability to see without eyes might not be a limitation for Allah. In what areas of your life do you unconsciously project your own limitations onto Him? Will you now make a habit of hesitating before assuming what Allah can or cannot do?
?
Razi's hesitation is an act of intellectual humility—admitting that the evidence is not conclusive. Is there a belief about Allah that you hold with certainty, but which you have never critically examined? Would you have the courage to set aside that certainty and honestly explore the other possibility, as Razi did?
?
If you truly accept that Allah's attributes might operate entirely differently from human attributes, how would that change your longing to see Him in the next life? Does your heart yearn for that vision, or are you content with intellectual assent? What will you do today to cultivate that longing?

The Perfection Argument: Affirming Divine Vision and Hearing
Razi presents the perfection argument to overcome hesitation and affirm divine vision and hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses the principle of divine perfection to resolve the hesitation and affirm that Allah possesses vision and hearing.

Core Idea
Since God is the most perfect being, He must have all perfections, including vision and hearing.

After presenting the hesitation, Razi introduces a stronger argument based on the nature of divine perfection. He argues that because God is the most perfect existent, He must be described with every attribute of perfection. Vision and hearing are clearly perfections, as they involve a movement from knowledge to direct seeing, which is a higher state. Therefore, it is rationally necessary to affirm that God has these attributes, while remaining transcendent above any physical means.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Principle of Perfection
Razi asserts that God is the most perfect being and must possess all attributes of perfection.
This is a foundational axiom in Islamic theology.
2
Recognize Vision and Hearing as Perfections
Moving from knowledge to direct perception is a transition from deficiency to perfection.
Razi previously explained this point.
3
Apply the Principle
Since God is perfect, He must have these perfections, and lacking them would be a deficiency, which is impossible for God.
This overrides the earlier hesitation.
4
Affirm Divine Attributes Transcendently
We affirm that God has vision and hearing, but without physical organs, in a manner befitting His majesty.
The final statement emphasizes transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثاني : في أنه هل دل الدليل على إثبات هذا النوع من التجلي والانكشاف الله تعالى ؟ اعلم . أنا وإن دللنا على أن هذه الحالة مغايرة لتأثر العين والأذن ، إلا أنا متوفقون في مقام آخر ، فإنه من المحتمل أن يكون حصول هذه الحالة مشروطاً بحصول هذا التأثر في العين والأذن . والدليل عليه : أنه كلما اختلت هذه الآلات اختلت هذه الإدراكات ، وكلما كانت الآلات سليمة كانت الإدراكات سليمة ، فهذا يوهم كونها مشروطة بسلامة هذه الآلات ، ويحتمل أيضاً : أن يقال : إن حصول هذا النوع من الإدراكات للنفس البشرية ، وإن كانت مشروطة بسلامة هذه الآلات ، إلا أنه لا يمتنع حصولها في حق المفارقات من غير هذه الآلات، كما أن حصول الوجود لهذه الممكنات مشروط بتأثير المؤثر فيه ، ثم إن الوجود حاصل لواجب الوجود لذاته ، من غير حصول إلى حصول هذا المؤثر . فهذان الاحتمالان قائمان. وعلى التقدير الأول يكون إثبات هذه الصفة الله محالاً . وعلى التقدير الثاني لا يكون محالاً ، فلا جرم وجب التوقف . إلا أن المقدمة المبنية على الأولى والأخلق يقتضي إثباتها الله تعالي . وذلك لأنه سبحانه أكمل الموجودات ، فوجب أن يكون موصوفاً بكل صفات الكمالات ، ومعلوم أن الحالة المسماة بالإبصار والسماع صفة من صفات الكمال . فإنا بينا أن من علم شيئاً ثم رآه ، فإنه يجد في نفسه كأنه انتقل من نقصان إلى كمال ، ومن خفاء إلى جلاء ، فإذا كانت هذه الحالة من صفات الكمال ، وكان عدمها من صفات النقصان ، وكان الحق سبحانه وتعالى منزهاً عن جميع صفات النقص ، واجب الاتصاف بجميع صفات الكمال ، لزم بمقتضى العقل الأول أن نعتقد كونه تعالى موصوفاً به ، منزهاً عن النقص الحاصل بسبب فقدانه .
The second discussion: concerning whether the proof indicates the affirmation of this type of manifestation and disclosure for God, exalted. Know that even though we have proven that this state is different from the affect of the eye and ear, we are hesitant in another position, for it is possible that the occurrence of this state is conditioned by the occurrence of this affect in the eye and ear. However, the premise based on the first and more appropriate [principle] requires affirming it for God, exalted. And it is known that the state called vision and hearing is an attribute of perfection. So if this state is one of the attributes of perfection, and its absence is one of the attributes of deficiency, and the Truth, glorified and exalted, is transcendent above all attributes of deficiency and necessarily characterized by all attributes of perfection, then it follows by the primary intellect that we believe Him to be characterized by it, transcendent above the deficiency resulting from its lack.

Correction: The passage affirms divine perception but emphasizes transcendence: God has these attributes without organs, in a way befitting His majesty.

Checkpoint
What does Razi conclude about divine vision and hearing based on the perfection argument?
A) They are impossible because of conditionality.
B) They are possible but uncertain.
C) They must be affirmed because God is perfect and their absence would be a deficiency.
D) They are metaphorical and not real attributes.

Key Takeaway
Razi's perfection argument conclusively establishes that Allah possesses vision and hearing, transcending physical instruments.

Objection to Divine Perception: The Problem of Change and Dependency
Razi presents two arguments against attributing temporal perception to Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing temporal perception to Allah would imply unacceptable change and dependency.

Core Idea
Temporal perception requires change and dependence—impossible for Allah.

This lesson examines an objection raised against the Ash'ari position that Allah perceives temporal events. The objection argues that if Allah's perception were temporal, His attributes would change and would depend on external objects. We will explore the two arguments presented by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, noting that the discussion is incomplete in our text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation without analysing yet. Notice the two reasons given.
2
Understand the first argument
The first argument says perception would cause a change in God's attribute from not perceiving to perceiving, which is impossible.
Change in God's attributes is established as impossible earlier.
3
Examine the second argument (incomplete)
The second argument argues that perception requires the existence of the perceived object, leading to a dependency chain. The text cuts off, but the implication is that God would be dependent on creation.
The argument is incomplete due to page ending.

The third discussion: concerning the proof that prevents Him, exalted, from being characterized by it. Its exposition: that seeing a thing before its occurrence is impossible, and hearing speech before its occurrence is impossible. If this is established, then you say: they said this prevents Him, exalted, from being characterized by it for two reasons: The first: that if He, exalted, were characterized by this perception, then this attribute would be changing, because He is a seer of the thing at the time of its existence, and He was not a seer of it before its existence. Likewise, He is a hearer of the sound at the time of its occurrence, and He is not a hearer of it before its occurrence. Necessarily, alteration occurs in the attribute of God, which is impossible, due to the proofs we have mentioned that the occurrence of alteration in any of God's attributes is impossible. The second argument: that perception of a thing is conditioned by the existence of the perceived object in itself. So if His essence necessitated the occurrence of this perception, and it is established that the occurrence of this perception is dependent on the existence of the perceived object in itself, then at that time God's essence would not be sufficient to necessitate this attribute; rather, this necessitation would be dependent on the state of something else. So we say: His essence is dependent on that attribute, and that attribute is dependent on something else. And that which is dependent on that which is dependent on something else is dependent on something else. And everything that is dependent on...
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage presents the third discussion, which addresses an objection that prevents God from being described with perception (vision and hearing). The objection is based on two arguments. The first argument: perception is temporal — one sees a thing only when it exists, not before. If God had such perception, His attribute would change from not perceiving to perceiving when the object comes into existence. But change in God's attributes is impossible, as previously established. The second argument (incomplete in our text): perception is conditioned by the existence of the perceived object. If God's essence required this perception, but the perception depends on an external object, then God's essence would not be sufficient; it would depend on another. This leads to a chain of dependency, implying that God is dependent on others, which is impossible. The argument cuts off mid-sentence, but the intended conclusion is that this chain of dependency is impossible, so God cannot be described with such perception. The author will later respond to these objections in subsequent pages not included here.

Correction: Knowledge is timeless, while perception is temporal. This argument shows that temporal perception would entail change and dependency, which are impossible for God.

Rational Proof for God's Sight and Hearing
The author gives a rational proof that God must be seeing and hearing from perfection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the rational principle of perfection leads to affirming that God possesses the attributes of sight and hearing.

Core Idea
God must have all perfections, so He necessarily sees and hears.

This passage presents a key rational argument in Islamic theology: that divine attributes like sight and hearing are not merely derived from revelation but are necessitated by the very concept of divine perfection. By examining this proof, we learn how reason itself can affirm attributes of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through without analysis. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
The passage is a concise syllogism; notice each premise.
2
Identify the major premise
Find where the passage states that God is the most perfect being and must possess all perfections.
This is the foundation of the argument.
3
Understand the minor premise
The passage argues that sight and hearing are perfections, using the human experience of moving from knowledge to direct perception.
This is the key analogy.
4
See the conclusion
Combine the premises: since God has all perfections and sight/hearing are perfections, He must be seeing and hearing.
The conclusion is drawn by the primary intellect.
5
Reflect on the methodology
Consider how this rational proof avoids anthropomorphism by relying on the concept of perfection rather than human-like sensory organs.
This guards against a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أكمل الموجودات ، فوجب أن يكون موصوفاً بكل صفات الكمالات ، ومعلوم أن الحالة المسماة بالإبصار والسماع صفة من صفات الكمال . فإنا بينا أن من علم شيئاً ثم رآه ، فإنه يجد في نفسه كأنه انتقل من نقصان إلى كمال ، ومن خفاء إلى جلاء ، فإذا كانت هذه الحالة من صفات الكمال ، وكان عدمها من صفات النقصان ، وكان الحق سبحانه وتعالى منزهاً عن جميع صفات النقص ، واجب الاتصاف بجميع صفات الكمال ، لزم بمقتضى العقل الأول أن نعتقد كونه تعالى موصوفاً به ، منزهاً عن النقص الحاصل بسبب فقدانه
The most perfect of existents, so it is necessary that He be described with every attribute of perfections, and it is known that the state called vision and hearing is an attribute of perfection. For we have explained that one who knows something then sees it, he finds in himself as if he moved from deficiency to perfection, from obscurity to clarity. So if this state is from the attributes of perfection, and its absence is from the attributes of deficiency, and the Truth (Exalted is He) is free from all attributes of deficiency and necessarily described with all attributes of perfection, then by the dictate of the primary intellect we must believe that He (Exalted) is described with it, free from the deficiency resulting from its absence.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage provides a rational proof for God's attributes of sight and hearing. It begins by stating that God is the most perfect being (أكمل الموجودات). Therefore, He must possess all attributes of perfection. The author then argues that the state called seeing (الإبصار) and hearing (السماع) are among the perfections. He explains by analogy: when a person knows something and then sees it, he feels he has moved from deficiency to perfection, from obscurity to clarity. Thus, these states are perfections. Since God is free from all deficiency and necessarily possesses all perfections, He must be described with sight and hearing. The conclusion is that by the dictate of the primary intellect (بمقتضى العقل الأول), we must believe that God is described with them and free from the deficiency of their absence.

Key Terms
الإبصار
السماع
صفات الكمال
العقل الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this proof relies on anthropomorphism (tashbīh) by attributing physical organs to God.
Correction: No, the proof uses a rational principle of perfection, not physical organs. It argues that the state of directly perceiving (without physical means) is a perfection, so God, being perfect, must have that state.

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof, why must God be described with sight and hearing?
A) Because they are sensory attributes common in creation
B) Because God is alive and thus has senses
C) Because they are attributes of perfection and God possesses all perfections
D) Because scripture explicitly states they are perfections

Key Takeaway
God's sight and hearing are rationally necessary because they are perfections, and God is the most perfect being.

Two Rational Arguments Against God Having Sight and Hearing
Two reasons proving God cannot be described with sight and hearing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main arguments (change and dependency) that show why God cannot be described with sight and hearing as humans experience them.

Core Idea
If God saw or heard as we do, His essence would change and depend on others—both impossible.

In this passage, the author presents a rational investigation into whether God can be described with attributes like sight and hearing. The core issue is that perception presupposes the existence of the perceived object, which would introduce change and dependency into God's essence. These two arguments are foundational in kalam for establishing divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the setup
Read the opening lines noting that the investigation begins by asserting that seeing or hearing something before it exists is impossible. This premise is key to the arguments that follow.
The phrase 'seeing a thing before its existence is impossible' is a rational axiom.
2
Examine the first argument: change
Trace the reasoning: if God had perception, He would see only when the object exists, not before. This change in His state would mean change in His attribute, which is impossible.
The author refers to earlier proofs that no attribute of God can undergo change.
3
Examine the second argument: dependency
Follow the chain: perception depends on the object. If God's essence necessitated perception, it would depend on the object. That makes His essence dependent, leading to contingency—a contradiction with His necessary existence.
The conclusion 'the Necessary in itself would be possible in itself' is a direct contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البحث الثالث : في الدليل الذي يمنع من كونه تعالى موصوفاً به ، وتقريره : أن إبصار الشيء قبل حصوله محال ، وسماع الكلام قبل حصوله محال ، إذا ثبت هذا تقول : إنهم قالوا : إن هذا يمنع من كونه تعالى موصوفاً به لوجهين : الأول : إنه تعالى لو كان موصوفاً بهذا الإدراك لكانت هذه الصفة متغيرة ، لأنه يكون رائياً للشيء حال وجوده ، وما كان رائياً له قبل وجوده . وكذلك يكون سامعاً للصوت حال حصوله ، ولا يكون سامعاً له قبل حصوله ، فيلزم وقوع التغير في صفة الله ، وهو محال ، للدلائل التي ذكرناها في أن وقوع التغير في كل صفات الله محال . الحجة الثانية : وهي إن إدراك الشيء مشروط بحصول المدرك في نفسه ، فلو اقتضت ذاته حصول هذا الإدراك ، وثبت أن حصول هذا الإدراك موقوف على حصول المدرك في نفسه ، فحينئذ لا تكون ذات الله كافية في اقتضاء هذه الصفة ، بل كان هذا الاقتضاء موقوفاً على حال الغير ، فنقول : ذاته موقوفة على تلك الصفة، وتلك الصفة موقوفة على الغير . والموقوف على الموقوف على الغير موقوف على الغير، وكل ما كان موقوفاً على الغير كان ممكناً لذاته ، فالواجب لذاته ممكن لذاته . هذا خلف . وهذا مجموع ما لخصناه من المباحث العقلية في هذا الباب
The third investigation: On the evidence that prevents Him (Exalted) from being described with it. Its formulation: seeing a thing before its existence is impossible, and hearing speech before its existence is impossible. If this is established, you say: they said that this prevents Him from being described with it for two reasons: The first: That if He (Exalted) were described with this perception, then this attribute would be changeable, because He would be seeing the thing at the time of its existence, and He was not seeing it before its existence. Likewise, He would be hearing the sound at the time of its occurrence, and He was not hearing it before its occurrence. This entails the occurrence of change in the attribute of God, which is impossible, due to the proofs we have mentioned that the occurrence of change in all of God's attributes is impossible. The second proof: That perception of a thing is conditional upon the existence of the perceived thing itself. So if His essence necessitated the occurrence of this perception, and it is established that the occurrence of this perception is dependent upon the existence of the perceived thing itself, then God's essence would not be sufficient to necessitate this attribute; rather this necessitation would be dependent upon the state of another. So we say: His essence is dependent upon that attribute, and that attribute is dependent upon another. That which is dependent upon that which is dependent upon another is dependent upon another, and everything that is dependent upon another is possible in itself. Therefore, the Necessary in itself would be possible in itself. This is a contradiction. This is the sum of what we have summarized from the rational investigations in this chapter.
Source pages p. 193

Commentary
This passage is the third investigation (البحث الثالث) which presents evidence that prevents God from being described with the attributes of sight and hearing. The author formulates the evidence: seeing or hearing something before it exists is impossible. Then two reasons are given. First, if God had such perception, it would be changeable: He would be seeing when the thing exists, but not before. Change in God's attribute is impossible. Second, perception depends on the existence of the perceived object. If God's essence necessitated this perception, but that perception depends on something else (the object), then God's essence would not be sufficient; it would depend on another. This leads to a chain of dependencies, and anything dependent on another is contingent (ممكن), so the Necessary Being (الواجب لذاته) would be contingent, a contradiction. Thus, God cannot be described with these attributes in a way that implies change or dependency.

Key Terms
الإدراك
التغير
الموقوف
الواجب لذاته
الممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this argument denies God's attributes entirely, whereas it only shows the impossibility of a particular conception.
Correction: The argument does not deny that God sees or hears in a way befitting His majesty; it only shows that human-like perception (which involves change and dependency) cannot be attributed to God.

Checkpoint
According to the second reason, why does attributing sight and hearing to God lead to a contradiction?
A) Because it implies change
B) Because it implies dependency and contingency
C) Because it implies ignorance
D) Because it implies multiplicity

Key Takeaway
God cannot be described with sight and hearing because they would imply change and dependency, contradicting His necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever picture God as having human-like senses, such as eyes that see or ears that hear? How does this lesson force you to tear down that mental image? What will you do today to start thinking of God's knowledge as timeless, not dependent on events?

When you read that change and dependence are impossible for God, does your heart feel the weight of His absolute perfection, or do you take it as a dry theological fact? Commit to a specific moment today to sit in silence and let the reality of God's independence sink into your soul.
?
The argument shows that if God needed anything outside Himself, He would not be God. Have you been living as if you need something besides God to be whole? Identify one attachment—a person, a possession, a goal—that you have elevated to a necessity, and decide to dethrone it today.

The Ash'ari Argument for Hearing and Sight
Al-Razi presents the Ash'ari argument and introduces its weak nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the Ash'ari argument for God's hearing and sight and identify its key premises.

Core Idea
God's life necessarily entails hearing and sight to avoid deficiency.

Before diving into Al-Razi's refutation, we must first understand the argument he is critiquing. The Ash'aris claim that God's attribute of life logically requires the attributes of hearing and sight. This passage presents their reasoning, which Al-Razi considers weak. Pay close attention to the logical steps they use.

Guided Path
1
Read the Ash'ari argument
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the translation to understand the content.
Notice that the argument is presented as a conditional chain.
2
Identify the key premises
Break down the argument into its premises: (1) God is living, (2) every living being can potentially hear and see, (3) for any attribute, one must have it or its opposite, (4) the opposites are deficiencies, (5) deficiency is impossible for God.
The conclusion is that God must have hearing and sight.
3
Map the logical structure
Write the argument in a formal logical format: If P then Q, etc. See how the premises lead to the conclusion.
4
Reflect on Al-Razi's characterization
Al-Razi calls this argument 'weak' before presenting it. Why might he say that? Consider what assumptions the argument makes about life and its implications.
This preview prepares you for the objections in the next lessons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللمتكلمين وجهين ضعيفين في هذا الباب نذكرهما وننبه على ضعفها : فالطريق الأول : وهو الذي عول عليه الأشعرية . قالوا : إنه تعالى حي ، وكل من كان حياً فإنه يصح عليه السمع والبصر . وكل من صنع عليه وصف ، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بتلك الصفة ، وإن لم يكن موصوفاً بها ، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بضدها ، وضد السمع الصمم ، وضد البصر العمى وذلك نقص . والنقص على الله محال ، فوجب كونه موصوفاً بالسمع والبصر . هذا تقدير كلام القوم .
ولقائل أن يقول : السؤال عليه من وجوه :
السؤال الأول : ما الذي تريدون بقولكم : إنه تعالى حي ؟ إن عنيتم به : أنه تعالى لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر . فهذا مسلّم . لكن لم قلتم : إن كل من لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر ، فإنه لا يمتنع أن يسمع ويبصر ؟ وهل النزاع وقع إلا فيه ؟ وإن عنيتم بكونه حياً معنى سوى ما قلنا إنه لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر ، فلا بد من تفسيره ، فإنه محمول التصور ، فضلاً عن كونه معلوم التصديق .
السؤال الثاني : إن سلمنا أن كونه حياً صفة زائدة على سبب ذلك الامتناع فلم قلتم : إنه لما حصلت الحياة ، وجب أن يحصل هناك صحة أن يسمع ويبصر ؟ وبيانه من وجوه :
الوجه الأول : أن حياته تعالى مخالفة لحياة الواحد منا ، ولا يلزم من كون حياة الواحد منا مؤثرة في صحة السمع والبصر أن تكون حياته كذلك لما ثبت أن المختلفات في الماهية ، لا يجب استواؤها في اللوازم والأحكام . ألا ترى أن حياة الواحد منا تقتضي صحة الشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة والجهل والظن ، مع أن حياة الله تعالى لا تصحح هذه الصفات ، وما ذاك إلا لأن حياة الله تعالى مخالفة لحياتنا ، فلم يلزم من كون حياتنا مقيدة بصحة هذه الصفات كون حياته كذلك . وإذا ثبت هذا التفاوت في تلك الصورة ، فلم يجوز مثله ههنا ؟
الوجه الثاني : إن بتقدير حصول المماثلة بين الحياتين ، إلا أنا نقول : كما يعتبر في حصول الصحة قيام المصحح ، فكذلك يعتبر فيها كون الذات قابلة . فهب أن الحياة المصححة حاصلة هناك ، إلا أنه لا يمتنع أن يقال : تلك الذات المخصوصة بخصوصها لسائر الذوات ، غير قابلة لهذه الصفة ، ولأجل كون تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصحة انتفت هذه الصحة . والدليل عليه : أن الوجود يصحح جميع الصفات ، ثم إن الوجود حاصل في حق الله تعالى ، مع أن أكثر الصفات ممتنعة في حق الله تعالى ، وما ذاك إلا لأجل أن تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصفات . فكذا ههنا .
والوجه الثالث : هب أن الحياة المصححة لهاتين الصفتين ، والذات المخصوصة قابلة لها من حيث هي هي، إلا أنه لا يمتنع أن يكون حصول هذه الصحة مشروطاً بشرط مخصوص ، ويكون ذلك الشرط ممتنع الحصول في حق الله تعالى ، ففات ذلك الإمكان لفوات الشرط، لا لقوات المصحح . ولقائل أن يقول: وإذا كانت هذه الاحتمالات قائمة فقد سقط هذا الدليل.
The mutakallimun have two weak ways in this chapter, which we will mention and point out their weakness. The first way: that upon which the Ash'aris relied. They said: He, exalted, is living (ḥayy), and anyone who is living, it is valid for him to hear and see. And anyone to whom a description is attributed, it is necessary that he be described by that attribute, and if he is not described by it, then it is necessary that he be described by its opposite, and the opposite of hearing is deafness, and the opposite of sight is blindness, and that is deficiency. And deficiency is impossible for God, so it is necessary that He be described by hearing and sight. This is the estimation of their statement.
And one may say: The objection against it is from several aspects:
The first objection: What do you mean by your saying: He, exalted, is living? If you mean by it that He, exalted, is not impossible to know and have power, then this is conceded. But why do you say: Everyone who is not impossible to know and have power, it is not impossible for him to hear and see? Is the dispute about anything other than that? And if you mean by His being living a meaning other than what we said—that He is not impossible to know and have power—then it must be explained, for it is problematic in conception, let alone being known for assent.
The second objection: If we concede that His being living is an additional attribute to the cause of that impossibility, why do you say: When life is obtained, it becomes necessary that there obtain a possibility (ṣiḥḥa) that He hear and see? Its explanation is from several aspects:
The first aspect: His life, exalted, is different from the life of one of us, and it does not follow from the life of one of us being effective in the possibility of hearing and sight that His life is likewise, because it is established that things differing in essence are not necessarily equal in their concomitants and rulings. Do you not see that the life of one of us necessitates the possibility of desire, aversion, pain, pleasure, ignorance, and conjecture, while the life of God, exalted, does not validate these attributes? And that is only because the life of God, exalted, is different from our life. So it does not follow from our life being restricted to the possibility of these attributes that His life is likewise. And if this disparity is established in that case, why is its like not allowed here?
The second aspect: Even assuming similarity between the two lives, we say: Just as the presence of the enabler (muṣaḥḥiḥ) is considered in obtaining the possibility, so also is the subject being receptive considered. Grant that the enabling life is present there, but it is not impossible to say: That particular subject, by its particularity with respect to other subjects, is not receptive to this attribute, and because that particular subject is not receptive to this possibility, this possibility is eliminated. The proof of that is that existence validates all attributes, yet existence is obtained with respect to God, exalted, while most attributes are impossible with respect to God, exalted—and that is only because that particular subject is not receptive to these attributes. So likewise here.
The third aspect: Grant that the enabling life for these two attributes is present, and the particular subject is receptive to them in itself, but it is not impossible that obtaining this possibility be conditioned by a particular condition, and that condition is impossible of obtaining with respect to God, exalted. So that possibility is lost due to the loss of the condition, not due to the absence of the enabler. And one may say: When these probabilities stand, this proof has fallen.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of the first weak argument of the mutakallimun (theologians) regarding the proof of God's attributes of hearing and sight. Al-Razi begins by stating that the theologians have two weak arguments, and he will mention them and point out their weakness. He then presents the first argument, which is relied upon by the Ash'aris. The Ash'ari argument runs as follows: God is living. Anyone who is living can potentially hear and see. For any attribute, a being must either have it or its opposite. The opposite of hearing is deafness, and of sight is blindness, both of which are deficiencies. Deficiency is impossible for God. Therefore, God must be described with hearing and sight.

Al-Razi then introduces a refutation via three objections, but only the first two are fully presented here. The first objection challenges the meaning of 'living'. If 'living' means that God is not incapable of knowledge and power, then that is conceded, but it does not necessarily imply the possibility of hearing and sight. The dispute is precisely about whether life entails these specific attributes. If 'living' means something else, it must be defined, which is problematic.

The second objection proceeds in three sub-arguments (aspects) to show that even if life is an additional attribute, it does not necessitate hearing and sight. The first sub-argument points out that God's life is different in essence from human life. Human life entails possibilities like desire, pain, etc., which are not applicable to God. Therefore, the analogy from human life does not hold. The second sub-argument: even if the lives were similar, the subject (God's essence) may not be receptive to hearing and sight. The proof is that existence itself validates all attributes, but God's essence is not receptive to many attributes, so receptivity is not automatic. The third sub-argument: even if the life is enabling and the subject is receptive, there could be an additional missing condition that prevents the possibility from actualizing. Since these possibilities remain, the Ash'ari proof is invalidated. Al-Razi concludes that with these probabilities standing, the argument collapses.

Key Terms
حي (living)
سميع (hearing)
بصير (seeing)
ضد (opposite)
نقص (deficiency)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Ash'ari argument is a valid and sound proof for God's hearing and sight.
Correction: Al-Razi considers it weak and proceeds to refute it by showing that the meaning of 'living' is ambiguous and that the analogy from human life fails.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari argument presented in this passage, what is the reason God must have hearing and sight?
A) Because God is powerful and wise.
B) Because God is living, and the opposite of hearing/sight is deficiency, which is impossible for God.
C) Because the Quran explicitly states that God hears and sees.
D) Because all living beings have hearing and sight.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument attempts to deduce God's hearing and sight from His life and the impossibility of deficiency, but is vulnerable to critique.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi begins this lesson by calling the Ash'ari argument 'weak' before even presenting it. When was the last time you honestly admitted that a cherished belief or argument you held was weak? What stopped you from being that honest with yourself?
?
The Ash'aris argued that if God is not described by hearing and sight, He must have their opposites—deafness and blindness—which are deficiencies. In your own life, when you fail to act in a way that reflects God's attributes, do you assume you must be the opposite? Or do you recognize that deficiency is impossible for God but possible for you, and that you need to change?
?
This lesson shows a theologian presenting an argument and then criticizing it. Do you surround yourself with people who only agree with you, or do you invite critique? What would you need to set aside in order to truly welcome someone pointing out weakness in your beliefs?

First Objection: What Does 'Living' Mean?
Al-Razi's first objection questioning the definition of 'living'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the ambiguity in the definition of 'living' undermines the Ash'ari argument for hearing and sight.

Core Idea
If 'living' only means knowledge and power, it does not imply hearing and sight.

Al-Razi's first objection targets the very first premise of the Ash'ari argument: 'God is living.' He challenges the mutakallimun to clarify what they mean by 'living.' Are they using the term in a sense that already includes hearing and sight, or in a more limited sense? This question exposes a key ambiguity.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation. Note how Al-Razi presents two alternative interpretations of 'living'.
2
Analyze the two meanings
First meaning: 'living' = 'not impossible to know and have power.' Second meaning: something else. Under the first meaning, the argument is conceded but the conclusion does not follow.
The second meaning is undefined and thus problematic.
3
Understand the logical move
Al-Razi is forcing the Ash'ari to define their terms. If 'living' is defined narrowly, it doesn't entail hearing/sight. If defined broadly, it's unclear and needs explanation.
This is a classic dialectical move.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this objection, even alone, challenges the entire argument. If the key term is ambiguous, the proof fails.

وللمتكلمين وجهين ضعيفين في هذا الباب نذكرهما وننبه على ضعفها : فالطريق الأول : وهو الذي عول عليه الأشعرية . قالوا : إنه تعالى حي ، وكل من كان حياً فإنه يصح عليه السمع والبصر . وكل من صنع عليه وصف ، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بتلك الصفة ، وإن لم يكن موصوفاً بها ، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بضدها ، وضد السمع الصمم ، وضد البصر العمى وذلك نقص . والنقص على الله محال ، فوجب كونه موصوفاً بالسمع والبصر . هذا تقدير كلام القوم .
ولقائل أن يقول : السؤال عليه من وجوه :
السؤال الأول : ما الذي تريدون بقولكم : إنه تعالى حي ؟ إن عنيتم به : أنه تعالى لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر . فهذا مسلّم . لكن لم قلتم : إن كل من لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر ، فإنه لا يمتنع أن يسمع ويبصر ؟ وهل النزاع وقع إلا فيه ؟ وإن عنيتم بكونه حياً معنى سوى ما قلنا إنه لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر ، فلا بد من تفسيره ، فإنه محمول التصور ، فضلاً عن كونه معلوم التصديق .

الوجه الثاني : إن بتقدير حصول المماثلة بين الحياتين ، إلا أنا نقول : كما يعتبر في حصول الصحة قيام المصحح ، فكذلك يعتبر فيها كون الذات قابلة . فهب أن الحياة المصححة حاصلة هناك ، إلا أنه لا يمتنع أن يقال : تلك الذات المخصوصة بخصوصها لسائر الذوات ، غير قابلة لهذه الصفة ، ولأجل كون تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصحة انتفت هذه الصحة . والدليل عليه : أن الوجود يصحح جميع الصفات ، ثم إن الوجود حاصل في حق الله تعالى ، مع أن أكثر الصفات ممتنعة في حق الله تعالى ، وما ذاك إلا لأجل أن تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصفات . فكذا ههنا .

The mutakallimun have two weak ways in this chapter, which we will mention and point out their weakness. The first way: that upon which the Ash'aris relied. They said: He, exalted, is living (ḥayy), and anyone who is living, it is valid for him to hear and see. And anyone to whom a description is attributed, it is necessary that he be described by that attribute, and if he is not described by it, then it is necessary that he be described by its opposite, and the opposite of hearing is deafness, and the opposite of sight is blindness, and that is deficiency. And deficiency is impossible for God, so it is necessary that He be described by hearing and sight. This is the estimation of their statement.
And one may say: The objection against it is from several aspects:
The first objection: What do you mean by your saying: He, exalted, is living? If you mean by it that He, exalted, is not impossible to know and have power, then this is conceded. But why do you say: Everyone who is not impossible to know and have power, it is not impossible for him to hear and see? Is the dispute about anything other than that? And if you mean by His being living a meaning other than what we said—that He is not impossible to know and have power—then it must be explained, for it is problematic in conception, let alone being known for assent.
The second objection: If we concede that His being living is an additional attribute to the cause of that impossibility, why do you say: When life is obtained, it becomes necessary that there obtain a possibility (ṣiḥḥa) that He hear and see? Its explanation is from several aspects:
The first aspect: His life, exalted, is different from the life of one of us, and it does not follow from the life of one of us being effective in the possibility of hearing and sight that His life is likewise, because it is established that things differing in essence are not necessarily equal in their concomitants and rulings. Do you not see that the life of one of us necessitates the possibility of desire, aversion, pain, pleasure, ignorance, and conjecture, while the life of God, exalted, does not validate these attributes? And that is only because the life of God, exalted, is different from our life. So it does not follow from our life being restricted to the possibility of these attributes that His life is likewise. And if this disparity is established in that case, why is its like not allowed here?
The second aspect: Even assuming similarity between the two lives, we say: Just as the presence of the enabler (muṣaḥḥiḥ) is considered in obtaining the possibility, so also is the subject being receptive considered. The proof of that is that existence validates all attributes, yet existence is obtained with respect to God, exalted, while most attributes are impossible with respect to God, exalted—and that is only because that particular subject is not receptive to these attributes. So likewise here.
The third aspect: Grant that the enabling life for these two attributes is present, and the particular subject is receptive to them in itself, but it is not impossible that obtaining this possibility be conditioned by a particular condition, and that condition is impossible of obtaining with respect to God, exalted. So that possibility is lost due to the loss of the condition, not due to the absence of the enabler. And one may say: When these probabilities stand, this proof has fallen.

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of the first weak argument of the mutakallimun (theologians) regarding the proof of God's attributes of hearing and sight. Al-Razi begins by stating that the theologians have two weak arguments, and he will mention them and point out their weakness. He then presents the first argument, which is relied upon by the Ash'aris. The Ash'ari argument runs as follows: God is living. Anyone who is living can potentially hear and see. For any attribute, a being must either have it or its opposite. The opposite of hearing is deafness, and of sight is blindness, both of which are deficiencies. Deficiency is impossible for God. Therefore, God must be described with hearing and sight.

Al-Razi then introduces a refutation via three objections, but only the first two are fully presented here. The first objection challenges the meaning of 'living'. If 'living' means that God is not incapable of knowledge and power, then that is conceded, but it does not necessarily imply the possibility of hearing and sight. The dispute is precisely about whether life entails these specific attributes. If 'living' means something else, it must be defined, which is problematic.

The second objection proceeds in three sub-arguments (aspects) to show that even if life is an additional attribute, it does not necessitate hearing and sight. Therefore, the analogy from human life does not hold. The second sub-argument: even if the lives were similar, the subject (God's essence) may not be receptive to hearing and sight. The proof is that existence itself validates all attributes, but God's essence is not receptive to many attributes, so receptivity is not automatic. The third sub-argument: even if the life is enabling and the subject is receptive, there could be an additional missing condition that prevents the possibility from actualizing. Since these possibilities remain, the Ash'ari proof is invalidated. Al-Razi concludes that with these probabilities standing, the argument collapses.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the term 'living' is unambiguous and automatically entails all other attributes.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that 'living' can be interpreted in at least two ways, and the argument fails if the term is not properly defined.

Checkpoint
According to Al-Razi's first objection, if 'living' means 'not impossible to know and have power', what is the problem?
A) That meaning is false for God.
B) That meaning does not prove that God can hear and see; it only proves knowledge and power.
C) That meaning leads to a contradiction.
D) That meaning is not accepted by the Ash'aris.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari argument fails if 'living' is only defined as having knowledge and power, because that does not entail hearing and sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is living', do you truly understand what that means, or are you just repeating a word? Will you commit to seeking deeper knowledge of God's attributes so that your belief is not based on assumption?
?
Al-Razi exposes the danger of using a term without a shared, precise meaning. In your own life, where might you be assuming clear understanding where ambiguity actually exists—especially regarding Allah's names? How will you address that today?
?
This objection forces you to examine whether you have ever accepted a theological argument without scrutinizing its foundational terms. Are you willing to challenge your own beliefs and ensure they are built on solid definitions, even if it feels uncomfortable?

Second Objection: First Aspect – Divine Life vs. Human Life
Al-Razi's first sub-argument of the second objection: the dissimilarity of divine life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essential difference between God's life and human life invalidates the analogy used in the Ash'ari argument.

Core Idea
Divine life is unlike human life, so what follows from human life does not follow from divine life.

Al-Razi's second objection begins by attacking the assumption that life implies the possibility of hearing and sight. Even if we grant that life is an additional attribute, he argues, God's life is different in essence from human life. The first sub-argument demonstrates that since they differ, the effects of human life—such as enabling hearing and sight—cannot be transferred to God without proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the first aspect of the second objection
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the example Al-Razi gives: human life enables desire, pain, etc., which are not possible for God.
2
Identify the key principle
Things that differ in essence do not have the same concomitants and rulings.
This is a logical principle used to block analogies.
3
See the application
Human life enables many things (desire, pain, etc.) that divine life does not. Therefore, human life enabling hearing/sight does not imply divine life enables them.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The Ash'aris were drawing an analogy from human life to divine life. Al-Razi shows this analogy fails because the natures are different.

Source Text
