السؤال الثاني : إن سلمنا أن كونه حياً صفة زائدة على سبب ذلك الامتناع فلم قلتم : إنه لما حصلت الحياة ، وجب أن يحصل هناك صحة أن يسمع ويبصر ؟ وبيانه من وجوه :
الوجه الأول : أن حياته تعالى مخالفة لحياة الواحد منا ، ولا يلزم من كون حياة الواحد منا مؤثرة في صحة السمع والبصر أن تكون حياته كذلك لما ثبت أن المختلفات في الماهية ، لا يجب استواؤها في اللوازم والأحكام . ألا ترى أن حياة الواحد منا تقتضي صحة الشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة والجهل والظن ، مع أن حياة الله تعالى لا تصحح هذه الصفات ، وما ذاك إلا لأن حياة الله تعالى مخالفة لحياتنا ، فلم يلزم من كون حياتنا مقيدة بصحة هذه الصفات كون حياته كذلك . وإذا ثبت هذا التفاوت في تلك الصورة ، فلم يجوز مثله ههنا ؟
الوجه الثاني : إن بتقدير حصول المماثلة بين الحياتين ، إلا أنا نقول : كما يعتبر في حصول الصحة قيام المصحح ، فكذلك يعتبر فيها كون الذات قابلة . فهب أن الحياة المصححة حاصلة هناك ، إلا أنه لا يمتنع أن يقال : تلك الذات المخصوصة بخصوصها لسائر الذوات ، غير قابلة لهذه الصفة ، ولأجل كون تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصحة انتفت هذه الصحة . والدليل عليه : أن الوجود يصحح جميع الصفات ، ثم إن الوجود حاصل في حق الله تعالى ، مع أن أكثر الصفات ممتنعة في حق الله تعالى ، وما ذاك إلا لأجل أن تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصفات . فكذا ههنا .
والوجه الثالث : هب أن الحياة المصححة لهاتين الصفتين ، والذات المخصوصة قابلة لها من حيث هي هي، إلا أنه لا يمتنع أن يكون حصول هذه الصحة مشروطاً بشرط مخصوص ، ويكون ذلك الشرط ممتنع الحصول في حق الله تعالى ، ففات ذلك الإمكان لفوات الشرط، لا لقوات المصحح . ولقائل أن يقول: وإذا كانت هذه الاحتمالات قائمة فقد سقط هذا الدليل.
The mutakallimun have two weak ways in this chapter, which we will mention and point out their weakness. The first way: that upon which the Ash'aris relied. They said: He, exalted, is living (ḥayy), and anyone who is living, it is valid for him to hear and see. And anyone to whom a description is attributed, it is necessary that he be described by that attribute, and if he is not described by it, then it is necessary that he be described by its opposite, and the opposite of hearing is deafness, and the opposite of sight is blindness, and that is deficiency. And deficiency is impossible for God, so it is necessary that He be described by hearing and sight. This is the estimation of their statement.
And one may say: The objection against it is from several aspects:
The first objection: What do you mean by your saying: He, exalted, is living? If you mean by it that He, exalted, is not impossible to know and have power, then this is conceded. But why do you say: Everyone who is not impossible to know and have power, it is not impossible for him to hear and see? Is the dispute about anything other than that? And if you mean by His being living a meaning other than what we said—that He is not impossible to know and have power—then it must be explained, for it is problematic in conception, let alone being known for assent.
The second objection: If we concede that His being living is an additional attribute to the cause of that impossibility, why do you say: When life is obtained, it becomes necessary that there obtain a possibility (ṣiḥḥa) that He hear and see? Its explanation is from several aspects:
The first aspect: His life, exalted, is different from the life of one of us, and it does not follow from the life of one of us being effective in the possibility of hearing and sight that His life is likewise, because it is established that things differing in essence are not necessarily equal in their concomitants and rulings. Do you not see that the life of one of us necessitates the possibility of desire, aversion, pain, pleasure, ignorance, and conjecture, while the life of God, exalted, does not validate these attributes? And that is only because the life of God, exalted, is different from our life. So it does not follow from our life being restricted to the possibility of these attributes that His life is likewise. And if this disparity is established in that case, why is its like not allowed here?
The second aspect: Even assuming similarity between the two lives, we say: Just as the presence of the enabler (muṣaḥḥiḥ) is considered in obtaining the possibility, so also is the subject being receptive considered. Grant that the enabling life is present there, but it is not impossible to say: That particular subject, by its particularity with respect to other subjects, is not receptive to this attribute, and because that particular subject is not receptive to this possibility, this possibility is eliminated. The proof of that is that existence validates all attributes, yet existence is obtained with respect to God, exalted, while most attributes are impossible with respect to God, exalted—and that is only because that particular subject is not receptive to these attributes. So likewise here.
The third aspect: Grant that the enabling life for these two attributes is present, and the particular subject is receptive to them in itself, but it is not impossible that obtaining this possibility be conditioned by a particular condition, and that condition is impossible of obtaining with respect to God, exalted. So that possibility is lost due to the loss of the condition, not due to the absence of the enabler. And one may say: When these probabilities stand, this proof has fallen.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of the first weak argument of the mutakallimun (theologians) regarding the proof of God's attributes of hearing and sight. Al-Razi begins by stating that the theologians have two weak arguments, and he will mention them and point out their weakness. He then presents the first argument, which is relied upon by the Ash'aris. The Ash'ari argument runs as follows: God is living. Anyone who is living can potentially hear and see. For any attribute, a being must either have it or its opposite. The opposite of hearing is deafness, and of sight is blindness, both of which are deficiencies. Deficiency is impossible for God. Therefore, God must be described with hearing and sight.

Al-Razi then introduces a refutation via three objections, but only the first two are fully presented here. The first objection challenges the meaning of 'living'. If 'living' means that God is not incapable of knowledge and power, then that is conceded, but it does not necessarily imply the possibility of hearing and sight. The dispute is precisely about whether life entails these specific attributes. If 'living' means something else, it must be defined, which is problematic.

The second objection proceeds in three sub-arguments (aspects) to show that even if life is an additional attribute, it does not necessitate hearing and sight. The first sub-argument points out that God's life is different in essence from human life. Human life entails possibilities like desire, pain, etc., which are not applicable to God. Therefore, the analogy from human life does not hold. The second sub-argument: even if the lives were similar, the subject (God's essence) may not be receptive to hearing and sight. The proof is that existence itself validates all attributes, but God's essence is not receptive to many attributes, so receptivity is not automatic. The third sub-argument: even if the life is enabling and the subject is receptive, there could be an additional missing condition that prevents the possibility from actualizing. Since these possibilities remain, the Ash'ari proof is invalidated. Al-Razi concludes that with these probabilities standing, the argument collapses.

Key Terms
حياة (life)
صحة (possibility)
مخالفة (different)
ماهية (essence)
لوازم (concomitants)
أحكام (rulings)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if human life enables hearing/sight, then divine life must also enable them because life is the same.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that life is not the same in essence; therefore, what is true for human life need not be true for divine life.

Checkpoint
What logical principle does Al-Razi use in the first aspect of the second objection?
A) Things that are the same in essence have the same concomitants.
B) Things that differ in essence are not necessarily equal in their concomitants and rulings.
C) Life always entails the possibility of hearing and sight.
D) God's life is identical to human life.

Key Takeaway
Since God's life is essentially different from human life, the effect of enabling hearing and sight does not transfer.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is alive,' do you realize you are uttering a word that is not the same as your own life? How does this affect your awe of Him?
?
If God's life is so different, how should that change your confidence in the way you think about His attributes? Will you adopt a humble hesitation before comparing Him to what you know?
?
This argument shows that our human reasoning has limits. Are you willing to accept that some of God's attributes are beyond your comparison, even if it means you cannot fully comprehend them? Will you surrender your intellect to the mystery of His transcendence?

Divine Attributes

Second Objection: Second Aspect – Receptivity of the Subject
Al-Razi's second sub-argument: primacy of subject receptivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if life were similar, the subject's receptivity is not guaranteed, as shown by existence itself.

Core Idea
The subject's receptivity is as important as the enabling cause; God's essence may not be receptive to hearing and sight.

Al-Razi now presents a second sub-argument within the second objection. He grants, for the sake of argument, that divine life is similar to human life. Yet he points out that the possibility of an attribute depends not only on the enabler (muṣaḥḥiḥ) but also on the subject's receptivity (qābiliyya). Using existence itself as an analogy, he shows that God's essence is not receptive to many attributes despite existence being present.

Guided Path
1
Read the second aspect carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the counterexample Al-Razi gives: existence validates all attributes, yet God's essence is not receptive to most attributes.
2
Understand the two conditions
For a possibility to obtain, you need both: the enabler (e.g., life) and the subject's receptivity. Al-Razi argues that even if life is present, receptivity may be absent.
3
Analyze the proof from existence
Existence is a universal enabler, but many attributes are impossible for God. This shows that the subject's specific nature limits receptivity.
4
Apply to hearing and sight
Similarly, even with divine life, God's essence may simply not be receptive to hearing and sight, so they are not necessarily possible.

وللمتكلمين وجهين ضعيفين في هذا الباب نذكرهما وننبه على ضعفها : فالطريق الأول : وهو الذي عول عليه الأشعرية . قالوا : إنه تعالى حي ، وكل من كان حياً فإنه يصح عليه السمع والبصر . وكل من صنع عليه وصف ، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بتلك الصفة ، وإن لم يكن موصوفاً بها ، فإنه يجب أن يكون موصوفاً بضدها ، وضد السمع الصمم ، وضد البصر العمى وذلك نقص . والنقص على الله محال ، فوجب كونه موصوفاً بالسمع والبصر . هذا تقدير كلام القوم .
ولقائل أن يقول : السؤال عليه من وجوه :
السؤال الأول : ما الذي تريدون بقولكم : إنه تعالى حي ؟ إن عنيتم به : أنه تعالى لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر . فهذا مسلّم . لكن لم قلتم : إن كل من لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر ، فإنه لا يمتنع أن يسمع ويبصر ؟ وهل النزاع وقع إلا فيه ؟ وإن عنيتم بكونه حياً معنى سوى ما قلنا إنه لا يمتنع أن يعلم ويقدر ، فلا بد من تفسيره ، فإنه محمول التصور ، فضلاً عن كونه معلوم التصديق .

الوجه الثاني : إن بتقدير حصول المماثلة بين الحياتين ، إلا أنا نقول : كما يعتبر في حصول الصحة قيام المصحح ، فكذلك يعتبر فيها كون الذات قابلة . فهب أن الحياة المصححة حاصلة هناك ، إلا أنه لا يمتنع أن يقال : تلك الذات المخصوصة بخصوصها لسائر الذوات ، غير قابلة لهذه الصفة ، ولأجل كون تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصحة انتفت هذه الصحة . والدليل عليه : أن الوجود يصحح جميع الصفات ، ثم إن الوجود حاصل في حق الله تعالى ، مع أن أكثر الصفات ممتنعة في حق الله تعالى ، وما ذاك إلا لأجل أن تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصفات . فكذا ههنا .

They said: He, exalted, is living (ḥayy), and anyone who is living, it is valid for him to hear and see. And anyone to whom a description is attributed, it is necessary that he be described by that attribute, and if he is not described by it, then it is necessary that he be described by its opposite, and the opposite of hearing is deafness, and the opposite of sight is blindness, and that is deficiency. And deficiency is impossible for God, so it is necessary that He be described by hearing and sight. This is the estimation of their statement.
And one may say: The objection against it is from several aspects:
The first objection: What do you mean by your saying: He, exalted, is living? If you mean by it that He, exalted, is not impossible to know and have power, then this is conceded. But why do you say: Everyone who is not impossible to know and have power, it is not impossible for him to hear and see? Is the dispute about anything other than that? And if you mean by His being living a meaning other than what we said—that He is not impossible to know and have power—then it must be explained, for it is problematic in conception, let alone being known for assent.
The second objection: If we concede that His being living is an additional attribute to the cause of that impossibility, why do you say: When life is obtained, it becomes necessary that there obtain a possibility (ṣiḥḥa) that He hear and see? Its explanation is from several aspects:
The first aspect: His life, exalted, is different from the life of one of us, and it does not follow from the life of one of us being effective in the possibility of hearing and sight that His life is likewise, because it is established that things differing in essence are not necessarily equal in their concomitants and rulings. Do you not see that the life of one of us necessitates the possibility of desire, aversion, pain, pleasure, ignorance, and conjecture, while the life of God, exalted, does not validate these attributes? And that is only because the life of God, exalted, is different from our life. So it does not follow from our life being restricted to the possibility of these attributes that His life is likewise. And if this disparity is established in that case, why is its like not allowed here?

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of the first weak argument of the mutakallimun (theologians) regarding the proof of God's attributes of hearing and sight. For any attribute, a being must either have it or its opposite. The opposite of hearing is deafness, and of sight is blindness, both of which are deficiencies. Deficiency is impossible for God. Therefore, God must be described with hearing and sight.

Al-Razi then introduces a refutation via three objections, but only the first two are fully presented here. The first objection challenges the meaning of 'living'. If 'living' means that God is not incapable of knowledge and power, then that is conceded, but it does not necessarily imply the possibility of hearing and sight. The dispute is precisely about whether life entails these specific attributes. If 'living' means something else, it must be defined, which is problematic.

The second objection proceeds in three sub-arguments (aspects) to show that even if life is an additional attribute, it does not necessitate hearing and sight. Since these possibilities remain, the Ash'ari proof is invalidated. Al-Razi concludes that with these probabilities standing, the argument collapses.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if an enabling cause is present, the attribute must be possible.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the subject's receptivity is also necessary, and God's essence may not be receptive.

Checkpoint
What example does Al-Razi use to show that an enabler does not guarantee possibility without subject receptivity?
A) Human life enables desire, but not for God.
B) Existence enables all attributes, but God's essence is not receptive to many.
C) The opposite of hearing is deafness, which is a deficiency.
D) Life is a single attribute that has multiple effects.

Key Takeaway
Even with a similar life, God's essence may not be receptive to hearing and sight, just as it is not receptive to many other attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a virtue you desire but find difficult to attain—like patience or humility. Do you assume the obstacle is a lack of 'enabler' (time, opportunity, divine grace) or could it be that your own heart is not yet receptive? What specific steps can you take today to increase your receptivity to that virtue?
?
The lesson teaches that even existence itself does not guarantee all attributes are possible. How does this challenge your assumption that simply being a Muslim or being alive automatically makes certain spiritual qualities (e.g., khushūʿ in prayer, gratitude) easy? What does it mean for you to actively work on being receptive, rather than passively expecting attributes to manifest?

Second Objection: Third Aspect – The Missing Condition
Al-Razi's third sub-argument: possibility of a missing condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if life is enabling and the subject is receptive, a missing condition can prevent the possibility of an attribute.

Core Idea
Possibility may depend on a condition that is absent in God, even if the enabler and receptivity are present.

In the third and final sub-argument of the second objection, Al-Razi goes even further. He grants not only that life is similar but also that the subject is receptive to hearing and sight in principle. Yet he argues that there may be an additional condition required for the possibility to be actual, and that condition might be impossible for God. Since this possibility cannot be ruled out, the Ash'ari proof remains uncertain.

Guided Path
1
Read the third aspect
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the phrase 'مشروطاً بشرط مخصوص' (conditioned by a particular condition).
2
Identify the new variable
Al-Razi introduces a third factor: a condition (sharṭ) beyond enabler and receptivity. This condition may be absent for God.
3
Understand the logical impact
Because we don't know all the conditions, the possibility remains that hearing/sight are not possible for God due to a missing condition.
4
See the conclusion
Al-Razi ends by stating that with these probabilities (iḥtimālāt) standing, the Ash'ari proof fails.

السؤال الثاني : إن سلمنا أن كونه حياً صفة زائدة على سبب ذلك الامتناع فلم قلتم : إنه لما حصلت الحياة ، وجب أن يحصل هناك صحة أن يسمع ويبصر ؟ وبيانه من وجوه :
الوجه الأول : أن حياته تعالى مخالفة لحياة الواحد منا ، ولا يلزم من كون حياة الواحد منا مؤثرة في صحة السمع والبصر أن تكون حياته كذلك لما ثبت أن المختلفات في الماهية ، لا يجب استواؤها في اللوازم والأحكام . ألا ترى أن حياة الواحد منا تقتضي صحة الشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة والجهل والظن ، مع أن حياة الله تعالى لا تصحح هذه الصفات ، وما ذاك إلا لأن حياة الله تعالى مخالفة لحياتنا ، فلم يلزم من كون حياتنا مقيدة بصحة هذه الصفات كون حياته كذلك . وإذا ثبت هذا التفاوت في تلك الصورة ، فلم يجوز مثله ههنا ؟
الوجه الثاني : إن بتقدير حصول المماثلة بين الحياتين ، إلا أنا نقول : كما يعتبر في حصول الصحة قيام المصحح ، فكذلك يعتبر فيها كون الذات قابلة . فهب أن الحياة المصححة حاصلة هناك ، إلا أنه لا يمتنع أن يقال : تلك الذات المخصوصة بخصوصها لسائر الذوات ، غير قابلة لهذه الصفة ، ولأجل كون تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصحة انتفت هذه الصحة . والدليل عليه : أن الوجود يصحح جميع الصفات ، ثم إن الوجود حاصل في حق الله تعالى ، مع أن أكثر الصفات ممتنعة في حق الله تعالى ، وما ذاك إلا لأجل أن تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصفات . فكذا ههنا .
والوجه الثالث : هب أن الحياة المصححة لهاتين الصفتين ، والذات المخصوصة قابلة لها من حيث هي هي، إلا أنه لا يمتنع أن يكون حصول هذه الصحة مشروطاً بشرط مخصوص ، ويكون ذلك الشرط ممتنع الحصول في حق الله تعالى ، ففات ذلك الإمكان لفوات الشرط، لا لقوات المصحح . ولقائل أن يقول: وإذا كانت هذه الاحتمالات قائمة فقد سقط هذا الدليل.
The mutakallimun have two weak ways in this chapter, which we will mention and point out their weakness. The first way: that upon which the Ash'aris relied. They said: He, exalted, is living (ḥayy), and anyone who is living, it is valid for him to hear and see. And anyone to whom a description is attributed, it is necessary that he be described by that attribute, and if he is not described by it, then it is necessary that he be described by its opposite, and the opposite of hearing is deafness, and the opposite of sight is blindness, and that is deficiency. And deficiency is impossible for God, so it is necessary that He be described by hearing and sight. This is the estimation of their statement.
And one may say: The objection against it is from several aspects:
The first objection: What do you mean by your saying: He, exalted, is living? If you mean by it that He, exalted, is not impossible to know and have power, then this is conceded. But why do you say: Everyone who is not impossible to know and have power, it is not impossible for him to hear and see? Is the dispute about anything other than that? And if you mean by His being living a meaning other than what we said—that He is not impossible to know and have power—then it must be explained, for it is problematic in conception, let alone being known for assent.
The second objection: If we concede that His being living is an additional attribute to the cause of that impossibility, why do you say: When life is obtained, it becomes necessary that there obtain a possibility (ṣiḥḥa) that He hear and see? Its explanation is from several aspects:
The first aspect: His life, exalted, is different from the life of one of us, and it does not follow from the life of one of us being effective in the possibility of hearing and sight that His life is likewise, because it is established that things differing in essence are not necessarily equal in their concomitants and rulings. Do you not see that the life of one of us necessitates the possibility of desire, aversion, pain, pleasure, ignorance, and conjecture, while the life of God, exalted, does not validate these attributes? And that is only because the life of God, exalted, is different from our life. So it does not follow from our life being restricted to the possibility of these attributes that His life is likewise. And if this disparity is established in that case, why is its like not allowed here?
The second aspect: Even assuming similarity between the two lives, we say: Just as the presence of the enabler (muṣaḥḥiḥ) is considered in obtaining the possibility, so also is the subject being receptive considered. Grant that the enabling life is present there, but it is not impossible to say: That particular subject, by its particularity with respect to other subjects, is not receptive to this attribute, and because that particular subject is not receptive to this possibility, this possibility is eliminated. The proof of that is that existence validates all attributes, yet existence is obtained with respect to God, exalted, while most attributes are impossible with respect to God, exalted—and that is only because that particular subject is not receptive to these attributes. So likewise here.
The third aspect: Grant that the enabling life for these two attributes is present, and the particular subject is receptive to them in itself, but it is not impossible that obtaining this possibility be conditioned by a particular condition, and that condition is impossible of obtaining with respect to God, exalted. So that possibility is lost due to the loss of the condition, not due to the absence of the enabler. And one may say: When these probabilities stand, this proof has fallen.
Source pages p. 194

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and refutation of the first weak argument of the mutakallimun (theologians) regarding the proof of God's attributes of hearing and sight. Al-Razi begins by stating that the theologians have two weak arguments, and he will mention them and point out their weakness. He then presents the first argument, which is relied upon by the Ash'aris. The Ash'ari argument runs as follows: God is living. Anyone who is living can potentially hear and see. For any attribute, a being must either have it or its opposite. The opposite of hearing is deafness, and of sight is blindness, both of which are deficiencies. Deficiency is impossible for God. Therefore, God must be described with hearing and sight.

Al-Razi then introduces a refutation via three objections, but only the first two are fully presented here. The first objection challenges the meaning of 'living'. If 'living' means that God is not incapable of knowledge and power, then that is conceded, but it does not necessarily imply the possibility of hearing and sight. The dispute is precisely about whether life entails these specific attributes. If 'living' means something else, it must be defined, which is problematic.

The second objection proceeds in three sub-arguments (aspects) to show that even if life is an additional attribute, it does not necessitate hearing and sight. The first sub-argument points out that God's life is different in essence from human life. Human life entails possibilities like desire, pain, etc., which are not applicable to God. Therefore, the analogy from human life does not hold. The second sub-argument: even if the lives were similar, the subject (God's essence) may not be receptive to hearing and sight. The proof is that existence itself validates all attributes, but God's essence is not receptive to many attributes, so receptivity is not automatic. The third sub-argument: even if the life is enabling and the subject is receptive, there could be an additional missing condition that prevents the possibility from actualizing. Since these possibilities remain, the Ash'ari proof is invalidated. Al-Razi concludes that with these probabilities standing, the argument collapses.

Key Terms
شرط (condition)
مشروط (conditioned)
مصحح (enabler)
إمكان (possibility)
محال (impossible)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if the enabler and receptivity are present, the attribute must be possible.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that a third factor—a condition—may be needed, and it may be missing for God.

Checkpoint
What does Al-Razi conclude after presenting the third aspect of the second objection?
A) The Ash'ari argument is strengthened.
B) The proof is valid because life and receptivity are sufficient.
C) The proof has fallen because these probabilities stand.
D) There is no need for a condition beyond the enabler.

Key Takeaway
Even with enabler and receptivity, a missing condition can block possibility; the Ash'ari proof is thus uncertain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi grants every premise of the Ash'ari proof except the certainty that no hidden condition blocks possibility. Where in your own belief do you assume certainty without considering that a missing condition—unknown to you—might change everything? How does that humbling thought affect your worship?
?
This lesson shows a logical argument falling because of an unknown possibility. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes feel that despite all the right conditions (knowledge, sincerity, action), something is missing? What condition might God be waiting for you to fulfill?

First Argument: The Incomparability of Divine Life
First argument: the incomparability of divine life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the uniqueness of God's life invalidates any analogy with human life regarding attributes.

Core Idea
God's life is unlike ours, so human prerequisites don't apply.

The author begins his refutation by establishing a foundational principle: God's life is utterly different from human life. Since our life is conditioned by certain limitations, it does not follow that God's life shares those limitations. This simple observation undermines the entire analogy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument.
Notice the author's direct and simple style.
2
Identify the key claim
The author asserts that God's life is different, so the restriction of human life to certain attributes does not imply the same for God.
The word 'مخالفة' is crucial.
3
Reflect on logical structure
The argument is simple: if A is different from B, then properties of A do not necessarily apply to B.
4
Consider the implication
This argument alone dismisses the need for further discussion if accepted.
The rhetorical question reinforces the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أن حياة الله تعالى تصحح هذه الصفات وما ذاك إلا لأن حياة الله تعالى مخالفة لحياتنا فلم يلزم من كون حياتنا مقيدة بصحة هذه الصفات كون حياته كذلك وإذا ثبت هذا التفاوت في تلك الصورة فلم يجوز مثله ههنا الوجه الثاني إن بتقدير حصول المماثلة بين الحياتين إلا أنا نقول كما يعتبر في حصول الصحة قيام المصحح فكذلك يعتبر فيها كون الذات قابلة فهب أن الحياة المصححة حاصلة هناك إلا أنه لا يمتنع أن يقال تلك الذات المخصوصة ( المخالفة ) بخصوصها لسائر الذوات غير قابلة لهذه الصفة ولأجل كون تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصحة انتفت هذه الصحة والدليل عليه أن الوجود يصحح جميع الصفات ثم إن الوجود حاصل في حق الله تعالى مع أن أكثر الصفات ممتنعة في حق الله تعالى ( وما ذاك إلا لأجل أن تلك الذات المخصوصة غير قابلة لهذه الصفات ) فكذا ههنا والوجه الثالث هب أن الحياة المصححة لهاتين الصفتين والذات المخصوصة قابلة لها من حيث هي هي إلا أنه لا يمتنع أن يكون حصول هذه الصحة مشروطاً بشرط مخصوص ويكون ذلك الشرط ممتنع الحصول في حق الله تعالى ففات ذلك الإمكان لفوات الشرط لا لقوات المصحح ولقائل أن يقول وإذا كانت هذه الاحتمالات قائمة فقد سقط هذا الدليل
That the life of God, may He be exalted, confirms these attributes, and that is only because the life of God is different from our life, so it does not follow from the fact that our life is restricted to the validity of these attributes that His life is likewise. And if this disparity is established in that case, why is its like not permissible here? The second aspect: If we grant the likeness between the two lives, yet we say: just as the existence of the corrective (musahhih) is considered for the existence of validity, so also the capability of the essence is considered. Grant that the corrective life exists there, yet it is not impossible to say: that particular essence, (being different) by its specificity from other essences, is not capable of this attribute, and because that particular essence is not capable of this validity, this validity is absent. The evidence for it is that existence confirms all attributes, yet existence is present in God, while most attributes are impossible for God, and that is only because that particular essence is not capable of these attributes. So it is here. The third aspect: Grant that the life that corrects these two attributes, and the particular essence is capable of them as such, yet it is not impossible that the attainment of this validity is conditional upon a specific condition, and that condition is impossible to attain in God, so that possibility is lost due to the loss of the condition, not due to the loss of the corrective. And it may be said: Since these possibilities exist, this proof has collapsed.
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage presents three arguments (wujuh) to refute an opponent who claims that because God's life is the source of His attributes, it must be analogous to human life, which is a prerequisite for attributes like hearing and sight. The author structures the refutation in three numbered aspects (al-wajh al-awwal is not included here, starting with the second). The first argument (implicit) notes that God's life is utterly different from ours, so the analogy fails. The second argument concedes similarity but introduces the idea that the particular divine essence may be inherently incapable of certain attributes, just as existence is common to God and creatures but most attributes are impossible for God. The third argument posits that even if the essence is capable, a missing condition prevents the attribute. The conclusion is that the opponent's proof collapses because these possibilities undermine their reasoning.

Key Terms
حياة
مخالفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's life must be like human life to be a source of attributes.
Correction: The author argues the opposite: God's life is different, so human restrictions don't apply.

Checkpoint
What is the main premise of the first argument?
A) God's life is the same as human life.
B) God's life is different from human life.
C) Human life is not a source of attributes.
D) The analogy is valid.

Key Takeaway
God's incomparable life invalidates any analogy with human attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's attributes, do you ever unconsciously imagine them as limited like human attributes? How does this lesson challenge that tendency? What specific step will you take to remove any trace of analogy from your understanding of God's life?
?
If you truly internalize that God's life is incomparably different from yours, how should that change your reliance on Him? In what area of your life are you still acting as if God's abilities are bounded like human ones?
?
How does accepting the principle of God's incomparable life affect the way you pray or ask from Him? Are there any requests you have held back because you thought God might be too 'limited' to answer? What would it mean to pray with the confidence that His life is utterly beyond your understanding?

Second Argument: Essence Incapability
Second argument: essence incapability despite corrective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the particular divine essence may be incapable of certain attributes even if a corrective like life is present.

Core Idea
Even if life were similar, the divine essence may reject some attributes.

The author now concedes for argument's sake that God's life resembles ours, yet he introduces a critical distinction: the essence itself must be capable of receiving the attribute. Using the example of existence, he shows that even though existence is the ultimate corrective, many attributes are impossible for God because His essence is not amenable to them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the second argument's structure.
The passage begins with 'الوجه الثاني'.
2
Understand the concession
The author grants similarity of life to highlight another flaw.
3
Grasp the two conditions
For an attribute to be valid, we need both a corrective (life) and a capable essence.
The term 'قابلة' appears.
4
Analyze the example of existence
Existence confirms all attributes, yet most are impossible for God due to His essence.
This is the evidence.
5
Apply the analogy
The same reasoning applies to life: even if life is present, the essence may not accept the attribute.

?

Third Argument: Missing Condition
Third argument: missing condition prevents attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that even if both the corrective and essence capability are present, a missing condition can prevent the attribute.

Core Idea
An attribute may be possible in essence but prevented by a missing condition.

The author presents a third possibility: even if life is a valid corrective and the divine essence is capable of the attribute, there may be a specific condition required that is not met in God. This final argument shows that the opponent's proof fails unless all possibilities are eliminated, which they are not.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Focus on the third argument and the conclusion.
The passage begins with 'الوجه الثالث'.
2
Understand the generous concessions
The author grants both corrective and essence capability.
3
Introduce the condition
He proposes that a missing condition (shart) could be the obstacle.
The term 'شرط' is key.
4
See the implication
Since such a condition cannot be ruled out, the opponent's proof collapses.
The conclusion: 'فقد سقط هذا الدليل'.
5
Appreciate the rhetorical style
The author concludes that the proof has fallen due to these possibilities.

This passage presents three arguments (wujuh) to refute an opponent who claims that because God's life is the source of His attributes, it must be analogous to human life, which is a prerequisite for attributes like hearing and sight. The author structures the refutation in three numbered aspects (al-wajh al-awwal is not included here, starting with the second). The first argument (implicit) notes that God's life is utterly different from ours, so the analogy fails. The second argument concedes similarity but introduces the idea that the particular divine essence may be inherently incapable of certain attributes, just as existence is common to God and creatures but most attributes are impossible for God. The third argument posits that even if the essence is capable, a missing condition prevents the attribute. The conclusion is that the opponent's proof collapses because these possibilities undermine their reasoning.

Key Terms
شرط
مصحح
إمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if a cause is present, the effect must necessarily follow.
Correction: The author shows that additional conditions can prevent the effect even if the cause is present.

Checkpoint
According to the third argument, what could prevent an attribute even if the corrective and essence capability are present?
A) The corrective is weak.
B) The essence is not capable.
C) A required condition is missing.
D) The attribute is impossible by nature.

Key Takeaway
A missing condition can prevent an attribute even when the cause and recipient are ready.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you examine your expectations and submit to what Allah reveals, even if it challenges your logic?

?
This argument shows that a proof can collapse if possibilities remain unaddressed. Are you willing to let go of your own preconceived notions about God when they are shown to be based on conditions that do not apply to Him? What will you do today to align your understanding with what Allah has revealed, rather than what your mind dictates?

The Meaning of Deafness and Blindness: Objection and Response
Objection and response about the meaning of deafness and blindness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the opponent's objection by showing that their definitions of deafness and blindness lead to either circular reasoning or an unsubstantiated claim.

Core Idea
Deafness as absence is circular; as existential opposite, unproven.

In this passage, the opponent concedes that the attribute of hearing and sight exist in God but questions what deafness and blindness mean, since they claim these are opposites. Sanusi forces them into a dilemma: either deafness is mere absence, which makes their argument circular, or it is an existential opposite, which they cannot prove. This lesson unpacks that refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the opponent concedes, then asks a question, then the author presents a two-part refutation.
The opponent's concession is important—they grant that the attribute exists in God.
2
Examine the first horn: deafness as absence
Sanusi presents the conditional: 'If you mean by deafness the absence of hearing...' Then he shows that this reduces to saying absence is impossible, which is exactly what is being disputed. This is circular.
The argument becomes a repetition of the claim.
3
Examine the second horn: deafness as existential opposite
The other possibility is that deafness is an existential meaning (ma'nā wujūdī) opposing hearing. Sanusi denies this and demands proof.
Opponents rarely succeed in proving such a claim.
4
Summarize the refutation
Both possible definitions are refuted: one is circular, the other is unproven. Therefore, the objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث سلمنا أن صحة هذه الصفة حاصلة في ذات الله تعالى · ولكن ما المراد بالصمم والعمى اللذين جعلتموهما ضدين للسمع والبصر إن عنيتم بالصمم عدم السمع وبالعمى عدم البصر فحينئذ يرجع حاصل قولكم إلى أن السمع والبصر لو لم يحصلا لكان عدمهما حاصلا وعدمهما نقص وذلك محال على الله فيقال لكم وهل النزاع وقع إلا فيه وهو أن حصول هاتين الصفتين هل هو واجب أم لا وعدمها هل هو ممتنع أم لا وإذا كان حاصل دليلكم يرجع إلى عدم هاتين الصفتين محال في حق الله تعالى كان هذا إعادة لنفس الدعوى ومعلوم أنه باطل وإن ادعيتم أن الصمم معنى وجودي يضاد السماع وأن العمى معنى وجودي يضاد الإبصار فكل ذلك ممنوع وما الدليل على صحته
The third question: We grant that the validity of this attribute exists in God's essence. But what is meant by deafness and blindness which you have made opposites of hearing and sight? If you mean by deafness the absence of hearing, and by blindness the absence of sight, then the gist of your statement becomes: if hearing and sight did not occur, then their absence would occur, and their absence is a deficiency. And that is impossible for God. Then it is said to you: Is the dispute about anything other than that? Namely, whether the existence of these two attributes is necessary or not, and whether their absence is impossible or not. And if the essence of your proof reduces to the statement that the absence of these two attributes is impossible for God, this is a repetition of the very claim. And it is known that it is false. And if you claim that deafness is an existential meaning opposing hearing, and that blindness is an existential meaning opposing sight, then all that is denied. And what is the proof of its correctness?
Source pages p. 195

Commentary
This passage is an objection (su'al) and its response. The opponent concedes the validity of the attribute in God but asks about the meaning of deafness and blindness. The author presents a dilemma: If the opponent means absence (adam), then their argument merely restates that absence is impossible, which is the very point in dispute, making it circular. If they mean existential opposites (ma'nā wujūdī), then they are making an unsubstantiated claim. The response thus refutes both possibilities.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Deafness is always defined as the absence of hearing, and the author agrees with that definition.
Correction: The author does not define deafness himself; he merely exploits the opponent's possible definitions to show that either leads to failure. The point is to refute the objection, not to define terms.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if deafness is defined as the absence of hearing?
A) It becomes an existential opposite.
B) The opponent's argument becomes circular.
C) It proves that God must have hearing.
D) It is a valid definition that leads to the desired conclusion.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi refutes the opponent by showing that any definition of deafness either makes the argument circular or requires unproven assumptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponent demanded proof for a definition they themselves proposed. Are you willing to hold yourself to the same standard of intellectual honesty, even when defending deeply held beliefs? What step will you take today to check a belief you hold for which you have no proof?

Objection: Capability for Opposites Does Not Force Actualization
Objection and response about essence capable of opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection to the claim that an essence capable of two opposites must be characterized by one of them, and the counterexample involving bodies and human will.

Core Idea
Capability for opposites does not force actualization.

In this passage, the interlocutor continues to press objections against the necessity that a capable essence must have one of two opposites. They concede the existential nature of deafness and blindness but challenge the logical premise. The response offers concrete examples from bodies, air, and human psychology to show that capacity does not entail actual possession. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premise
Understand the opponent's concession and the core question: why must an essence capable of two opposites be described by one?
The opponent grants that deafness and blindness are existential opposites.
2
Identify the counterexamples
Find the two examples given: the body of air (capable of tastes and colors but has none) and human will (capable of will and aversion but often neither).
These show capability does not entail actual possession.
3
Reflect on the incomplete conclusion
The passage ends with 'so why...'—consider what conclusion the author is driving at: the premise that capability necessitates actualization is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الرابع سلمنا كون الصمم والعمى معنيين وجوديين مضادين للسمع والبصر فلم قلتم إن الذات القابلة للفسدين يجب اتصافها بأحدهما وما الدليل على صحة هذه المقدمة ثم نقول الذي يدل على فساده أن الأجسام بأمرها متساوية فتكون متساوية في قبول الصفات فجسم الهواء قابل للطعوم المختلفة والألوان المختلفة أنه غير موصوف بشيء منها وكذلك الواحد منا يصح أن يكون موصوفا بالإرادة والكراهية ثم إنا نعلم أفعال أهل السوقة مع أنا لا نريدها ولا نكرهها فلم
The fourth question: We grant that deafness and blindness are existential meanings opposing hearing and sight. Why did you say that the essence capable of the two opposites must be described by one of them? And what is the proof of the validity of this premise? Then we say: What indicates its corruption is that bodies are equal in themselves, so they are equal in the capacity for attributes. Thus the body of air is capable of different tastes and different colors, yet it is not described by any of them. Similarly, one of us can be described with will and aversion. Then we know the actions of common people, while we neither will them nor dislike them. So why...
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is an objection (su'al) and its response. The opponent concedes that deafness and blindness are existential opposites but challenges the premise that an essence capable of two opposites must be characterized by one of them. The response provides counterexamples: bodies are equally capable of various attributes but do not possess them; air can receive tastes and colors but has none; a person can will or dislike actions but often has no attitude toward common people's actions. The argument is left incomplete at 'so why...', suggesting the conclusion is that the premise is false.

Correction: In this context, 'الفسدين' refers to the two opposites (deafness and blindness), not corruption.

Key Takeaway
Capacity for opposites does not logically require actual possession of one of them, as illustrated by bodies, air, and human will.

The Fifth Objection: Defining Deficiency
Objection about the meaning of deficiency when claiming deafness and blindness are deficiencies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining key terms like 'deficiency' is critical in theological argumentation, and how this objection exposes ambiguity in the opponent's claim.

Core Idea
A term without a clear definition is a weapon that can cut both ways.

This passage presents the fifth objection from the theologian against the claim that deafness and blindness are deficiencies in God. Instead of immediately refuting the claim, the objection forces the opponent to clarify what they mean by 'deficiency.' This is a classic technique in kalam: exposing ambiguity before proceeding. By doing so, the theologian ensures the argument is built on clear foundations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhetorical structure come through.
2
Identify the two possible meanings of deficiency
The passage presents two conditional branches. Identify what each branch says about the meaning of 'deficiency' and what follows from it.
Notice how the objection does not itself define deficiency but asks the opponent to do so.
3
Reflect on the rhetorical strategy
Consider why the theologian would start by asking for a definition rather than arguing directly. How does this strengthen the overall refutation?
This connects to the learning objective about clarity in theological terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الخامس قولكم الصمم والعمى نقص فما المراد من النقص فإن عنيتم بالنقص حصول أمر يمنع من كونه تعالى فاعلاً للعالم وخالقاً له قلتم إن حصول هاتين الصفتين يمنع من كونه تعالى خالقا للعالم وفاعلاً للعالم وإن عنيتم به معنى آخر فلا بد من بيانه
The fifth question: Your saying that deafness and blindness are a deficiency. What is meant by deficiency? But if you mean another meaning, then it must be clarified.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is an objection (su'al) that asks for clarification of the term 'deficiency' (naqs). The opponent's claim is that deafness and blindness are deficiencies, but the term is ambiguous. The response presents two possible interpretations: either deficiency is something that prevents God from being the agent and creator of the world, in which case the opponent must prove that these attributes actually prevent that; or it means something else, which must be explained. The objection thus forces the opponent to define their terms clearly.

The Proof that Deficiency is Impossible for God
Objection asking for proof, with alternative self-evident argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two approaches (self-evidence and transmitted proofs) used to argue that deficiency is impossible for God, and the alternative self-evident argument presented in the passage.

Core Idea
Self-evidence tells us that God, being perfect, cannot be deficient.

This lesson examines the sixth objection to the concept of God's perfection. Scholars have debated whether the impossibility of deficiency in God is known through self-evident intuition or through transmitted religious proofs. In this passage, the author presents a simple self-evident argument that a being that hears and sees is perfect, and therefore God must be described by perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection asks for a proof (dalil) that deficiency is impossible for God.
2
Identify the two scholarly positions
The passage mentions two groups: those who claim self-evidence (badiha) and those who rely on transmitted proofs (adilla sam'iyya). Consider the difference.
3
Analyze the alternative argument
The author offers a 'closer way' involving a conditional syllogism: a living being that hears and sees is perfect; God is such a being; therefore God is perfect. Recognize the logical structure.
This argument uses rational intuition, not revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال السادس ما الدليل على أن النقص على الله محال ثم ههنا منهم من ادعى البديهة فيه ومنهم من عول على الدلائل السمعية أما الأول فنقول إن صح ادعاء البديهة فيه فههنا طريق آخر أقرب منه وهو أن يقال الحي الذي يكون سميعاً بصيراً كامل والذي لا يكون كذلك ناقص والبديهة حاكمة بأنه يجب وصف الله بصفة الكمال لا بصفة النقصان
The sixth question: What is the proof that deficiency is impossible for God? Then there are among them those who claim self-evidence in it, and among them those who rely on transmitted proofs. As for the first, we say: If the claim of self-evidence is valid concerning it, then here is a way that is even closer than it: which is to say: The living being that is hearing and seeing is perfect, and the one that is not so is deficient, and self-evidence judges that it is necessary to describe God with the attribute of perfection, not with the attribute of deficiency.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is an objection (su'al) asking for the proof that deficiency is impossible for God. It notes that some scholars claim this is self-evident (badiha), while others rely on transmitted proofs (adilla sam'iyya). The author then offers an alternative argument that is even more immediate: self-evidence tells us that a living being that hears and sees is perfect, and the opposite is deficient; therefore, God must be described by perfection, not deficiency. This provides a logical argument based on rational intuition.

Correction: The passage only offers an alternative self-evident argument; it does not reject transmitted proofs. It says 'if the claim of self-evidence is valid... here is a way that is even closer'.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of deficiency in God can be established through a self-evident rational argument: a being that hears and sees is perfect, so God, being living, is perfect, not deficient.

The Circularity of Claiming Deficiency is Impossible for God
The author refutes a claim that deficiency is impossible for God by showing it is circular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how circular reasoning undermines theological arguments about divine attributes, using the example of deafness and blindness.

Core Idea
Circular arguments merely repeat the claim in dispute.

The author confronts an opponent who argues that deficiency (like deafness and blindness) cannot be attributed to God. He exposes a logical flaw: the opponent's proof is nothing but a restatement of the very claim under debate. This lesson examines why such circular reasoning invalidates the argument and why additional premises must be justified.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the author's direct style and the structure of the refutation.
The opening word 'نقص' may be a fragment from the opponent's premise.
2
Identify the opponent's claim and the author's rebuttal
Find where the opponent asserts deficiency is impossible for God. Then see how the author points out that the 'proof' is just repeating the same claim. This is the core of the critique.
3
Understand why circular arguments are invalid
Reflect on why a proof that restates the claim is not a valid argument. In theology, such reasoning does not advance discussion; it assumes what needs to be proven.
The author also challenges the opponent's claim that deafness and blindness are positive attributes, demanding proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقص . وذلك محال على الله . فيقال لكم: وهل النزاع وقع إلا فيه ؟ وهو أن حصول هاتين الصفتين . هل هو واجب أم لا ؟ وعدمها هل هو ممتنع أم لا ؟ وإذا كان حاصل دليلكم يرجع إلى عدم هاتين الصفتين محال في حق الله تعالى ، كان هذا إعادة لنفس الدعوى . ومعلوم أنه باطل . وإن ادعيتم أن الصمم معنى وجودي يضاد السماع، وأن العمى معنى وجودي يضاد الإبصار ، فكل ذلك ممنوع . وما الدليل على صحته ؟
Deficiency. And that is impossible for God. So it is said to you: Has the dispute occurred except concerning it? That is, the occurrence of these two attributes—is it necessary or not? And their absence—is it impossible or not? And if the conclusion of your proof reduces to the absence of these two attributes being impossible in the right of God Almighty, this is a repetition of the same claim. And it is known that it is false. And if you claim that deafness is a positive meaning opposing hearing, and that blindness is a positive meaning opposing sight, all of that is denied. And what is the proof of its correctness?
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an opponent's argument. The opponent claimed that deficiency (such as deafness and blindness) is impossible for God. The author points out that this is circular: the opponent is simply asserting what is in dispute, namely whether the absence of hearing and sight is impossible for God. Then the author rejects the opponent's claim that deafness and blindness are positive attributes opposing hearing and sight, calling it an unfounded assertion without proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One can prove that deficiency is impossible for God by simply stating that deficiency is impossible for God.
Correction: That is circular reasoning; a valid proof must offer independent evidence, not just repeat the claim.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main criticism of the opponent's argument?
A) The opponent used weak scriptural evidence.

C) The opponent denied the existence of deafness and blindness.
D) The opponent failed to define 'deficiency'.

Key Takeaway
The opponent's argument fails because it simply reasserts the point at issue without providing independent evidence, a classic case of circular reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Refuting the Claim: Capability Does Not Require Actual Attributes
The author presents and refutes a logical fallacy regarding capability and actual attribute possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the capability to possess opposite attributes does not necessitate possessing one of them, as demonstrated through counterexamples.

Core Idea
Capability does not imply actual possession; many things are capable but unqualified.

This lesson presents the fourth objection against the claim that deafness and blindness are positive attributes. The opponent argues that if a substance can have two opposite attributes, it must have one. The author refutes this by providing clear counterexamples from everyday experience, showing that capability alone is insufficient for actual qualification.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Understand the opponent's claim: since deafness and blindness are positive opposites of hearing and sight, a substance capable of both must be qualified by one.
2
Identify the refutation's logic
The author denies the premise that capability entails possession, and points out the logical flaw.
3
Examine the examples
Note the two counterexamples: air is capable of tastes and colors but has none; a human can be capable of will and aversion without actually willing or disliking. These show the flaw in the opponent's reasoning.

السؤال الرابع : سلمنا كون الصمم والعمى معنيين وجوديين مضادين للسمع والبصر . فلم قلتم : إن الذات القابلة للفسدين يجب اتصافها بأحدهما ؟ وما الدليل على صحة هذه المقدمة ؟ ثم نقول : الذي يدل على فساده : أن الأجسام بأمرها متساوية ( فتكون متساوية ) في قبول الصفات . فجسم الهواء قابل للطعوم المختلفة والألوان المختلفة، أنه غير موصوف بشيء منها، وكذلك الواحد منا، يصح أن يكون موصوفا بالإرادة والكراهية . مع أنا لا نريدها ولا نكرهها . فلم
The fourth objection: We grant that deafness and blindness are two positive meanings opposing hearing and sight. So why do you say that a substance capable of the two defects must be qualified by one of them? And what is the proof of the correctness of this premise? Then we say: What indicates its invalidity is that bodies in themselves are equal (thus they are equal) in accepting attributes. For a body of air is capable of different tastes and different colors, yet it is not qualified by any of them. Similarly, one of us can be qualified with will and aversion, even though we neither will nor dislike them.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth objection against an opponent who claims that deafness and blindness are positive attributes. The argument is: Even if we grant that deafness and blindness are positive opposites of hearing and sight, it does not follow that a substance capable of both must possess one. The author refutes this by giving examples: air is capable of tastes and colors but has none; a human can be capable of will and aversion without actually willing or disliking something. This shows that capability does not necessitate actual possession.

The Sixth Objection: Proving God's Perfection through Intuition and Scripture
The author challenges two proofs for God's perfection and offers a better intuitive argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author critiques two common arguments for the impossibility of deficiency in God and proposes a more direct intuitive alternative.

Core Idea
Intuition and scripture both point to God's perfection, but a simple intuitive argument is more effective.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection: how do we prove that deficiency is impossible for God? He examines two approaches used by opponents—intuition and scriptural evidence—and shows their shortcomings. More importantly, he offers a simpler, more direct intuitive argument that cuts through unnecessary complexity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the author's structure: he first poses the question, then discusses two groups, and finally offers his own critique.
The passage is dense; read slowly to follow the argument.
2
Identify the two groups mentioned
The author mentions two kinds of opponents: those who claim intuition (بديهة) and those who rely on scriptural evidence (دلائل سمعية). Find where each is discussed.
The first group is addressed with 'أما الأول' and the second with 'وأما الذين'.
3
Understand the author's alternative intuitive argument
For the intuition group, the author proposes a shorter intuitive argument: a living being that hears and sees is perfect, one that does not is deficient; intuition demands we attribute perfection to God, so He must be hearing and seeing. Compare this to the 'lengthy way' of the opponents.
The author claims this way is 'closer' (أقرب) and 'more appropriate' (أولى).
4
Analyze the critique of scriptural evidence
For those relying on consensus (إجماع), the author says their argument is weak because the verses proving consensus are themselves weaker than the direct verses about God's hearing and sight. Direct scripture is preferable.
This shows the author's methodological preference for direct scriptural evidence over secondary proofs like consensus.

السؤال السادس : ما الدليل على أن النقص على الله محال ؟ ثم ههنا منهم من ادعى البديهة فيه ، ومنهم من عول على الدلائل السمعية . أما الأول فنقول : إن صح ادعاء البديهة فيه . فههنا طريق آخر أقرب منه : وهو ان يقال : الحي الذي يكون سميعاً بصيراً كامل والذي لا يكون كذلك ناقص والبديهة حاكمة بأنه يجب وصف الله بصفة الكمال ، لا بصفة النقصان ، فوجب وصفه بكونه سميعاً بصيراً . وإذا ثبت هذا ظهر أن ذكر هذا الطريق أولى من ذكر الطريق الطويل الذي ذكرتموه . وأما الذين حولوا في تقرير هذه المقدمة على الإجماع. فكلامهم ضعيف ، لأن الآيات الدالة على كون الإجماع حجة أضعف دلالة بكثير من الآيات الدالة على كونه سميعاً بصيراً . وأقول : فإن كان الاكتفاء بالمقدمة السمعية جائزاً فالاكتفاء بتلك الآيات أولى ، لأنها أدل على المقصود ، وأولى من المقدمات . فهذا هو الكلام في هذا الدليل .
The sixth objection: What is the proof that deficiency is impossible for God? Then here among them are those who claimed intuition concerning it, and among them are those who relied on scriptural evidences. As for the first, we say: If the claim of intuition is valid, then here is another way closer than it: which is to say: The living being that is hearing and seeing is perfect, and that which is not so is deficient, and intuition rules that it is necessary to describe God with the attribute of perfection, not deficiency. So it is necessary to describe Him as hearing and seeing. And when this is established, it appears that mentioning this way is more appropriate than mentioning the lengthy way that you mentioned. As for those who rely on establishing this premise through consensus, their speech is weak, because the verses indicating that consensus is a proof are much weaker in indication than the verses indicating that He is hearing and seeing. And I say: If reliance on a scriptural premise is permissible, then reliance on those verses is more appropriate, because they are more indicative of the purpose, and more appropriate than the premises. This is the discussion concerning this proof.
Source pages p. 196

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth objection, asking for proof that deficiency is impossible for God. The author then discusses two approaches used by opponents: intuition (badiha) and scriptural evidence. For those using intuition, the author offers a shorter, more direct intuitive argument: a living being that hears and sees is perfect, one that does not is deficient, and intuition demands we attribute perfection to God, thus He must be hearing and seeing. This is better than the opponent's lengthy proof. For those relying on consensus (ijma'), the author argues that consensus itself is proven by weak scriptural evidence compared to the clear verses on God's hearing and sight. Thus, directly using those verses is preferable. The author concludes that this covers the discussion of this proof.

Correction: The author demonstrates that a simple intuitive argument (from the nature of perfection) is more direct and powerful, and that direct scriptural verses are stronger than consensus-based arguments.

The Mu'tazilite Argument for God's Perception
Mu'tazilite proof for God's perception with an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite syllogism for God's perception and the initial objection regarding the difference between human and divine life.

Core Idea
God's perception is argued by analogy to human perception, but the analogy is questioned.

In this passage, the author presents the Mu'tazilite rational argument for God's perception based on His being alive and defect-free. They use human sensory examples to prove the premise that a living being without defect perceives. However, an immediate objection is raised, pointing out the dissimilarity between human and divine life. This lesson examines both the argument and the initial objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazilite syllogism
Identify the two premises: God is living without defect; every living being without defect perceives perceptibles when present. The conclusion: God perceives perceptibles when present.
The argument is a simple syllogism.
2
Examine the human examples
Notice the examples given: a sound eye sees visible objects, a sound ear hears sounds, a sound limb feels heat. These are meant to prove the second premise empirically.
The Mu'tazila use induction from human experience.
3
Understand the objection
The objection asserts that the human case is not analogous to God because human life and essence differ from God's life and essence in quiddity. Therefore, the ruling that applies to humans may not apply to God.
The objection is cut off but continues in the next pages.

والطريق الثاني - طريق المعتزلة )) وأما المعتزلة فقالوا : الله تعالى حي لا آفة به ، وكل من كان حياً ، لا آفة به ، فإنه يجب أن يكون مدركاً للمدركات عند حضورها ينتج أنه تعالى يدركها عند حضورها . أما المقدمة الأولى ، وهي أنه تعالى حي لا آفة به ، فقد ثبتت . وأما المقدمة الثانية وهي أن كل من كان حياً لا آفة به ، فإنه يدرك المدركات عند حضورها . فقد احتجوا عليه بأن الواحد منا إذا كان حياً ، وكان سليماً عن الآفات فإنه لا بد وأن يدرك . ألا ترى أن الحي إذا كان سليم العين ، وحضر المرئي مع الشرائط فإنه يبصره ، وإذا كان سليم الأذن وحضر المسموع فإنه يسمعه ، وإذا كان سليم الأعضاء ، ووضع الجمر على جسده ، فإنه يحس بتلك الحرارة ، فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الحي إذا كان سليماً عن الآفة ، فإنه يجب أن يدرك المدركات عند حضورها . ولقائل أن يقول : ( السؤال الأول ) كون الواحد منا حياً ، يخالف كون الله تعالى حياً . وأيضاً : فذات الواحد مخالفة لذات الله تعالى بالماهية ، ولا يلزم من ثبوت حكم
And the second way—the way of the Mu'tazila. As for the Mu'tazila, they said: God Almighty is living, with no defect in Him, and every living being that has no defect must perceive perceptibles when they are present. This yields that He Almighty perceives them when present. As for the first premise—that He Almighty is living with no defect—it has been established. As for the second premise—that every living being with no defect perceives perceptibles when present—they argued for it by saying that one of us, when alive and free from defects, necessarily perceives. Do you not see that a living being, if its eye is sound and the visible object is present with the conditions, sees it; if its ear is sound and the audible object is present, it hears it; if its limbs are sound and a coal is placed on its body, it feels that heat. So it is established by what we mentioned: that a living being, if free from defect, must perceive perceptibles when they are present. And someone may say: The first objection: That one of us is living differs from God Almighty being living. Also, the essence of one is different from the essence of God Almighty in quiddity, and it does not follow from the establishment of a ruling...
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilite argument for God's perception. They argue: God is living without defect. Any living being without defect necessarily perceives perceptibles when present. Therefore, God perceives all perceptibles when present. To prove the second premise, they use examples from human experience: a person with sound eyes sees visible objects, with sound ears hears sounds, with sound limbs feels heat. This shows that a healthy living being perceives. An objection is then raised: the human case is different because human life is not the same as divine life, and human essence differs from divine essence in quiddity (māhiyya). Therefore, the ruling that applies to humans may not apply to God. The passage cuts off at this point, but the objection continues in the following pages.

The Mu'tazila Syllogism for Divine Perception
The Mu'tazila present a syllogism for God's perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila syllogism that argues God's perception of all things from His life and freedom from defect.

Core Idea
Life without defect implies universal perception; God is such a living being.

The Mu'tazila put forward a logical argument to prove that God perceives all perceptible things. They build their case on two premises: first, that God is alive and free from defect, and second, that any living being without defect must perceive everything it encounters. The conclusion then follows necessarily. This lesson unpacks that syllogism and the empirical evidence they use to support the second premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic source once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the literal translation to ensure you understand the meaning.
Pay attention to the logical structure: premises and conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises and conclusion
The argument is a syllogism. Find the first premise (God is alive, free from defect), the second premise (any such being perceives perceptibles when present), and the conclusion (God perceives them).
The Mu'tazila claim the first premise is already established elsewhere.
3
Examine the proof for the second premise
The Mu'tazila use empirical examples from human experience. Note the three examples: sight with sound eyes, hearing with sound ears, and feeling heat with sound limbs.
They generalize from humans to all living beings, including God.
4
Reflect on the strength of the argument
Consider whether extending human sensory experience to God is valid. The commentary will help clarify the logical structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المعتزلة فقالوا : الله تعالى حي لا آفة به ، وكل من كان حياً ، لا آفة به ، فإنه يجب أن يكون مدركاً للمدركات عند حضورها ينتج أنه تعالى يدركها عند حضورها . أما المقدمة الأولى ، وهي أنه تعالى حي لا آفة به ، فقد ثبتت . وأما المقدمة الثانية وهي أن كل من كان حياً لا آفة به ، فإنه يدرك المدركات عند حضورها . فقد احتجوا عليه بأن الواحد منا إذا كان حياً ، وكان سليماً عن الآفات فإنه لا بد وأن يدرك . ألا ترى أن الحي إذا كان سليم العين ، وحضر المرئي مع الشرائط فإنه يبصره ، وإذا كان سليم الأذن وحضر المسموع فإنه يسمعه ، وإذا كان سليم الأعضاء ، ووضع الجمر على جسده ، فإنه يحس بتلك الحرارة ، فيثبت بما ذكرنا : أن الحي إذا كان سليماً عن الآفة ، فإنه يجب أن يدرك المدركات عند حضورها .
As for the Mu'tazila, they said: God Almighty is living, free from defect. And whoever is living, free from defect, must be perceptive of perceptibles when they are present, yielding that He perceives them when they are present. As for the first premise, that He is living, free from defect, it has been established. As for the second premise, that whoever is living, free from defect, perceives perceptibles when they are present, they argued for it by [the fact] that one of us, if he is living and sound from defects, must perceive. Do you not see that the living person, if he has sound eyes and the visible object is present with the conditions, he sees it; if he has sound ears and the audible is present, he hears it; if he has sound limbs and a piece of burning coal is placed on his body, he senses that heat. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned: that the living person, if he is free from defect, must perceive perceptibles when they are present.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazila argument for proving that God perceives all perceptibles (including hearing and seeing). The argument is structured as a syllogism. Premise 1: God is living and free from defect. Premise 2: Every living being free from defect necessarily perceives perceptibles when they are present. Conclusion: Therefore, God perceives all perceptibles when they are present. The Mu'tazila assert that the first premise is already established (presumably from earlier arguments for God's existence and attributes). To prove the second premise, they rely on empirical observation of humans. They argue that a human being who is alive and free from physical defects inevitably perceives sensory objects when they are present. For example, a person with sound eyes sees visible objects, with sound ears hears sounds, and with sound limbs feels heat when a hot object touches the skin. This inductive generalization from human experience is then extended to God, who is also alive and free from defect. Thus, they conclude that God must have the same kind of perception, including sight and hearing.

Key Terms
حي
آفة
مدرك
المدركات
الحضور
سليم
الإدراك الحسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila argument is a simple analogy between humans and God.
Correction: The argument is a deductive syllogism with a universal premise. The empirical examples serve as evidence for the universal premise, not as a direct comparison to God. The logic is: The universal premise (all defect-free living beings perceive) applies to God because He is a defect-free living being. It is not an analogy but a categorical inference.

Checkpoint
What is the second premise in the Mu'tazila argument for God's perception?
A) God is living and free from defect.
B) Whoever is living and free from defect perceives perceptibles when present.
C) Humans perceive with their senses.
D) God perceives all things eternally.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila use a syllogism rooted in human empirical observation to argue that God's life entails perception of all things when they are present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live knowing that God sees and hears every thought, every glance, every secret moment? If you were fully convinced of His immediate perception, what would you change today?
?
When no one else is watching, do you act as if God is present? What specific habit or hidden sin would you abandon if you believed that God perceives it right now?
?
The Mu'tazila argued that God's life entails perception. If God is living and aware of all your actions, does that give you comfort or terror? How will you respond to that reality tonight?

First Objection: Disanalogy of Life and Essence
First objection against the Mu'tazila argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against the Mu'tazila argument that God perceives because He is alive: that human life and essence differ from God's, so the analogy fails.

Core Idea
Human and divine life are fundamentally different, so the analogy breaks.

The Mu'tazila argue that since life in humans entails perception, and God is alive, He must also perceive. The first objection challenges this by pointing out that the life and essence of humans are not the same as God's life and essence. Therefore, what holds for human life may not hold for divine life.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic text of the first objection and its translation. Notice how the objection distinguishes between human and divine life and essence.
The objection uses the concept of quiddity (ماهية) to stress essential difference.
2
Understand the logic
The objection claims that if two things differ in essence, a ruling established for one need not apply to the other. This is a basic principle of analogy.
Consider why this is a strong critique of the Mu'tazila argument.
3
Reflect on the implication
If the foundation of the analogy is flawed, the entire argument for divine perception collapses. This objection targets the core assumption of the Mu'tazila.
