ولقائل أن يقول : السؤال الأول : كون الواحد منا حياً ، يخالف كون الله تعالى حياً . وأيضاً : فذات الواحد مخالفة لذات الله تعالى بالماهية ، ولا يلزم من ثبوت حكم في ماهية ، ثبوت مثله فيها يخالف تلك الماهية . السؤال الثاني : هب أن الحياة صفة مشتركة بين الشاهد والغائب ، إلا أن يكون إيجاب الحياة لصفة المدركية ، موقوف على شرائط تمنع ثبوتها في حق الله تعالى ، وحينئذ يمتنع حصول ذلك الحكم في الغائب لفوات تلك الشرائط . وقال بعض المتأخرين من المعتزلة : إن تلك الأشياء لما كانت ممتنعة الثبوت في حق الله تعالى خرجت عن كونها شرائط بصحة الإدراك ، لأن المعدوم يمتنع كونه شرط لتأثر المقتضى ، وإذا خرجت عن كونها شرائط ، وكان المقتضى حاصلاً وجب ترتب الأثر عليه . ونقول : هذا باطل . لأن بتقدير أن يكون الشيء مشروطاً لشيء ، فعند قوات الشرط ، يجب فوات المشروط - إلا أنه يحصل المشروط بدونه - ولو صححنا ما ذكرتم لبطل قياس الخلف ، فإنا إذا قلنا : لم يوجد اللازم فوجب أن لا يوجد الملزوم . فيقال : لم لم يوجد اللازم فقد خرج عن كونه لازماً ، فوجب أن يحصل الملزوم بدونه ، فثبت : أن الكلام الذي ذكرتم ، لو صح لبطل قياس الخلف . السؤال الثالث : هب أن كون الحي حياً يوجب المدركية ، إلا أن هذا إنما يجري في الإدراك الحسي ، فإن كون الحي حياً يوجب هذا النوع من الإدراك فأما الإبصار والسماع ، فلا يحصلان المجرد كون الحي حياً . فبطل قولكم : إن الموجب لهذا الإدراك ، مجرد كونه حياً . وهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب . والله أعلم .
And someone might say: The first objection: That one of us is living differs from God being living. Also, the essence of one of us differs from the essence of God in quiddity, and it does not follow from the establishment of a ruling in one quiddity, the establishment of its like in what differs from that quiddity. The second objection: Granted that life is a common attribute between the witnessed and the unseen, nevertheless the causation of life for the attribute of perceptiveness may be dependent on conditions that prevent its establishment with respect to God Almighty, and then the occurrence of that ruling in the unseen is impossible due to the absence of those conditions. And some later Mu'tazila said: Since those things are impossible to establish with respect to God Almighty, they cease to be conditions for the validity of perception, because the non-existent cannot be a condition for the effect of the cause. And when they cease to be conditions, and the cause is present, the effect must follow. And we say: This is false. Because if something is a condition for something, then when the condition is absent, the conditioned must be absent—except that the conditioned occurs without it. And if we validate what you mentioned, the argument of modus tollens would be invalidated. For if we say: The necessary did not exist, so the necessitated must not exist, it would be said: Why did the necessary not exist? Then it would have ceased to be necessary, so the necessitated must occur without it. Thus it is established that what you said, if valid, would invalidate the argument of modus tollens. The third objection: Granted that being alive necessitates perceptiveness, but this only applies to sensory perception. For being alive necessitates this type of perception. As for seeing and hearing, they are not obtained by mere being alive. Therefore your statement is false: that the necessitator of this perception is mere being alive. And this is the complete discussion on this topic. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 197

Commentary
This passage presents three objections to the Mu'tazila argument that God perceives because He is alive. The first objection challenges the analogy between human life and divine life: human life is different from God's life, and the essences (quiddities) are different, so a ruling established for one quiddity (i.e., human life entails perception) does not necessarily apply to a different quiddity. The second objection concedes that life may be common but argues that perception might be conditional on certain factors that are absent in God, thus preventing the inference. Some later Mu'tazila responded to this by claiming that because those conditions are impossible for God, they cease to be conditions, and the cause (life) must produce its effect. The author refutes this response: if a condition is truly a condition, its absence necessitates the absence of the conditioned; the claim that the conditioned occurs without it is false. Moreover, if this reasoning were accepted, it would destroy the logical principle of modus tollens (قياس الخلف), which is used to infer the absence of a cause from the absence of its effect. The third objection states that life only necessitates sensory perception, not the specific acts of hearing and seeing, which are distinct modes of perception. Therefore, the Mu'tazila argument fails to prove that God hears and sees specifically. The passage concludes the discussion in this chapter.

Key Terms
السؤال
الشاهد
الغائب
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila assume that life is a univocal attribute that yields the same effects in God and humans.
Correction: The objection shows that life is not univocal; the life of humans and God differ in nature, so the inference is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the first objection?
A) Life is a common attribute between God and humans.
B) Human life and God's life are essentially different, so the analogy fails.
C) God's essence is unknown.
D) Perception requires additional conditions.

Key Takeaway
The first objection establishes that analogical reasoning fails when the entities being compared differ in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever unconsciously assumed that God's mercy or justice must feel exactly like human mercy or justice? How does this lesson's first objection challenge that assumption, and what concrete step will you take this week to remind yourself that God is not like His creation?
?
The objection warns against transferring rulings from one essence to another. Is there a time you judged God's actions by a human standard and felt confusion or anger? What would you say to yourself now, armed with the insight that divine and human essences are fundamentally different?
?
If even the attribute of 'life' is not transferable from human to God, how much more cautious should you be when trying to understand God's other attributes? What habit can you adopt to pause before assuming you know how God 'should' act?

Second Objection and Refutation: Conditions of Perception
Second objection, Mu'tazila response, and refutation via modus tollens.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection about conditions of perception, the Mu'tazila response, and the author's refutation using modus tollens.

Core Idea
If conditions are necessary, their absence prevents the effect; the Mu'tazila response undermines logical reasoning.

The second objection concedes that life might be common but argues that perception could depend on conditions absent in God. The later Mu'tazila responded by claiming that since those conditions are impossible for God, they cease to be conditions. The author refutes this by showing it would invalidate the logical principle of modus tollens (قياس الخلف).

Guided Path
1
Analyze the objection
Read the Arabic of the second objection. Note how it introduces the idea of conditions (شرائط) that might prevent perception in God.
2
Understand the Mu'tazila response
The later Mu'tazila argue that impossible conditions cannot be genuine conditions, so the cause (life) must produce perception. Compare this to the original objection.
This response attempts to safeguard the argument.
3
Examine the refutation
The author refutes by showing that if we accept that conditioned can occur without its condition, then modus tollens (if the necessary effect is absent, the necessitating cause is absent) becomes invalid. This is a logical reductio.
Understand the example of modus tollens given in the text.
4
Connect to broader theology
This refutation defends the validity of logical inference in theological arguments, a key principle in kalam.

?

Third Objection: Life Only Entails Sensory Perception
Third objection: life causes sensory perception, not specific hearing/sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that life only necessitates sensory perception, not specific modes like seeing and hearing.

Core Idea
Life gives general perception, not specific senses; the Mu'tazila overreach.

The third objection narrows the scope: even if life entails perception, it only entails sensory perception (الإدراك الحسي) in general, not specifically hearing or seeing. The Mu'tazila argument fails to prove that God hears and sees, which are the desired conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic of the third objection. Notice how it distinguishes between general sensory perception and specific modalities.
2
Understand the distinction
The objection argues that life gives a capacity for sensory perception, but not every specific sense. This challenges the Mu'tazila claim that life necessarily gives sight and hearing.
3
Connect to the overall argument
This objection shows that even if the earlier objections are overcome, the Mu'tazila cannot prove that God sees and hears specifically, which is what they wanted to establish.

Refuting the Second Objection: Conditions and Logical Consistency
Author refutes second objection about conditions for divine perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the second objection by demonstrating that the Mu'tazilite reasoning about conditions would destroy the logical method of reductio (qiyās al-khalf).

Core Idea
A condition cannot be dismissed because it is impossible for the subject.

The second objection concedes that life is a common attribute but adds that perception requires conditions that are impossible for God. Some later Mu'tazilites respond that such conditions cease to be conditions. In this passage, the author refutes this by showing that their reasoning would invalidate a fundamental logical tool: the argument from converse (qiyās al-khalf). Let's see how he preserves logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and Mu'tazilite response
Read the full passage carefully, noting the objector's concession and the Mu'tazilite claim that impossible conditions cease to be conditions.
The Mu'tazilite argument seems plausible, but the author will show its flaw.
2
Understand the author's counter
The author states that if something is conditional, the absence of the condition necessitates the absence of the conditioned. This is a basic logical rule.
3
Grasp the reductio argument (qiyās al-khalf)
The author uses a standard logical method: if the necessary consequence is absent, the necessitating must be absent. Accepting the Mu'tazilite view would break this link.
Qiyās al-khalf is a form of proof by contradiction.
4
See the consequence
If the Mu'tazilite reasoning were valid, one could always argue that the absence of a necessary consequence does not prove the absence of the necessitating, because the consequence might be 'impossible' and thus not required. This would destroy logical arguments.
The author's refutation preserves the integrity of logical reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : هب أن الحياة صفة مشتركة ، بين الشاهد والغائب ، إلا أن يكون إيجاب الحياة لصفة المدركية ، موقوف على شرائط تمنع ثبوتها في حق الله تعالى ، وحينئذ يمتنع حصول ذلك الحكم في الغائب لفوات تلك الشرائط . وقال بعض المتأخرين من المعتزلة : إن تلك الأشياء لما كانت ممتنعة الثبوت في حق الله تعالى خرجت عن كونها شرائط بصحة الإدراك ، لأن المعدوم يمتنع كونه شرط لتأثر المقتضى ، وإذا خرجت عن كونها شرائط ، وكان المقتضى حاصلاً وجب ترتب الأثر عليه (1) ونقول : هذا باطل . لأن بتقدير أن يكون الشيء مشروطاً لشيء ، فعند قوات الشرط ، يجب فوات المشروط - إلا أنه يحصل المشروط بـدونـه - ولو صححنا ما ذكرتم لبطل قياس الخلف ، فإنا إذا قلنا : لم يوجد اللازم ( فوجب أن لا يوجد الملزوم ) . فيقال : لم لم يوجد اللازم ؟ فقد خرج عن كونه لازماً ، فوجب أن يحصل الملزوم بدونه ، فثبت : أن الكلام الذي ذكرتم ، لو صح لبطل قياس الخلف .
The second question: Suppose that life is a common attribute between the witnessed and the unseen, except that making life necessary for the attribute of perception is dependent on conditions that prevent its establishment in the case of God Almighty, and then that judgment cannot occur in the unseen due to the absence of those conditions. And some later Mu'tazilites said: Those things, since they are impossible to establish in the case of God Almighty, cease to be conditions for the validity of perception, because the non-existent cannot be a condition for the effect of the effective cause, and if they cease to be conditions and the effective cause exists, the effect must follow (1). And we say: This is false. Because if something is conditional for something else, then when the condition is absent, the conditioned must be absent — unless the conditioned occurs without it — and if we were to accept what you said, the argument from converse (qiyās al-khalf) would be invalid. For if we say: The necessary consequence does not exist, so the necessitating must not exist. It would be said: Why does the necessary consequence not exist? It has ceased to be a necessary consequence, so the necessitating must occur without it. So it is established: The statement you made, if correct, would invalidate the argument from converse.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage records the second objection and its refutation. The objector concedes that life is a common attribute but argues that life necessitates perception only under certain conditions that are impossible for God, so perception is not proven for God. Some later Mu'tazilites respond that those conditions, being impossible for God, cease to be conditions, so the effective cause (life) must produce its effect (perception). The author rejects this as false, explaining the logic of conditionals: if a condition is absent, the conditioned must be absent. He uses the reductio method (qiyās al-khalf) to show that accepting their reasoning would destroy the very method they use in other arguments. The passage demonstrates careful logical analysis of conditions and the preservation of the reductio argument.

Key Terms
الحياة
المدركية
الشرائط
قياس الخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a condition is impossible for a subject, it ceases to be a condition.
Correction: The author shows that conditions remain logically binding; dismissing them destroys logical consistency, e.g., the reductio argument.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main logical criticism of the Mu'tazilite response regarding conditions for perception?
A) The Mu'tazilites misunderstood the nature of life.
B) Accepting their view would invalidate the reductio argument (qiyās al-khalf).
C) The conditions are actually present in God.
D) The Mu'tazilites denied the existence of life in God.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the second objection by demonstrating that the Mu'tazilite reasoning would destroy the logical principle of reductio (qiyās al-khalf), preserving the necessity of conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a necessary condition because it seemed impossible, only to later realize your reasoning was inconsistent? Ponder a time you avoided a logical requirement. Will you now commit to following logical necessity even when it challenges your assumptions?
?
The author shows that even in theology, we must adhere to strict logic. Is there an area in your faith where you have been intellectually lazy, accepting a weak argument because it was convenient? What one step will you take this week to test your beliefs against sound reasoning?
?
If you were to apply the Mu'tazilite reasoning to your own life—dismissing a condition because you thought it 'impossible'—where would you find yourself justifying wishful thinking over reality? Confess that area to yourself and decide to embrace truth even if it demands something difficult.

Refutation of the Third Objection on Sensory Perception
Author refutes third objection restricting life's necessity to sensory perception only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the argument that life necessitates perception, and how it distinguishes between general sensory perception and specific faculties like sight and hearing.

Core Idea
Life gives perception, but not every type — sight and hearing need more.

The argument that life entails perception faces a challenge: perhaps life only grants a general capacity for sensation, not the specific powers of seeing and hearing. This objection forces us to refine our understanding of how attributes relate to their effects. Let's examine the objection and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the objector first concedes a point (life necessitates perception) but then restricts it to sensory perception only.
The objection is presented as a hypothetical: 'Suppose that being alive necessitates perception...'
2
Identify the logical structure
The objection has three parts: a concession, a restriction, and a conclusion. Find each part and understand how they connect.
The conclusion is that the opponent's claim is invalid.
3
Reflect on the refutation
Although the passage ends with 'And this is the end of the discussion,' the refutation is implied: the objector's own concession that life necessitates perception supports the original argument, but the restriction is not proven. Consider whether the objection truly undermines the claim.
The commentary suggests the author considers the matter resolved, possibly by earlier arguments.

Source Text

السؤال الثالث : هب أن كون الحي حياً يوجب المدركية ، إلا أن هذا إنما يجري في الإدراك الحسي ، فإن كون الحي حياً يوجب هذا النوع من الإدراك ، فأما الإبصار والسماع ، فلا يحصلان بمجرد كون الحي حياً . فبطل قولكم : إن الموجب لهذا الإدراك ، مجرد كونه حياً . وهذا تمام الكلام في هذا الباب . والله أعلم .
The third question: Suppose that being alive necessitates perception, but this applies only to sensory perception, for being alive necessitates this type of perception. As for sight and hearing, they do not occur merely by being alive. So your statement that the cause of this perception is merely being alive is invalid. And this is the end of the discussion in this chapter. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 198

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection and its implied refutation. The objector concedes that life necessitates perception but restricts it to sensory perception (al-idrāk al-ḥissī) in general, not specifically to seeing and hearing. Thus, the opponent's claim that life alone necessitates perception is invalid because sight and hearing require additional factors. The author ends the chapter with 'And this is the end of the discussion in this chapter. And God knows best,' indicating that the objection stands unanswered or that the author considers it resolved by earlier arguments. The passage highlights the nuance in how the attribute of life relates to different types of perception.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That life alone is sufficient for all perception, including sight and hearing.
Correction: The objection clarifies that life only entails general sensory capacity, not specific faculties. Sight and hearing require additional conditions.

Checkpoint

A) All types of perception including sight and hearing.
B) Only sensory perception in general, not sight and hearing specifically.
C) Nothing at all.

Key Takeaway
The third objection refines the link between life and perception: life necessitates only general sensory capacity, not specific faculties like sight and hearing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is a gift from Allah, and He has given you general perception. But specific gifts like sight and hearing require additional means. When was the last time you thanked Him specifically for the ability to see and hear, not just for being alive?
?
In your spiritual journey, have you assumed that simply being a believer (alive in faith) automatically gives you deep understanding of Allah? Where must you seek the 'extra' means—like reflection, study, and sincere supplication—to truly 'see' and 'hear' the truths of faith? What specific step will you take today?
?
The objector argued that life alone does not produce sight and hearing. Are you neglecting to use the additional means Allah has provided for spiritual perception? What habits or attitudes are blinding you or deafening you to His signs, and what will you change starting now?

The Dependency of God's Speech on Free Agency and Knowledge of Particulars
The author introduces the second issue and states its dependence on two prior attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that proving God's attribute of speech (kalam) depends on prior knowledge of God as a free agent and as one who knows particulars.

Core Idea
God's attribute of speech is not independent; it depends on His being a free agent and knowing particulars.

The author now turns to the second issue concerning divine attributes: proving that God is a speaker (mutakallim). He immediately clarifies that this attribute is not self-standing but branches from two prior attributes: God's free agency and His knowledge of particulars. Understanding this dependence is crucial for the argument that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author states the second issue and then immediately lists two prior attributes that it depends on. The passage ends mid-sentence, so we only have the beginning of an argument.
The passage is incomplete; we will only cover the introductory part.
2
Identify the two dependencies
The author says that proving God is a speaker 'branches from' (يتفرع على) two things: God being a free agent (فاعل مختار) and God knowing particulars (عالم بالجزئيات). Understand why these might be necessary for speech.
Think: For God to speak, He must have free will to choose to speak, and He must know the details of what He speaks about.
3
Examine the beginning of the argument
The author introduces a group who interprets 'demand' (الطلب) as will (الإرادة). They argue that since God wills some things and dislikes others, if He creates sounds in a specific body, those sounds indicate His will, making Him a speaker. This argument is cut off, but note the connection.
This shows how the dependence on free agency and knowledge is used.
4
Reflect on the lesson's core idea
Consider why the author emphasizes that God's speech depends on other attributes. This prevents misunderstanding that God's speech is a separate, independent attribute. The lesson establishes that divine attributes are interconnected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية: في إثبات كونه تعالى متكلماً. اعلم أن هذا يتفرع على كونه تعالى فاعلاً مختاراً، وعلى كونه تعالى عالماً بالجزئيات. فأما الذين فسروا ذلك الطلب بالإرادة فقالوا: ثبت أنه تعالى مريداً لبعض الأشياء وكارهاً لبعضها، فإذا خلق أصواتاً في جسم مخصوص تدل تلك
The second issue: On proving that He, exalted, is a speaker (mutakallim). Know that this branches from His, exalted, being a free agent (fa'il mukhtar) and from His, exalted, being knowing of particulars (alim bil-juz'iyyat). As for those who interpreted that demand as will, they said: It is established that He, exalted, is willing of some things and disliking of some others. Then if He creates sounds in a particular body, those (sounds) indicate...
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage introduces the second major issue regarding God's attribute of speech (kalam). The author states that proving God is a speaker depends on two prior points: that God is a free agent (fa'il mukhtar) and that He knows particulars (juz'iyyat). He then begins to present the argument of those who equate demand (talab) with will: since it is established that God wills some things and dislikes others, then if He creates sounds in a particular body (e.g., a tree or a cloud), those sounds indicate His will or dislike, thus making Him a speaker. The passage is cut off before completing the argument.

Correction: The author shows it depends on His being a free agent and knowing particulars.

Key Takeaway
Proving God's attribute of speech requires establishing first that He is a free agent and knows particular events.

?

?
You believe God speaks, but do you truly live as if His words are personally crafted for you by the One who knows every detail of your soul? What would it look like today to respond to His speech with the urgency of someone being called by name?

The Two Interpretations of Divine Speech
Explains two interpretations of divine speech and their requirements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main interpretations of divine speech and the premises required by those who hold it to be a distinct attribute.

Core Idea
Divine speech is either will expressed through created sounds or an eternal attribute distinct from will.

Sanusi begins by clarifying that proving God is a speaker depends on His free agency and knowledge of particulars. He then distinguishes two theological camps: those who equate speech with will (expressed via created sounds) and those who posit a separate meaning. This lesson explores these positions and the challenges each faces.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation.
Pay attention to the structure: first group then second group.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find the two definitions of speech: (1) created sounds indicating will, (2) a meaning distinct from will. Note the premises needed for the second view.
The second view requires three premises; the third one claims speech is eternal.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why the second view is more complex and why the author notes that no good proof exists for it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المسألة الثانية : في إثبات كونه تعالى متكلماً : اعلم أن هذا يتفرع على كونه تعالى فاعلاً مختاراً ، وعلى كونه تعالى عالماً بالجزئيات . فأما الذين فسروا ذلك الطلب بالإرادة . فقالوا : ثبت أنه تعالى مريداً لبعض الأشياء وكارها لبعضها ، فإذا خلق أصواتاً في جسم مخصوص تدل تلك الأصوات بالوضع والاصطلاح على كونه تعالى مريداً ، لما أراد ، وكارهاً لما كره . وذلك هو الكلام . وأما الذين فسروا ذلك الطلب بمعنى مغاير للإرادة . فهم يحتاجون إلى إثبات مقدمات . أولها : الفرق المعقول بين ذلك الطلب وبين الإرادة وثانيها : إقامة الدلالة على كونه تعالى موصوفاً بذلك المعنى ، وليس للقوم فيه دليل جيد على ما سيأتي شرحه وثالثها : إن القائلين بهذا المعنى زعموا أن هذا الشيء قديم ، وهو بعيد ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه لا معنى للأمر والنهي إلا التزام الفعل ، والتزام الترك ، والعلم الضروري حاصل بأنه قبل وجود زيد فإنه يمتنع أن يحصل الزامه فعلاً ، والزامه تركاً . فإن معنى الإلزام أن نقول : يا زيد الزمنك هذا الفصل . ويا عمرو الزمتك هذا الترك . والعلم الضروري حاصل أن عند عدم زيد ، وعدم عمرو ، فإنه يمتنع أن نقول : يا زيد الزمتك هذا الفعل ، ويا عمرو الزمتك هذا الترك ، وإن لم يكن هذا العلم ضرورياً، فليس عند العقل شيء من العلوم الضرورية . والثاني : إنه تعالى أخبر عن أشياء كقوله : ( إنا أرسلنا نوحاً ) و : عصى آدم ومعلوم أن المخبر عنه سابق في الوجود على حصول الخبر ، فلو كان هذا الخبر أزلياً ، لزم أن يكون الأزلي مسبوقاً بغيره ، وهو محال . فإن عارضوا ذلك بالعلم نقول : قد ذكرنا : أن المذهب الصحيح في هذا الباب هو قول أبي الحسين البصري : وهو أنه يتغير العلم عند تغير المعلوم
The second issue: Proving that He, exalted, is a speaker. Know that this branches off from His being a free agent and His being knowledgeable of particulars. As for those who interpreted that demand as will, they said: It is established that He, exalted, is willing for some things and averse to some things. So when He creates sounds in a specific body, those sounds indicate, by convention and agreement, that He, exalted, is willing for what He willed and averse to what He disliked. That is speech. As for those who interpreted that demand as a meaning distinct from will, they need to establish premises. First: a reasonable distinction between that demand and will. Second: establishing the proof that He, exalted, is described by that meaning, and the people have no good proof for it, as will be explained. Third: those who hold this meaning claimed that this thing is eternal, and it is far-fetched. Proofs for this: First: There is no meaning to command and prohibition except committing to an action and committing to refraining. Necessary knowledge obtains that before the existence of Zayd, it is impossible to obligate him to an action or to refraining. For the meaning of obligation is that we say: O Zayd, I oblige you to this action, and O Amr, I oblige you to this refraining. Necessary knowledge obtains that when Zayd and Amr do not exist, it is impossible to say: O Zayd, I oblige you to this action, and O Amr, I oblige you to this refraining. If this knowledge were not necessary, then there would be no necessary knowledge in the intellect. Second: He, exalted, informed about things like His saying: 'Indeed, We sent Noah' and 'Adam disobeyed.' It is known that the thing informed about precedes the existence of the report. If this report were eternal, then the eternal would be preceded by something else, which is impossible. If they object to that by knowledge, we say: we have mentioned that the correct position in this matter is the statement of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri: that knowledge changes when the known changes.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage addresses the proof that God (taʿālā) is a speaker (mutakallim). It begins by noting that this depends on God being a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) and knowing particulars (ʿālim bi-l-juzʾiyyāt). Two interpretations of divine speech are then presented. The first group interprets the 'demand' (al-ṭalab) as will (irāda): God creates sounds in a specific body, and those sounds conventionally indicate His will and aversion; that is speech. The second group posits a meaning distinct from will. They must establish three premises: (1) a reasonable distinction between that demand and will; (2) a proof that God is described by that meaning (noting that no good proof exists); (3) they claim this thing is eternal (qadīm), which is far-fetched. Two proofs are given against the eternal view. First proof: command (amr) and prohibition (nahy) entail obligation (iltizām) of action or refraining. It is necessarily known that one cannot obligate a non-existent person (e.g., Zayd before his existence). If the knowledge were not necessary, there would be no necessary knowledge. Second proof: God informed about past events (e.g., 'We sent Noah,' 'Adam disobeyed'). The reported event precedes the report; if the report were eternal, the eternal would be preceded by something else, which is impossible. A potential objection involving God's knowledge is rebutted by citing Abu al-Husayn al-Basri: knowledge changes with the known.

Key Terms
متكلم
إرادة
كلام
قديم
أمر
نهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first interpretation, what is divine speech?
A) An eternal attribute distinct from will.
B) Created sounds that indicate God's will by convention.
C) A direct expression of God's knowledge.
D) The Quran as an uncreated entity.

Key Takeaway
The two main interpretations of divine speech are (1) it is will expressed through created sounds, and (2) it is a distinct eternal attribute, though the latter lacks strong proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you experience it as God's will addressing you through human language, or as an eternal utterance beyond creation? Which understanding makes your heart more present?
?
The author dismisses the second view as lacking proof. Are you willing to question a belief about God's speech that is widely held among Muslims, if the evidence is weak? What is at stake for you personally?
?
If God's speech is only His will expressed through created sounds, does that diminish the Quran's authority in your life, or does it make God's engagement with creation more immediate? Be honest about your reaction.

Two Proofs Against the Eternity of Speech
Two rational proofs against eternal speech and a rebuttal to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational proofs demonstrating that divine speech cannot be eternal, and how a potential objection regarding God's knowledge is rebutted.

Core Idea
Eternal command to a non-existent being is impossible; eternal reports of past events would make the eternal preceded.

After presenting the claim that speech is eternal, the author provides two decisive proofs. The first focuses on the impossibility of obligating a non-existent person, and the second on the impossibility of an eternal report about a past event. A subtle objection concerning God's knowledge is then addressed with the principle that knowledge changes with the known.

Guided Path
1
Read the two proofs
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Identify the first proof (about command and prohibition) and the second proof (about past reports).
The first proof uses the example of Zayd to illustrate a necessary truth.
2
Understand the logic
For the first proof, note that command entails obligation, which cannot apply to a non-existent person. For the second, note that a report must come after the event it describes.
These are intuitive arguments from necessary knowledge.
3
Examine the rebuttal
Read the objection regarding God's knowledge and the response citing Abu al-Husayn al-Basri: knowledge changes with the known.
This preserves God's omniscience without requiring eternal speech.

Source Text

المسألة الثانية : في إثبات كونه تعالى متكلماً : اعلم أن هذا يتفرع على كونه تعالى فاعلاً مختاراً ، وعلى كونه تعالى عالماً بالجزئيات . فأما الذين فسروا ذلك الطلب بالإرادة . فقالوا : ثبت أنه تعالى مريداً لبعض الأشياء وكارها لبعضها ، فإذا خلق أصواتاً في جسم مخصوص تدل تلك الأصوات بالوضع والاصطلاح على كونه تعالى مريداً ، لما أراد ، وكارهاً لما كره . وذلك هو الكلام . وأما الذين فسروا ذلك الطلب بمعنى مغاير للإرادة . فهم يحتاجون إلى إثبات مقدمات . أولها : الفرق المعقول بين ذلك الطلب وبين الإرادة وثانيها : إقامة الدلالة على كونه تعالى موصوفاً بذلك المعنى ، وليس للقوم فيه دليل جيد على ما سيأتي شرحه وثالثها : إن القائلين بهذا المعنى زعموا أن هذا الشيء قديم ، وهو بعيد ويدل عليه وجوه : الأول : إنه لا معنى للأمر والنهي إلا التزام الفعل ، والتزام الترك ، والعلم الضروري حاصل بأنه قبل وجود زيد فإنه يمتنع أن يحصل الزامه فعلاً ، والزامه تركاً . فإن معنى الإلزام أن نقول : يا زيد الزمنك هذا الفصل . ويا عمرو الزمتك هذا الترك . والعلم الضروري حاصل أن عند عدم زيد ، وعدم عمرو ، فإنه يمتنع أن نقول : يا زيد الزمتك هذا الفعل ، ويا عمرو الزمتك هذا الترك ، وإن لم يكن هذا العلم ضرورياً، فليس عند العقل شيء من العلوم الضرورية . والثاني : إنه تعالى أخبر عن أشياء كقوله : ( إنا أرسلنا نوحاً ) و : عصى آدم ومعلوم أن المخبر عنه سابق في الوجود على حصول الخبر ، فلو كان هذا الخبر أزلياً ، لزم أن يكون الأزلي مسبوقاً بغيره ، وهو محال . فإن عارضوا ذلك بالعلم نقول : قد ذكرنا : أن المذهب الصحيح في هذا الباب هو قول أبي الحسين البصري : وهو أنه يتغير العلم عند تغير المعلوم
The second issue: Proving that He, exalted, is a speaker. Know that this branches off from His being a free agent and His being knowledgeable of particulars. As for those who interpreted that demand as will, they said: It is established that He, exalted, is willing for some things and averse to some things. So when He creates sounds in a specific body, those sounds indicate, by convention and agreement, that He, exalted, is willing for what He willed and averse to what He disliked. That is speech. As for those who interpreted that demand as a meaning distinct from will, they need to establish premises. First: a reasonable distinction between that demand and will. Second: establishing the proof that He, exalted, is described by that meaning, and the people have no good proof for it, as will be explained. Third: those who hold this meaning claimed that this thing is eternal, and it is far-fetched. Proofs for this: First: There is no meaning to command and prohibition except committing to an action and committing to refraining. Necessary knowledge obtains that before the existence of Zayd, it is impossible to obligate him to an action or to refraining. For the meaning of obligation is that we say: O Zayd, I oblige you to this action, and O Amr, I oblige you to this refraining. Necessary knowledge obtains that when Zayd and Amr do not exist, it is impossible to say: O Zayd, I oblige you to this action, and O Amr, I oblige you to this refraining. If this knowledge were not necessary, then there would be no necessary knowledge in the intellect. Second: He, exalted, informed about things like His saying: 'Indeed, We sent Noah' and 'Adam disobeyed.' It is known that the thing informed about precedes the existence of the report. If this report were eternal, then the eternal would be preceded by something else, which is impossible. If they object to that by knowledge, we say: we have mentioned that the correct position in this matter is the statement of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri: that knowledge changes when the known changes.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage addresses the proof that God (taʿālā) is a speaker (mutakallim). It begins by noting that this depends on God being a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār) and knowing particulars (ʿālim bi-l-juzʾiyyāt). Two interpretations of divine speech are then presented. The first group interprets the 'demand' (al-ṭalab) as will (irāda): God creates sounds in a specific body, and those sounds conventionally indicate His will and aversion; that is speech. The second group posits a meaning distinct from will. They must establish three premises: (1) a reasonable distinction between that demand and will; (2) a proof that God is described by that meaning (noting that no good proof exists); (3) they claim this thing is eternal (qadīm), which is far-fetched. Two proofs are given against the eternal view. First proof: command (amr) and prohibition (nahy) entail obligation (iltizām) of action or refraining. It is necessarily known that one cannot obligate a non-existent person (e.g., Zayd before his existence). If the knowledge were not necessary, there would be no necessary knowledge. Second proof: God informed about past events (e.g., 'We sent Noah,' 'Adam disobeyed'). The reported event precedes the report; if the report were eternal, the eternal would be preceded by something else, which is impossible. A potential objection involving God's knowledge is rebutted by citing Abu al-Husayn al-Basri: knowledge changes with the known.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
إلزام
إخبار
العلم
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the two proofs contradict God's eternal knowledge, implying God is ignorant of the future.
Correction: The passage clarifies that knowledge changes with the known; God's knowledge of events is not an eternal speech but a relational attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the second proof against eternal speech?
A) Speech is a sound created in a body.
B) An eternal report about a past event would be preceded by that event, which is impossible.
C) Command cannot be eternal because God does not command nonexistent beings.
D) Knowledge is eternal and unchanging.

Key Takeaway
Eternal speech is impossible because command requires an existing addressee and report requires a preceding event; any objection about God's knowledge is resolved by the principle that knowledge changes with the known.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's command requires an existing addressee, how does this affect the way you respond to His commands in the Quran? Are you living as one who is truly addressed, or do you act as if the command is for someone else? What specific command of God will you obey today to make this real?
?
The proof about reports shows that God's speech about past events is dependent on those events occurring in time. Does this truth make the Quran feel more 'earthly' or more 'heavenly' to you? How can you deepen your trust in the Quran's historical accounts despite their temporal nature?
?
The passage says knowledge changes when the known changes. Reflect on your own knowledge of God: has it changed as you have encountered new experiences or trials? How can you ensure your knowledge of God remains dynamic and relational, not static and theoretical?

Divine Speech as Will: Definition and Opponents' Premises
The author defines divine speech as will/hatred and enumerates three premises of opponents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's definition of divine speech as will and hatred, and the three premises required by those who posit a distinct attribute of demand.

Core Idea
Divine speech is will and hatred, not sounds or letters.

This passage from al-Maṭālib al-ʿĀliyya presents a concise definition of divine speech as the attribute of will and hatred. The author then turns to a group of theologians who separate the concept of divine demand (al-ṭalab) from will, outlining the three premises they must establish. Understanding this foundational distinction is crucial for grasping later arguments about the nature of the Qur'an and divine command.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the author's central definition and the three premises listed.
2
Identify the definition of divine speech
The author states that sounds indicate that God is willing and hating, and that this is what speech (al-kalām) is. Understand that here 'speech' is defined as the attribute of will, not as articulate sound.
3
Examine the three premises
The author lists three things that those who separate demand from will must prove: (1) a rational distinction between demand and will, (2) evidence that God is described by this distinct meaning, and (3) that this demand is eternal.
The author notes that the opponents have no good evidence for the second premise.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why these premises are important: they frame the debate over whether divine command is identical to God's will or an additional eternal attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصوات بالوضع والاصطلاح على كونه تعالى مريداً، لما أراد، وكارهاً لما كره. وذلك هو الكلام. وأما الذين فسروا ذلك الطلب بمعنى مغاير للإرادة. فهم يحتاجون إلى إثبات مقدمات. أولها: الفرق المعقول بين ذلك الطلب وبين الإرادة وثانيها: إقامة الدلالة على كونه تعالى موصوفاً بذلك المعنى، وليس للقوم فيه دليل جيد على ما سيأتي شرحه بعيد وثالثها: إن القائلين بهذا المعنى زعموا أن هذا الشيء قديم، وهو يدل عليه وجوه:
Sounds by convention and terminology indicate that He, exalted, is willing for what He wills and hating for what He hates. That is speech. As for those who interpreted that demand (al-ṭalab) as a meaning distinct from will (al-irāda), they need to establish premises: First: the rational distinction between that demand and will. Second: establishing evidence that He, exalted, is described by that meaning, and the people have no good evidence for it as will soon be explained far off. Third: those who say this meaning claim that this thing is eternal, and it is supported by proofs:
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage begins by defining divine speech (al-kalām) as the attribute of will (irāda) and hatred (karāha) — God is described as willing what He wills and hating what He hates, and that is what speech means. Then the author turns to a group of theologians who interpret the 'demand' (al-ṭalab) — that is, divine command and prohibition — as a distinct meaning separate from the attribute of will. These opponents need to prove three things: first, that there is a rational distinction (al-farq al-maʿqūl) between this demand and will; second, that there is evidence (dalāla) that God is described by this distinct meaning (the author notes they have no good evidence, as will be explained later); third, they claim that this demand is eternal (qadīm). The author then says 'and it is supported by proofs', indicating that he will now refute their claim by presenting counter-proofs.

الإرادة (al-irāda)
الطلب (al-ṭalab)
المقدمات (al-muqaddimāt)
قديم (qadīm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Divine speech consists of sounds and letters like human speech.
Correction: The author defines divine speech as the attribute of will and hatred, not as conventional sounds.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech is defined as God's will and hatred, and those who separate demand from will must prove three premises: a rational distinction, evidence of description, and eternality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The opponents needed to prove a rational distinction between God's demand and His will. In your life, is there a gap between what you know God demands and what you genuinely will? Where is that gap most painful?
?
God's speech is eternal, yet it addresses you in your specific time and place. When you read or hear a verse, do you receive it as His eternal will for you right now, or do you treat it as a historical artifact? What would change if you truly believed it is His present speech to you?

First Proof: Command Requires an Existing Recipient
The author argues that command and prohibition necessarily imply the existence of the one commanded, so they cannot be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why command and prohibition cannot be eternal attributes of Allah, based on the necessary relationship between command and an existing commanded subject.

Core Idea
Command is an address; an address requires someone to address.

In this first proof, the author argues that the very meaning of command implies a recipient. Before any creature existed, there was no one to command, so command cannot be eternal. This establishes that divine speech (as command) is temporally originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
2
Understand the definition of command
The author defines command and prohibition as obligating (ilzām) an act or abandonment. Notice the central role of 'obligation' in the argument.
3
Grasp the necessary knowledge argument
The author appeals to necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī): everyone knows intuitively that you cannot obligate a non-existent person. This is presented as self-evident.
4
Connect to the eternal attribute debate
If command were eternal, it would have existed before any creatures, but there would be no one to command. Thus, command cannot be eternal.
This proof targets the view that divine speech (kalām) is eternal in all respects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: إنه لا معنى للأمر والنهي إلا التزام الفعل، والتزام الترك، والعلم الضروري حاصل بأنه قبل وجود زيد فإنه يمتنع أن يحصل الزامه فعلاً، والزامه تركاً. فإن معنى الإلزام أن نقول: يا زيد الزمنك هذا الفصل. ويا عمرو الزمتك هذا الترك. والعلم الضروري حاصل أن عند عدم زيد، وعدم عمرو، فإنه يمتنع أن نقول: يا زيد الزمتك هذا الفعل، ويا عمرو الزمتك هذا الترك، وإن لم يكن هذا العلم ضرورياً، فليس عند العقل شيء من العلوم الضرورية.
First: The meaning of command (al-amr) and prohibition (al-nahy) is nothing but obligating the act and obligating the abandonment. Necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī) is obtained that before the existence of Zaid, it is impossible to obtain his obligation of an act and his obligation of abandonment. For the meaning of obligation (al-ilzām) is that we say: 'O Zaid, I obligate you to do this act' and 'O Amr, I obligate you to do this abandonment'. Necessary knowledge is obtained that when Zaid is non-existent and Amr is non-existent, it is impossible for us to say: 'O Zaid, I obligate you to do this act' and 'O Amr, I obligate you to do this abandonment'. And if this knowledge is not necessary, then there is no necessary knowledge in the intellect.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage presents the first proof against the eternality of divine command (al-amr) and prohibition (al-nahy). The author defines command and prohibition as essentially obligating (ilzām) an action or an abandonment. He then appeals to necessary knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī): everyone knows intuitively that before a person like Zaid exists, it is impossible to address a command to him obligating him to do something, because there is no recipient. The act of obligation requires a subject who can be addressed. Since the command is an address (khiṭāb), it requires the existence of the one commanded. Therefore, if command were eternal, it would have existed before any creatures existed, which is impossible because there would be no one to address. Hence, command cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-amr)
النهي (al-nahy)
الإلزام (al-ilzām)
العلم الضروري (al-ʿilm al-ḍarūrī)
الوجود (al-wujūd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this argument denies that God can have knowledge of future acts.
Correction: The argument is about the act of commanding, not about knowledge. Command is an address that requires a recipient, whereas knowledge does not.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why can't command be eternal?
A) Because God does not have eternal attributes
B) Because command requires an existing recipient to address
C) Because command changes with time
D) Because command is identical to will

Key Takeaway
Command and prohibition are acts of addressing a subject, so they cannot exist before any subject exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah's command is an address to you personally—'O you who believe.' Do you truly feel that each command in the Quran is directed at you, or do you subconsciously distance yourself from the obligation? What would have to change in your daily life if you heard every 'O you who believe' as if Allah were calling you by name?
?
The proof shows that a command requires an existing person to address. Since Allah commands you now, He affirms your existence and your responsibility. When was the last time you felt the weight of being personally addressed by a command, and how did you respond? If you have been ignoring or delaying obedience to a specific command, what is stopping you from acting today?
?
If command is an address, then ignoring a divine command is like refusing to answer someone who is speaking directly to you. Think of a command you have been avoiding. How would you feel if a beloved person kept calling you and you refused to respond? What is one step you will take this week to answer that divine address, not just with words but with action?

Second Proof: Past Events Precede Divine Reports
Argument that divine reports about past events cannot be eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why divine reports about past events cannot be eternal and how this differs from divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Eternal reports cannot be preceded by events, so divine speech about the past is temporal.

This lesson presents the second proof against the eternity of the Quran. The author argues that since the reported event must come before the report, an eternal report would be preceded, which is impossible. He then addresses an objection concerning divine knowledge, citing Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's view that knowledge changes when the known changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
The proof uses Quranic examples: We sent Noah and Adam disobeyed.
2
Identify the logical structure
Understand the premise: the reported event precedes the report. If the report were eternal, it would be preceded by the event, but the eternal cannot be preceded. Thus the report cannot be eternal.
3
Consider the objection about knowledge
The author anticipates an objection: divine knowledge also relates to past events but is eternal. The response cites Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's view that knowledge changes when the known changes.
This distinguishes informative speech from knowledge.
4
Reflect on the significance
This proof undermines the idea of eternal informative speech in God, while allowing for eternal knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني: إنه تعالى أخبر عن أشياء كقوله: (إنا أرسلنا نوحاً) و: عصى آدم ومعلوم أن المخبر عنه سابق في الوجود على حصول الخبر، فلو كان هذا الخبر أزلياً، لزم أن يكون الأزلي مسبوقاً بغيره، وهو محال. فإن عارضوا ذلك بالعلم نقول: قد ذكرنا: أن المذهب الصحيح في هذا الباب هو قول أبي الحسين البصري: وهو أنه يتغير العلم عند تغير المعلوم
Second: He, exalted, informed about things like His saying: 'Indeed, We sent Noah' and 'Adam disobeyed'. It is known that the thing informed about (al-mukhbar ʿanhu) precedes in existence the occurrence of the report (al-khabar). So if this report were beginningless (azaliyyan), it would necessitate that the beginningless be preceded by something else, which is impossible. If they object to that by [the case of] knowledge, we say: we have mentioned that the correct doctrine in this matter is the saying of Abu al-Husayn al-Basri: that knowledge changes when the known changes.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This is the second proof. The author points to divine statements in the Quran that report past events, such as 'We sent Noah' and 'Adam disobeyed'. It is rationally known that the thing reported (al-mukhbar ʿanhu) must exist before the report (al-khabar) occurs. If the report were eternal (azalī), then it would be beginningless yet preceded by the event it reports, which is a contradiction—something beginningless cannot be preceded by anything. Therefore, such reports cannot be eternal. The author then anticipates an objection: if one argues that divine knowledge (al-ʿilm) also relates to past events and is eternal, the author replies by citing Abu al-Husayn al-Basri's position that knowledge changes when the object of knowledge changes. This suggests that even knowledge, according to this school, is not eternal in the sense of being unchanging in its relation to temporal events.

Key Terms
الخبر (al-khabar)
أزلي (azalī)
المخبر عنه (al-mukhbar ʿanhu)
العلم (al-ʿilm)
أبو الحسين البصري (Abu al-Husayn al-Basri)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this proof denies God's eternal knowledge of past events.
Correction: This proof addresses only informative speech (khabar), not knowledge. The author distinguishes the two, citing a view that knowledge itself changes with its object.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the second proof?
A) Divine reports cannot be eternal because they would then precede the events reported
B) Divine reports are identical to divine knowledge
C) Divine reports are created and temporal
D) Divine reports are not real speech

Key Takeaway
Divine informative speech about past events cannot be eternal because it would be preceded by the events it reports.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic stories of past prophets, do you ever stop to think that God is reporting events that actually happened in time? How does that reality make the message more urgent for you personally?
?
The lesson distinguishes between divine knowledge and divine speech. In your own life, do you sometimes treat your knowledge of God as static and unchanging, while your response to His speech remains dormant? What would it mean for you to truly hear God's temporal report as a call to action today?
?
The objection about eternal knowledge is answered by saying that knowledge changes when the known changes. Are you willing to let your knowledge of God change as you encounter new revelations from His speech? What old assumptions about God might you need to revise in light of this lesson?

Third Proof: The Eternal Command Dilemma
The author argues that if a command is eternal, it must either persist after obedience (making fulfillment impossible) or cease (contradicting eternality).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why an eternal command addressed to a temporal being leads to an absurd dilemma: either the command never ceases (making obedience impossible) or it ceases (contradicting its eternity).

Core Idea
An eternal command after obedience either remains forever or ceases—both are impossible.

This lesson examines the third proof against the doctrine of an eternal command (qadīm) that subsists in God. The argument considers a specific command, like the obligation to pray Fajr. Once the commanded act is performed, what happens to the command? The author presents a dilemma that exposes the impossibility of an eternal command directed at temporal beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the two possibilities: the command either remains after obedience or ceases.
The passage is concise; focus on the logical structure.
2
Understand the dilemma
Identify the two horns of the dilemma. If the command remains, fulfillment is never achieved. If it ceases, then something eternal has passed away, which is impossible for a mutakallim.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Connect to divine attributes
Reflect on why this argument matters for divine attributes. It shows that commands cannot be eternal in the same way as God's essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: وهو أنه تعالى لما ألزم زيداً إقامة صلاة الصبح، فإذا أتى زيد بذلك الفعل. فهل بقي ذلك الإلزام الأول، أو لم يبق، فإن بقي وجب أن لا يكون له سبيل إلى الخروج عن العهدة، لأنه وإن أتى بذلك الفعل ألف ألف مرة، فالإلزام الأول باقي (وإن لم يبق ذلك الالزام فقد عدم، والمتكلمون مصرون على أن القديم يمتنع عليه العدم، فلما علم هذا الإلزام) وهذا الأمر، وجب على مقتضى قولهم: أن لا يكون قديماً.
Third: When He, exalted, obligated Zaid to perform the morning prayer, and Zaid performed that act, does that first obligation remain or not? If it remains, then it must be that he has no way to discharge the obligation, because even if he performs that act a thousand thousand times, the first obligation remains. (And if that obligation does not remain, then it has ceased, and the mutakallimūn insist that the eternal cannot cease. Since this obligation is known) and this command, it is necessary according to their doctrine that it not be eternal.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
The third proof considers the case of a specific command, like the obligation to pray Fajr. Once Zaid performs the prayer, does the original command (al-ilzām al-awwal) remain or not? If it remains, then Zaid can never fulfill the obligation because the command continues, so he is perpetually bound—obedience would be impossible. If it ceases, then the command has undergone nonexistence (ʿadam), but the theologians (al-mutakallimūn) hold that what is eternal (qadīm) cannot cease to exist. Therefore, whether the command remains or ceases, contradiction arises if it is eternal. Thus, the command cannot be eternal.

Key Terms
الإلزام (al-ilzām)
العهدة (al-ʿuhda)
الامتثال (al-imtithāl)
القدم (al-qidam)
العدم (al-ʿadam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument claims that God's commands are ineffective or that He cannot issue effective commands.
Correction: The argument does not claim that commands are ineffective; it shows the absurdity of an eternally subsisting command that is addressed to a temporal being. The problem is with the attribute of eternity (qidam) being applied to a directive command, not with the effectiveness of God's commands.

Checkpoint
What are the two possibilities for an eternal command after obedience, and why are both problematic?
A) It either remains (making fulfillment impossible) or ceases (contradicting eternality)
B) It either becomes knowledge or will
C) It either increases or decreases
D) It either applies to all or none

Key Takeaway
An eternal command leads to an impossible dilemma: either obedience is never fulfilled, or the eternal ceases, which is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read this proof, do you truly believe that God's commands can be fulfilled, or do you sometimes behave as if the obligation remains forever, paralyzing you? How does this understanding of command and fulfillment change how you approach prayer?
?
If you had to face the fact that God's command to you is not eternal but temporal and designed for fulfillment, would you still procrastinate in obedience? What does this dilemma reveal about your own excuses?
?
This argument shows the absurdity of an eternal command that cannot be discharged. Allah's commands are meant to be fulfilled. Is there any command of Allah that you have not yet taken steps to fulfill? What is stopping you?

Divine Attributes

Fourth Proof: Abrogation Implies Cessation of the Command
The author uses abrogation to argue for the temporality of divine commands.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of abrogation (naskh) is used to prove that divine commands are temporal and not eternal.

Core Idea
Abrogation proves commands are temporal because what ends cannot be eternal.

The author presents a fourth proof based on abrogation (naskh) in Islamic law, which the opponents accept as permissible. This proof uses the fact that abrogation involves the cessation of a ruling after its existence. Since an eternal thing cannot cease to exist, the commands that are abrogated must be temporal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own phrasing come through.
Notice the two definitions of abrogation given.
2
Identify the two definitions of abrogation
The passage defines abrogation in two ways: as removing a ruling after its establishment, or as the end of its time period. Recognize that both imply cessation.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
On both definitions, the ruling ceases after existing. Since what ceases cannot be eternal, the command must be temporal. Reflect on why this refutes the claim of eternity.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: إن النسخ عندهم جائز، والنسخ عبارة عن رفع الحكم بعد ثبوته. أو عن انتهاء زمن ذلك الحكم. وعلى التقديرين فقد عدم بعد وجوده، وما يثبت عدمه امتنع قدمه.
Fourth: Abrogation (al-naskh) according to them is permissible, and abrogation is the raising of a ruling after its establishment, or the ending of the time of that ruling. On both suppositions, it has ceased after its existence, and that which is proven to cease cannot be eternal.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
The fourth proof appeals to the concept of abrogation (naskh) in Islamic law, which the opponents accept as permissible. Abrogation means either the removal (rafʿ) of a ruling after it has been established or the termination of its time period. In either case, the ruling that was abrogated came into existence at some point and then ceased. If the divine command were eternal (qadīm), it would be impossible for it to cease, because what is eternal cannot undergo nonexistence. Therefore, divine commands subject to abrogation cannot be eternal; they must be temporal (ḥādith).

Key Terms
النسخ (al-naskh)
الحكم (al-ḥukm)
الثبوت (al-thubūt)
العدم (al-ʿadam)
القدم (al-qidam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the fourth proof denies the permissibility of abrogation.
Correction: The proof actually uses the accepted permissibility of abrogation to argue that commands are temporal.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, why does abrogation prove that commands are not eternal?
A) Because abrogation is a change in God's will
B) Because abrogation involves the end of a ruling, and what is eternal cannot end
C) Because abrogation shows that commands are created
D) Because abrogation is only for human laws

Key Takeaway
Abrogation proves that commands are temporal because cessation after existence is incompatible with eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter abrogated verses in the Quran, do you trust that Allah’s wisdom in abrogating a command is perfect, or does it create unease in your heart about the constancy of His guidance? How does this proof help you submit to His timing?
?
If divine commands are temporal, then your obedience today is a response to a specific command from Allah. Are you living as if each command is a timely gift, or do you treat obedience as a routine burden? What would change if you saw every obligation as an urgent, temporal call from your Lord?
?
This proof shows that what ceases cannot be eternal. Reflect on your own deeds: do you hope that your good deeds, though temporal, are accepted and rewarded? Or do you rely on the excuse that they are imperfect and temporary? How does the temporality of commands motivate you to act now, before the time for obedience ends?
